401 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Welton v. Los Angeles


L.A. No. 30552, Crim. No. 19092


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 497; 556 P.2d 1119; 134 Cal. Rptr. 668; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 368



December 8, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-102195, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in Los Angeles Superior Court No. C-102195 is reversed. The writ of habeas corpus is issued and the order adjudging Mrs. Welton in contempt is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from and sought habeas corpus relief from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that found her guilty of contempt of an order enjoining her from distributing maps on a public road pursuant to a municipal code regulation which plaintiff claimed was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was convicted of violating a municipal ordinance that banned the sale of printed materials upon defendant city's roadways after she sold maps to the public on a parkway. The conviction was later reversed on the ground the ordinance was unconstitutional. However, when prosecution was again sought, the ordinance was found constitutional, and the trial court entered an injunction against plaintiff prohibiting her from selling the maps on any street. After violating the order, plaintiff was found in contempt. She appealed the contempt order and also petitioned for habeas corpus relief. The court reversed the contempt judgment and issued a writ of habeas corpus. The map sales were not removed from U.S. Const. amend. I protection by the commercial speech doctrine because plaintiff's activity was only the sale of printed information itself, not its advertisement. Further, the ordinance was in excess of defendant city's police power because it did not merely regulate traffic but rather banned the sale of constitutionally protected material. However, the court proposed a way of curing the ordinance's unconstitutional effect, which was by deleting a particular cross-reference.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order enjoining plaintiff from selling maps on a public street and finding her in contempt and also granted her petition for writ of habeas corpus. The sale of maps did not fall within the commercial speech exception to freedom of speech protection. Further, the ordinance banning the sale of maps was an unconstitutional exercise of the defendant city's police power.

 

402 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Rockwell v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 30645


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 420; 556 P.2d 1101; 134 Cal. Rptr. 650; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 363



December 7, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest for a rehearing was denied January 5, 1977.



DISPOSITION: Let the peremptory writ of prohibition issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal petitioner sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent Ventura County Superior Court (California) from proceeding with a retrial on "special circumstances" allegations included in an information charging him with murder.


OVERVIEW: Criminal petitioner was convicted of murder. The jury was dismissed and a mistrial declared as to the special circumstances phase of the trial, however, when the jury was unable to agree on the special circumstances allegations. Petitioner contended that he should not be retried for the special circumstances charges because the U. S. Supreme Court had held that death penalty statutes which did not provide for consideration of mitigating circumstances in the decision to impose capital punishment violated the Eighth and Fourteenth Amendments. On appeal, the court concluded Cal. Penal Code §§ 190 - 190.3 made death a mandatory punishment for first degree murders encompassed by the special circumstances without consideration of evidence of mitigating circumstances as to the offense or the personal characteristics of defendant. The statutes afforded no specific detailed guidelines as to the relevance of such evidence in determining whether death was an appropriate punishment, and they permitted arbitrary imposition of the death penalty in violation of the Eighth and Fourteenth Amendments to the U. S. Constitution. The peremptory writ of prohibition was issued.


OUTCOME: The court issued the peremptory writ of prohibition in favor of criminal petitioner. The death penalty statutes made death a mandatory punishment for first degree murders encompassed by the special circumstances without consideration of evidence of mitigating circumstances or the personal characteristics of petitioner.

 

403 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bright v. Los Angeles Unified School Dist.


L.A. No. 30555


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 450; 556 P.2d 1090; 134 Cal. Rptr. 639; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 364; 2 Media L. Rep. 1175



December 6, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' petition for a rehearing was denied January 5, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 90087, Campbell M. Lucas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings in conformity with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that was in favor of defendants in plaintiff's challenge to defendant school district's regulations pertaining to the sale and distribution of underground school newspapers.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a high school sophomore, wrote for a newspaper distributed to students but produced independent of the school system. After an issue was published containing an article accusing defendant principal of lying, defendants prohibited distribution of the newspaper pursuant to the authority granted them under defendant school district's regulations which were promulgated in light of Cal. Educ. Code § 10611. The trial court denied plaintiff's requests for relief, and she appealed. The court reversed and remanded. Any regulation prohibiting student expression violated the constitutional rights of students absent a showing that the students' activities materially and substantially disrupted the work and discipline of the school. Section 10611 did not authorize school districts to establish systems of prior restraint in respect to the distribution of prohibited categories of expression listed in the statute, nor did it authorize school districts to ban the sale of printed materials by students which were otherwise entitled to be distributed.


OUTCOME: Judgment in favor of defendants in plaintiff's suit challenging defendant school district's regulations prohibiting distribution of an underground newspaper was reversed and remanded. The statutory authority relied on by defendants did not actually authorized them to establish a system of prior restraint and did not permit them to prohibit distribution of otherwise legitimate materials.

 

404 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bowland v. Mun. Court for Santa Cruz County Judicial Dist.


S.F. No. 23484


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 479; 556 P.2d 1081; 134 Cal. Rptr. 630; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 366



December 6, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied January 5, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 54008, Harry F. Brauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California) that denied their petition for a writ of mandate challenging the criminal proceedings pending against them for violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2141 on the ground that statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs engaged in the practice of midwifery without holding a certificate to practice midwifery. Criminal charges were filed against plaintiffs for violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2141 which prohibited the unlicensed practice of the healing arts. Plaintiffs petitioned for a writ of mandate claiming § 2141 was unconstitutional. The trial court denied the petition, and plaintiffs appealed. The court affirmed. In interpreting § 2141, the court applied the general rule that a specific provision should be construed with reference to the entire statutory system of which it was a part, in such a way that the various elements of the overall scheme were harmonized. In light of the total statutory scheme governing the practice of "healing arts," the court concluded that § 2141's prohibition against unlicensed persons treating a "physical condition" was intended to encompass midwifery. As for the constitutionality of § 2141, the court held that it was not unconstitutionally vague because an accused could reasonably be held to understand by its terms what conduct was prohibited. Further, § 2141 did not, as plaintiffs claimed, prohibit constitutionally protected conduct.


OUTCOME: Judgment denying plaintiffs' petition for a writ of mandate declaring the statute prohibiting the unlicensed practice of healing arts as unconstitutional was affirmed. The statute could reasonably be interpreted as prohibiting the unlicensed practice of midwifery. Further, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague because plaintiffs were sufficiently put on notice by the statute's terms of what conduct was prohibited.

 

405 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Barber v. State Personnel Board


S.F. No. 23457


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 395; 556 P.2d 306; 134 Cal. Rptr. 206; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 361



November 30, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 232905, Lloyd Allan Phillips, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Insofar as the judgment denies petitioner damage from the time of actual dismissal to the date the board's decision was filed, it is reversed. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed. The parties shall bear their respective costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of judgment denying a petition for writ of mandate compelling civil service reinstatement in favor of respondent state personnel board in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California).


OVERVIEW: Appellant civil servant sought review of judgment denying a petition for administrative writ of mandate compelling civil service reinstatement in favor of respondent state personnel board in connection with an administrative dismissal. Appellant contended respondent's termination failed to comply with due process because punitive measures were imposed prior to the time he was afforded a hearing. The court determined such retroactive application of a decision was proper, holding due process was complied with if a civil servant was provided with preremoval safeguards of notice of the proposed action, reasons therefore, a copy of the charges, and materials upon which the action was based. The court reasoned that, at all time prior, employees who filed a written answer to notice of punitive action were afforded a substantive hearing, and therefore, retroactive application of the decision did not require respondent to hold hearings for all persons disciplined prior to the decision. The court concluded it was error to deny appellant damages from the time of actual dismissal until the date the decision was filed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment denying appellant civil servant's petition for administrative writ of mandate in favor of respondent because he was afforded due process when punitive action taken provided notice of the charges, reasons therefor, and the opportunity to be heard. The court reversed only to the extent appellant was denied damages from the date of actual dismissal until the time respondent's decision was filed.

 

406 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hardie v. March Fong Eu


S.F. No. 23450


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 371; 556 P.2d 301; 134 Cal. Rptr. 201; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 359



November 29, 1976 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ of mandate and the temporary stay heretofore issued are discharged. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directed to the Secretary of State and the Commission, commanding them to refrain from enforcing sections 85200- 85202 of the Government Code, and to perform their election duties without regard to those sections. Since respondent county registrars have indicated a willingness to comply voluntarily with our decision, there appears no need to direct the peremptory writ to them, and accordingly we do not do so. ( Citizens for Jobs & Energy v. Fair Political Practices Com., supra, 16 Cal.3d at p. 675.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought a peremptory writ of mandate against respondents to bar enforcement of Cal. Gov't Code §§ 85200-85202.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners wished to expend a certain amount of money to promote an initiative on the ballot in an upcoming election. Under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 85200-85202, the amount which could be expended was limited. Petitioners sought a peremptory writ of mandate barring respondents from enforcing §§ 85200-85202 against them. The court granted the petition. The United States Supreme Court recently held that limitations on expenditures by or on behalf of a candidate for federal office without proof of an overriding governmental interest infringed upon U.S. Const. amend. I's guarantees of free speech and association. The court held that the principles expressed in that decision were equally applicable to the initiative process. When U.S. Const. amend. I rights were affected, a strict scrutiny test was applied, and under this test, restraints would be upheld only if incidental to the promotion of a substantial or compelling governmental interest, unrelated to speech and unattainable by means less intrusive upon U.S. Const. amend. I rights. The court determined that no compelling interests were shown to justify §§ 85200-85202's limitations; therefore, the sections were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court issued the peremptory writ of mandate against respondents prohibiting enforcement of those provisions which limited the amount to be expended in promoting an initiative. Without proof of an overriding compelling governmental interest, such restrictions unconstitutionally restricted the rights of free speech and association.

 

407 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Southern Pac. Transportation Co. v. Public Utilities Com.


S.F. No. 23217


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 308; 556 P.2d 289; 134 Cal. Rptr. 189; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 354



November 23, 1976 



DISPOSITION:  The decisions are annulled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of a decision of the Public Utilities Commission (California) that held Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 1202.3, which regulated public railroad crossings, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: After an auto-train collision at a crossing on petitioner's main rail line, the Public Utilities Commission (Commission) investigated the crossing and determined that it was a public crossing as that term was used in Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 1202. However, the Commission determined that § 1202.3 was unconstitutional on the ground it unlawfully delegated discretion to private litigants to require closing of crossings merely by commencing a proceeding under § 1202. The Commission further ordered petitioner to pay the costs of installing and maintaining protective devices. Petitioner sought review, and the court annulled the Commission's decision. The court held that the standard of public convenience and necessity were acceptable reasons for granting power, and this standard had been repeatedly applied in utility cases. The court looked to the language of § 1202.3 and determined that the standard for compelling crossing improvement was a finding of necessity and convenience. Therefor, there was no improper delegation of power.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the Public Utilities Commission's finding that the statute pertaining to regulation of public crossings was an unconstitutional delegation of authority to private persons. Rather, the court found that there was a requisite finding of public convenience and necessity which had previously been found to be an acceptable grant of authority.

 

408 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bakke v. Regents of University of Cal.


S.F. No. 23311


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 34; 553 P.2d 1152; 132 Cal. Rptr. 680; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 336



September 16, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the defendant and appellant for a rehearing was denied October 28, 1976, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Tobriner, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.
Stay granted by Regents of University of Cal. v. Bakke, 429 U.S. 953, 50 L. Ed. 2d 321, 97 S. Ct. 373, 1976 U.S. LEXIS 3535 (1976)
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Regents of University of Cal. v. Bakke, 429 U.S. 1090, 51 L. Ed. 2d 535, 97 S. Ct. 1098, 1977 U.S. LEXIS 645 (1977)
Affirmed in part and reversed in part by Regents of University of California v. Bakke, 438 U.S. 265, 57 L. Ed. 2d 750, 98 S. Ct. 2733, 1978 U.S. LEXIS 5 (1978)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yolo County, No. 31287, F. Leslie Manker, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed insofar as it determines that the special admission program is invalid; the judgment is reversed insofar as it denies Bakke an injunction ordering that he be admitted to the University, and the trial court is directed to enter judgment ordering Bakke to be admitted. Bakke shall recover his costs on these appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff applicant, a white male, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Yolo County (California), denying an order admitting him to defendant university because, although defendant's special admission program discriminated against him because of his race in violation of the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, he would not have been selected even if there had been no special program for minorities.


OVERVIEW: Defendant university used a special admission program with a racial classification intended to assist minorities, which barred plaintiff applicant, a white male, because of his race. The court held that the program violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV because the purpose of the racial classification did not serve a compelling state interest and rigid scrutiny showed that there were other reasonable ways to achieve the state's goals by means imposing a lesser limitation on the rights of the group disadvantaged by the classification. Defendant did not meet its burdens to establish that a program that discriminated against white applicants because of their race was necessary to achieve an integrated student body and improved medical care for minorities, and to demonstrate that those goals could not be achieved by means less detrimental to the rights of the majority. Because plaintiff demonstrated that defendant had unconstitutionally discriminated against him, the burden should have shifted to defendant to prove that the special admission program did not result in his exclusion, which defendant conceded it could not do. Plaintiff, therefore, was entitled to an order that he be admitted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's denial of an injunction ordering plaintiff applicant's admission to defendant university, but it affirmed the determination that the special admission program was invalid because its racial preference for persons less well qualified for the study of medicine violated the constitutional rights of nonminority applicants who were denied admission. The trial court was directed to order plaintiff's admission.

 

409 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bagley v. Manhattan Beach


L.A. No. 30523


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 22; 553 P.2d 1140; 132 Cal. Rptr. 668; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 335; 93 L.R.R.M. 2435



September 16, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C76275, Campbell M. Lucas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant firefighters challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied a writ of mandate to compel a ballot initiative requiring that disputes between appellant respondent by resolved in final and binding arbitrations, after respondent city refused to put it on the ballot.


OVERVIEW: Appellant firefighters proposed an initiative measure, which provided that unresolved disputes between the city and the recognized firemen's employee organization would be submitted to arbitration and that the arbitrator's award would be final and binding. The arbitration requirement applied not only to unresolved disputes pertaining to the interpretation or application of contracts but also to all disputes as to wages, hours, and terms of employment. Respondent city refused to put the initiative on the ballot and appellants sought a writ of mandate compelling the ballot initiative. The court denied the writ and appellants sought review. On appeal the court affirmed, holding that the language of Cal. Gov't Code § 36506, the provisions of the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 3500- 3510, and the legislature's repeated refusal to enact any law permitting general law cities to fix salaries by arbitration compelled the conclusion that the legislature intended the city council of a general law city to fix compensation, precluding the fixing of compensation by an arbitrator.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of writ of mandate in favor of respondent city because the government code, the provisions of the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act, and the legislature's repeated refusal to enact any law permitting general law cities to fix salaries by arbitration compelled the conclusion that the legislature intended the city council of a general law city, and not an arbitrator, to fix compensation.

 

410 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Grant


Crim. No. 18847


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 1; 553 P.2d 590; 132 Cal. Rptr. 430; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 333



September 7, 1976 



DISPOSITION:  The Adult Authority is directed to grant parole consideration to petitioner in accordance with the views set forth in this opinion and without regard to the provisions of former section 11531 which prescribe the minimum period prior to parole eligibility. 13 Petitioner is not entitled to release, however, until such time as the Adult Authority duly determines that he is eligible for parole under the 10-year-to-life term imposed by former section 11531. The order to show cause is discharged and the writ of habeas corpus denied.



13 Such parole consideration without regard to specific mandatory minimum terms prescribed under the Health and Safety Code is to be granted to all those recidivist narcotics offenders currently imprisoned under sentences enhanced by mandatory minimum-term provisions struck down by our holding herein. (See fn. 10, ante.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought a writ of habeas corpus after he was convicted of sale of marijuana and sentenced to state prison for 10 years to life without possibility of parole for a period of not less than 10 years, as a consequence of his admission of prior convictions, where, absent the provision of former Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11531, he would be eligible for parole after serving 40 months.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate was convicted for the sale of marijuana and sentenced to state prison for 10 years to life without possibility of parole for a period of not less than 10 years, because of his prior narcotics convictions. He sought a writ of habeas corpus, contending that the provision of former Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11531, which set his minimum period for parole eligibility, constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The court discharged the order to show cause and denied the writ of habeas corpus, but directed the Adult Authority to grant parole consideration to petitioner without regard to the provisions of the former statute, because it constituted both cruel and unusual punishment under the California Constitution, based on its overbreadth in absolutely prohibiting parole for substantial periods of time without regard to the gravity of the particular offense, the nature of the offender, or the existence of possible mitigating circumstances. The court further concluded that the former law was unusual in its severity compared to other punishments levied in California for far more serious crimes and disproportionately long compared to similar offenses in states.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the order to show cause and denied the writ of habeas corpus, but directed the Adult Authority to grant parole consideration to petitioner inmate without regard to the provisions of the former state law which set the minimum period prior to parole eligibility at 10 years for recidivist narcotics offenders, because the former law constituted both cruel and unusual punishment in violation of the California Constitution.

 

411 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Payne v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L.A. No. 30455


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 908; 553 P.2d 565; 132 Cal. Rptr. 405; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 331



September 3, 1976 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing respondent court to vacate its judgment in civil action No. C 33884, and, if it finds that petitioner is incarcerated and indigent, to conduct further proceedings in a manner consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner appealed from the denial by the Court of Appeal (California) of a writ of mandate to order respondent trial court to vacate a default judgment against petitioner in a civil action, arguing that, because he was incarcerated, he had been denied the right to attend the trial and defend himself and the right to counsel in violation of due process and equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a).


OVERVIEW: A default judgment was entered against petitioner prisoner, who had been denied permission to attend the trial and denied right to counsel. The trial court refused to vacate the default judgment, and the court of appeal refused to issue a writ of mandate. The court decided that petitioner was unconstitutionally deprived of his right of access to both counsel and the courts, that the appointment of counsel, while not an absolute right, was only feasible method of granting access rights to indigent prisoners, and that proceeding by writ of mandate was proper. The state could not carry its burden of demonstrating a compelling state interest to justify the denial of both the right to counsel and the right to attend the trial and defend. The court concluded that such unqualified deprivation violated due process and equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a). Respondent trial court had a duty to determine whether petitioner had been denied meaningful access to the courts, and failure to do so was grounds for writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent trial court to vacate its civil judgment against petitioner prisoner, holding that no state interests could be advanced to support the denial of access to both counsel and the courts, that such deprivation constituted a violation of both state and federal due process and equal protection, and that appointment of counsel was an indigent prisoner's only feasible remedy.

 

412 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Loder v. Municipal Court for San Diego Judicial Dist.


L.A. No. 30406


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 859; 553 P.2d 624; 132 Cal. Rptr. 464; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 328



September 2, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 341574, William T. Low, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff arrestee appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), denying a writ of mandate to compel erasure or return of the record of an arrest which did not result in conviction, asserting that official retention and dissemination of his arrest record violated his right of privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, and deprived him of due process under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a); U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff arrestee attacked a police officer who was beating plaintiff's wife. Charges against plaintiff were dismissed. Plaintiff sought a writ of mandate to erase his arrest record, asserting that official retention and dissemination of it violated his right of privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, and to due process under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a); U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV. A writ required a clear, present, ministerial duty, and a clear, present, beneficial right to its performance. The court found that defendants, municipal court judge and police officials, had no such duty. To comply with plaintiff's demand would violate Cal. Gov't Code § 6200, for destroying public or court records. Absent the duty requirement, the court did not consider the right. The court held that incursions into individual privacy, if justified by a compelling interest, were not prohibited. The purposes for consulting records of arrests not resulting in conviction law enforcement, criminal justice, and protection from recidivist offenders were a compelling interest, which, given the available safeguards, outweighed plaintiff's legitimate concern of improper uses of his record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, concluding that plaintiff arrestee was not entitled to a writ of mandate because defendants, municipal court judge and police officials, had no duty to erase his record of arrest not resulting in conviction, and defendants' compelling interests in consulting the record outweighed plaintiff's legitimate concern about its improper uses.

 

413 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Connolly Development, Inc. v. Superior Court of Merced County


S.F. No. 23225


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 803; 553 P.2d 637; 132 Cal. Rptr. 477; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 326



August 31, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On September 29, 1976, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writs of mandate and prohibition issued by the Court of Appeal are discharged, and the peremptory writs denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, property owner and lender, obtained an alternative writ of mandate from the Court of Appeal (California), which prevented the trial court from enforcing the state mechanics' lien and the stop notice laws on constitutional grounds. Being of statewide importance, the cause then came to the court upon petition for hearing following the decision of the appellate court.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, property owner and lender, had a mechanic's lien and stop notice filed against it by defendant subcontractor in the trial court. Defendant sought to foreclose the lien, and petitioners obtained an alternative writ from the appellate court, which restrained the trial court from proceeding in the action. The court held that the mechanic's lien and stop notice statutes were constitutional and they inflicted only a minimal deprivation of a property interest, and, that state policy strongly supported the preservation of laws which gave the laborer and materialman security for their claims. In balancing the respective interests, the court held that the safeguards provided by the law to protect property owners against unjustified liens were sufficient to comply with due process requirements. The court held that the alternative writ was discharged and other writs denied.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writ sought by petitioners, property owner and lender, and issued by the appellate court, and held that the mechanic's lien and stop notice laws were constitutional. The court found that the statutes inflicted only a minimal deprivation of a property interest and the safeguards provided to protect property owners against unjustified liens were sufficient to comply with due process.

 

414 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


S. P. Growers Asso. v. Rodriguez


L.A. No. 30595


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 719; 552 P.2d 721; 131 Cal. Rptr. 761; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 319



August 10, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Ventura County Judicial District of Ventura County, Nos. VC 04545-04551, 04554-04557, 04792, 04793, Albert T. Blanford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Ventura County Judicial District of Ventura County (California) for plaintiff in an unlawful detainer action, arguing that plaintiff improperly evicted them in retaliation for their strike and for filing a federal lawsuit.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were lemon pickers who were employees of plaintiff, a farm labor contractor. Plaintiff provided housing for defendants. Defendants walked-off their jobs over the implementation of an agreement. Defendants sued plaintiff under the Farm Labor Contractor Registration Act, 7 U.S.C.S. § 2041. Plaintiff served defendants with eviction notices, and when they failed to leave the premises, filed unlawful detainer actions under Cal. Code Civ. P. § 1161. The trial court held in favor of plaintiff. The court held that plaintiff evicted defendants for an improper reason: retaliation for their efforts to vindicate an important statutory right. The public policies furthered by protecting defendants from eviction outweighed the interests in preserving the summary nature of unlawful detainer proceedings. A defense of retaliatory eviction may be raised in an unlawful detainer proceeding.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed because the public policies furthered by protecting defendants from eviction outweighed the interest in preserving the summary nature of unlawful detainer proceedings. A defense of retaliatory eviction may be raised in an unlawful detainer proceeding.

 

415 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Collins


Crim. No. 19365


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 687; 552 P.2d 742; 131 Cal. Rptr. 782; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 317



August 6, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied September 8, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CRN 3528, Fiorenzo V. Lopardo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his jury conviction in the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) for three counts of first degree robbery pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 211.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was on trial for first degree robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211. After the jury began its deliberations, a juror requested to be excused from the panel. The trial court questioned her extensively and she stated that she was involved emotionally in the case and could not decide the case on the evidence and the law. The trial court dismissed the juror for good cause under Cal. Penal Code § 1089 and substituted an alternate juror. The jury continued its deliberations and found defendant guilty of three counts of first degree robbery. He appealed. The court affirmed and held that substitution of an alternate juror after final submission of the case to the jury was constitutional under Cal. Const. art. I, § 16. However, it was error not to instruct the jury to begin its deliberations anew after the substitution. The court concluded that the error was harmless and not prejudicial because the case against defendant was very strong. There was no reasonable probability that a more favorable verdict would have been returned if the jury had been properly instructed following the substitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first degree robbery. The court held that an alternate juror was substituted for good cause and the substitution was constitutionally permissible. However, the trial court erred by failing to instruct the jury to begin its deliberations anew after the substitution. The error was harmless and did not require reversal because there was no reasonable probability of a more favorable verdict for defendant.

 

416 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Choudhry v. Free


L.A. No. 30516


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 660; 552 P.2d 438; 131 Cal. Rptr. 654; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 315



August 3, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On September 2, 1976, the judgment was modified to read as printed above.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner potential candidate and voters sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent county clerk to accept a nominating petition for director of an irrigation district, which with its board of directors, were the real parties in interest. The court issued an alternative writ to examine the claim that Cal. Water Code § 21100, requiring a candidate for director to be a freeholder, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner potential candidate sought to file a nominating petition for director of the irrigation district, which with its board of directors, were the real parties in interest. Respondent county clerk refused to accept the petition on the ground that petitioner potential candidate was not a freeholder. Petitioner potential candidate and voters sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to accept the nominating papers for filing. The court issued an alternative writ to review the claim that Cal. Water Code § 21100 was unconstitutional. The court held § 21100 unconstitutional as applied to the real parties in interest, because it deprived both candidates and voters in the district of equal protection of the law. The court held that neither the right to vote nor the right to serve as an irrigation district director could be confined to freeholders.


OUTCOME: The court declared unconstitutional a section of the Irrigation District Law requiring petitioner potential candidate to be a freeholder. The section was unconstitutional as applied to the irrigation district and its board of directors, the real parties in interest, because it deprived irrigation district candidates and voters, including petitioner voters, of equal protection.

 

417 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Javorek v. Superior Court of Monterey County


S.F. No. 23324


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 629; 552 P.2d 728; 131 Cal. Rptr. 768; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 313



August 2, 1976 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondeat superior court to quash the levy of the writ of attachment and to quash the service of summons in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant nonresidents sought a writ of mandate to order the Superior Court of Monterey County (California) to grant their motions to quash service of summons for lack of personal jurisdiction, and to quash, vacate, or discharge plaintiff's attachment of obligations of the liability insurer to defend and indemnify defendants, in the underlying attachment case that was brought under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 537.3(c).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were in a car accident involving defendants' vehicle in Oregon. Plaintiffs sought to sue defendants in California, but were unable to obtain personal jurisdiction over them. Plaintiffs sought to obtain quasi in rem jurisdiction by attaching property of defendants located in California. The property attached consisted of defendants' liability insurance company's obligation to indemnify and defend them. The insurance company was located in California. The superior court granted the attachment, and defendants specially appeared to file motions to quash service of summons for lack of personal jurisdiction, and to quash, vacate, or discharge the attachment, which were denied. Defendants' application for a writ to the court of appeals was denied. On appeal, the court concluded that the obligations of defendants' liability insurer to defend and indemnify them were contingent and uncertain, and not of such a nature as to be subject to attachment so as to confer on the court below quasi in rem jurisdiction. Defendants were thus entitled to quash service of summons and the writ of attachment.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the superior court to quash the levy of the writ of attachment and to quash the service of summons because the obligations of defendants' liability insurer to defend and indemnify defendants could not be attached so as to confer on the court below quasi in rem jurisdiction.

California Housing Finance Agency v. Elliott


S.F. No. 23431


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 575; 551 P.2d 1193; 131 Cal. Rptr. 361; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 309



July 21, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied August 18, 1976, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ of mandate is discharged and the petition for a peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, state housing finance agency, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent, agency chairperson and acting president, to print revenue bonds, pursuant to the Zenovich-Moscone-Chacon Housing and Home Finance Act, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 41000 et seq., and petitioner's resolutions.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, state housing finance agency, sought a writ of mandate compelling respondent agency chairperson and acting president to print revenue bonds, pursuant to the Zenovich-Moscone-Chacon Housing and Home Finance Act, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 41000 et seq. and resolutions promulgated thereunder. Respondent, by way of a demurrer to the petition for mandate, asserted that the Act and respondent's resolutions suffered from numerous constitutional infirmities and refused to issue the requested bonds without an appropriate order from the court. The court concluded that the Act, construed to incorporate the provisions of Cal. Const. art. XXXIV, § 1, which required local elections before housing projects could be developed, was constitutional. The court found that the Act and petitioner's resolutions did not violate the constitutional prohibition against the gift of public funds and extension of public credit. However, rather than compelling respondent to print the bonds, the court left petitioner free to choose whether or not it wished to proceed with the bond issuance in light of the local election requirement. The court denied the writ.


OUTCOME: The alternative writ of mandate was discharged and the petition for a peremptory writ was denied because the housing and home finance law was constitutional, but the state constitution required local elections before housing projects could be developed. Accordingly, the court did not compel respondent state housing finance agency chairperson to print revenue bonds in light of the election requirement.

National Ins. Underwriters v. Carter


L.A. No. 30384


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 380; 551 P.2d 362; 131 Cal. Rptr. 42; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 284



June 30, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied July 28, 1976. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 997588, Campbell M. Lucas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant insureds appealed an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted plaintiff insurer's motion for summary judgment in an action seeking declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: Defendant insureds owned an airplane that was maintained at an airport. Plaintiff insurer issued defendants a liability insurance policy for the plane. While the policy was in effect, the plane crashed. As a result of the accident, two of the three nonpaying guests were killed and a residence was damaged. Defendants had given the guests permission to use the plane. The victims' parents instituted a wrongful death action against defendants. In addition, the owners of the residence filed a suit against defendants seeking recovery for the damage done to their property. Plaintiff denied any liability under defendants' policy and filed a declaratory relief action to determine the extent of its liability. The trial court granted plaintiff's motion for summary judgment and defendants appealed. The court found that the pilot exclusion clause was unambiguous. Defendants could not have expected plaintiff to afford coverage without regard to the identity and qualifications of those persons entrusted with flying the aircraft. Further, the court found that Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 24230 et seq. did not contain an expression of a public policy mandating insurance coverage.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because plaintiff was not obligated to defend the pending actions or to pay any judgments entered against defendants.

 

420 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Crawford v. Board of Education


L.A. No. 30485


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 280; 551 P.2d 28; 130 Cal. Rptr. 724; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 294



June 28, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 822854, Alfred Gitelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, except insofar as it defines "desegregated" school in terms of specific percentages, the judgment of the trial court is affirmed and the case is remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion. Plaintiffs shall recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant school district appealed from a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which ordered defendant to prepare and implement a reasonably feasible plan for the desegregation of its schools.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, minority children, filed a class action against defendant school district for failing to desegregate the school district. A trial court found defendant had failed to take any steps to attempt to alleviate the segregated condition. The court ordered defendant to prepare and implement a reasonably feasible plan for the desegregation of its schools. Defendant appealed from the judgment contending the segregated condition of its district's schools should properly be characterized as de facto rather than de jure and thus it owed no constitutional duty to alleviate such de facto school segregation. The reviewing court found the findings in the case adequately supported the trial court's conclusion that the segregation was de jure in nature. However the court stated that all public school districts bore an obligation under the state constitution to undertake reasonably feasible steps to alleviate school segregation, regardless of the cause of such segregation. Therefore the court properly ordered defendant to prepare and implement a reasonably feasible desegregation plan. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment ordering defendant to desegregate its schools was affirmed, because all public school districts bore an obligation under the state constitution to undertake reasonably feasible steps to alleviate school segregation, regardless of the cause of such segregation.

 

421 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


NAACP v. San Bernardino City Unified School Dist.


L.A. No. 30484


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 311; 551 P.2d 48; 130 Cal. Rptr. 744; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 295



June 28, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 155286, Paul Egly, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, except insofar as it defines "desegregation" with reference to a racial balance concept, the judgment of the trial court is affirmed and the case is remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion. Plaintiffs shall recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant school district appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which ordered defendant to desegregate its schools in the desegregation class action filed by plaintiff association and plaintiff minority students.


OVERVIEW: Defendant school district decided against implementing any desegregation plan that required mandatory busing of students. Instead, defendant adopted a planning unit proposal that provided for the voluntary integration within a planning unit group. Plaintiff association and plaintiff minority students filed a class action lawsuit, which alleged that defendant failed to meet its constitutional obligation to desegregate and failed to comply with various statutes that were directed at the elimination of racial imbalance in the public schools. Without finding that defendant had engaged in de jure segregation or that schools were separate but unequal, the trial court ordered defendant to prepare and implement a more effective desecration plan that the totally voluntary planning unit program. On appeal, defendant argued that it had no constitutional duty to alleviate segregation without such a finding. The court disagreed and held that all school boards were constitutionally required to take reasonable steps to alleviate segregation, regardless of its cause. The court remanded the case for reevaluation of defendant's plan without defining desegregation in terms of racial imbalance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment ordering defendant school district to desegregate its schools with a modification of the definition of segregation, because defendant had a constitutional obligation to take reasonable and feasible steps that promised meaningful progress in the alleviation of segregation in its schools regardless of the cause of such segregation.

 

422 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Olivas


Crim. No. 19073


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 236; 551 P.2d 375; 131 Cal. Rptr. 55; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 292



June 22, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied September 7, 1976. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-518365, Gerald Malkan, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: There remains the question of the proper disposition in defendant's case. Since the maximum period of incarceration which could have initially been imposed by the sentencing court (six months) has already expired, defendant must be released from custody if not otherwise under lawful restraint. The judgment is modified to provide that defendant's commitment to the Youth Authority be terminated upon the finality of this decision. As so modified the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile appealed from a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which committed defendant to the California youth authority following a conviction of misdemeanor assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile appealed from an order that committed him to the California youth authority following a conviction of misdemeanor assault. The question at issue was whether a misdemeanant between the ages of 16 and 21 could constitutionally be committed to the youth authority for a term potentially longer than the maximum jail term that might have been imposed for the same offense if committed by a person over the age of 21 years. The reviewing court concluded that the selective process that permitted the extended incarceration of youthful misdemeanants constituted a denial of equal protection of the law. Because of defendant's commitment to the youth authority, defendant remained incarcerated and faced a potential period of confinement several times longer than the longest jail term that might have been imposed. The court assessed defendant's equal protection claim by first finding that defendant's personal liberty was a fundamental interest. The court then applied a strict scrutiny standard and found Cal. Penal Code § 1770 was unconstitutional. Therefore the court modified the decision so defendant was released from the youth authority.


OUTCOME: The court modified the decision so as to release defendant from the youth authority, because defendant had been committed to the youth authority for a term longer than the maximum jail term that would have been imposed for the same offense if committed by a person over the age of 21 years.

 

423 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Stanson v. Mott


L.A. No. 30567


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 206; 551 P.2d 1; 130 Cal. Rptr. 697; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 290



June 22, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On July 22, 1976, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 354505, Charles W. Froehlich, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in favor of defendant is reversed and the case is remanded for proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which sustained the demurrer of defendant, the Director of the state Department of Parks and Recreation, in plaintiff's suit alleging that defendant illegally expended public funds to promote the passage of a bond issue.


OVERVIEW: California voters approved a bond issue to provide funds for the future acquisition of park land and recreational and historical facilities. Plaintiff taxpayer filed a suit alleging that defendant, the Director of the state Department of Parks and Recreation (department), had illegally authorized the department to expend public funds to promote the passage of the bond issue. Plaintiff sought to have defendant repay the funds to the state treasury. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded, holding that in the absence of clear and explicit legislative authorization, a public agency could not expend public funds to promote a partisan position in an election campaign. Although the department had statutory authority to disseminate fair information to the public relating to the bond election, plaintiff specifically alleged that public funds were expended for "promotional," rather than "informational," purposes, and so stated a valid cause of action. The court concluded that defendant could be held personally liable to repay expended funds only if he failed to exercise due care in authorizing the expenditure of the funds.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded. The demurrer of defendant agency director against plaintiff taxpayer's suit alleging illegal expenditure of public funds was improperly sustained, because plaintiff specifically alleged that public funds were expended for illegal "promotional," rather than authorized "informational," purposes relating to a partisan initiative, and so stated a valid cause of action.

Birkenfeld v. Berkeley


S.F. No. 23370


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 129; 550 P.2d 1001; 130 Cal. Rptr. 465; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 280



June 16, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 428971, Robert L. Bostick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which declared a charter amendment regarding rent control void and enjoined defendant from enforcing the amendment against plaintiff landlords.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landlords brought a class action against defendant city contending a charter amendment regarding rent control was void. The trial court declared the amendment void and enjoined defendant from enforcing the amendment because defendant was not faced with a serious public emergency which was a constitutional prerequisite to the imposition of rent controls under the police power. Defendant appealed from the decision. The court found that the charter amendment transgressed the constitutional limits of the police power, not because of its objectives, but because certain procedures it provided imposed heavy burdens upon landlords not reasonably related to the accomplishment of those objectives. The amendment required a blanket rollback of all controlled rents and prohibited any adjustments in maximum rents except under a unit-by-unit procedure. The rollback provision imposed hardship on the landlords. Furthermore, the charter amendment violated the requirements under the police power. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

425 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Busch v. Projection Room Theater


L.A. Nos. 30432, 30433, 30434, 30435, 30436


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 42; 550 P.2d 600; 130 Cal. Rptr. 328; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 275



June 1, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 56568-C 56572, Charles S. Vogel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant law enforcement officers challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that dismissed appellants' actions and sustained respondents demurrers that stated that appellants failed to state a cause of action either under the public nuisance statutes or the Red Light Abatement Law.


OVERVIEW: Appellant law enforcement officers sought injunctive and other relief against respondents who, according to the complaints, operated book stores or motion picture theaters which exhibited obscene magazines or films. Respondents filed general demurrers to each complaint, asserting that appellants failed to state a cause of action either under the public nuisance statutes or the Red Light Abatement Law. The trial court sustained the demurrers without leave to amend and entered judgments of dismissal. Appellants challenged that judgment and the court reversed. The court held that the abatement in the present action must be directed to particular books or films which have been adjudged obscene following a fair and full adversary hearing, rather than against the premises in which the material is sold, exhibited or displayed. Additionally, the court held that evidence established that appellants' complaint was sufficient to state a cause of action based upon a general nuisance theory and dismissal was not warranted.


OUTCOME: The judgment dismissing appellant law enforcement officers' action against respondents, the owners of book stores or motion picture theaters which exhibited obscene magazines or films, was reversed, because appellants' complaint was sufficient to state a cause of action based upon a general nuisance theory. Any abatement had to be directed to particular books or films which had been adjudged obscene following a fair and full adversary hearing.

People v. Bradford


Crim. No. 19058


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 8; 549 P.2d 1225; 130 Cal. Rptr. 129; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 273



May 27, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied June 24, 1976. Wright, C. J., and Tobriner, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR10739, Robert Lee Shaw, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As so modified the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment entered on jury verdicts from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) convicting him of assault with a deadly weapon upon a peace officer, Cal. Penal Code, §245(b), by using a firearm, Cal. Penal Code § 2022.5, exhibiting a firearm, Cal. Penal Code § 417, and possession of a concealable firearm by an ex-felon, Cal. Penal Code § 12021.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and an accomplice robbed a bank in Ventura County and committed various other crimes in Ventura County as they were making their getaway, including assault of a police officer. The chase ended in Los Angeles County. Appealing from the Ventura County judgment, defendant contended that the separate prosecutions in Los Angeles and Ventura Counties violated Cal. Penal Code § 654. The court held that joinder of the prosecutions was precluded by Cal. Penal Code § 777. The court further held that Cal. Penal Code § 783 was inapplicable because the offenses were committed outside the getaway car at an identifiable spot along the highway; therefore, venue for each offense was in the county where it was committed. The court also held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in ordering the sentences imposed run consecutively to sentences imposed in previous proceedings because defendant's assault on the police officer was a particularly aggravated offense. The court, however, stayed the execution of defendant's firearm possession offense because his possession of the officer's revolver was not antecedent and separate from his use of it in assaulting the officer.


OUTCOME: The court modified and affirmed defendant's conviction. Execution of defendant's sentence for possession of a concealable firearm by an ex-felon was stayed because his possession of the police officer's revolver was not antecedent and separate from his use of the revolving in assaulting the officer.

 

427 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. White


Crim. No. 17986


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 791; 549 P.2d 537; 129 Cal. Rptr. 769; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 260



April 30, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied June 9, 1976. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. Cr 28942, William T. Low, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of conviction for first-degree robbery in the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which ordered that his sentence be consecutive to any prior incompleted sentences.


OVERVIEW: In each of three separate trials, for each of three separate robberies, defendant received a sentence of five years to life. The first two sentences were to be concurrent, and the third was to be consecutive to them. To the resultant minimum aggregate term of 10 years were added years of imprisonment imposed pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5, because defendant used a firearm in the robberies. Under Cal. Penal Code § 3024(d), defendant's aggregate minimum term, had he been convicted of the three felonies in a single trial, would have been 10 years. Accordingly, defendant appealed from the judgment of conviction in the third trial, on the ground that he was denied equal protection of the law. The court affirmed the judgment, on the ground that § 3024(d), was concerned with, and limited to, the minimum sentences imposed for the underlying felonies and had no application to the additional terms imposed under § 12022.5. Also, because defendant failed to establish that the trial judge acted improvidently, the court rejected defendant's contention that the trial court abused its discretion in imposing the third sentence consecutively.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction for robbery, with sentence to be consecutive to any prior incompleted sentences, was affirmed because defendant's aggregate minimum sentence for felonies tried separately did not exceed the statutory maximum minimum sentence for felonies tried together.

Cartwright v. Board of Chiropractic Examiners


L.A. No. 30454


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 762; 548 P.2d 1134; 129 Cal. Rptr. 462; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 258



April 28, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-55946, David A. Thomas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the California State Board of Chiropractic Examiners, sought review after the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered an order commanding defendant to set aside the order that revoked plaintiff doctor's license to practice. Defendant revoked plaintiff's license based on two charges of conviction of a crime involving moral turpitude in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 315, 316.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, the California State Board of Chiropractic Examiners, revoked plaintiff doctor's license to practice based on two charges of conviction of a crime involving moral turpitude. The trial court granted plaintiff's petition for a writ of mandate. One conviction was based on plaintiff's plea of guilty to a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 315, however, no competent evidence of the circumstances surrounding the offense was introduced at the board hearing. The trial court concluded that the bare conviction did not establish moral turpitude. The other conviction was based on plaintiff's plea of nolo contendere to a charge of violating Cal. Penal Code § 316. Although defendant did receive evidence of the circumstances surrounding the § 316 conviction, the trial court concluded that a conviction based on a plea of nolo contendere could not be the basis for discipline. The court agreed and affirmed the judgment. The court disapproved of Christensen v. Orr, 275 Cal. App. 2d 12, 79 Cal. Rptr. 656 (1969), which permitted a conviction based on a nolo contendere plea to have consequences adverse to the pleader outside of the criminal pleadings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment that ordered respondent, the California State Board of Chiropractic Examiners, to aside the order that revoked plaintiff doctor's license. The court held that convictions based on nolo contendere pleas could not serve as the basis for discipline or other adverse legal consequences unless a statute expressly specified such convictions as a basis for such consequences.

 

429 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Westinghouse Electric Corp. v. Superior Court of Alameda County


S.F. No. 23376


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 849; 549 P.2d 129; 129 Cal. Rptr. 657; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 264



Apr. 27, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] opinion modified and reprinted (see 17 C.3d 259)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner contractors sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Alameda County (California) to order a change of venue, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 394, for the entire underlying cause of action for damages, brought by the San Francisco Bay Area Rapid Transit District (BART) against petitioner and other contractors who provided BART with allegedly defective equipment and faulty services.


OVERVIEW: The San Francisco Bay Area Rapid Transit District (BART) sought damages from several petitioner contractors, who provided allegedly defective equipment and faulty services. Petitioners filed change of venue motions, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 394, to transfer the case out of respondent Superior Court of Alameda County. Respondent denied the motions for change of venue, and ordered a separate trial on certain causes of action related to an accident on one of BART's trains. Petitioners appealed. On appeal, the court directed respondent to order a change of venue to a neutral county for all causes of action except those related to the accident and held that one defendant was not doing business in Alameda County as defined in § 394 and was entitled to a change of venue. In order to avoid multiplicity of litigation, the court directed all causes not related to the accident removed to the same neutral county. As to the causes of action related to the accident, the court held that § 394 would have been invoked only if a nonresident party sought a change of venue. Because only a resident party sought a change of venue, that litigation had to stay in Alameda County.


OUTCOME: The court directed respondent Superior Court of Alameda County (California) to order a change of venue to a neutral county for all causes of action except those for which the trial court had ordered a separate trial because one of the petitioner contractors was not doing business in Alameda County, that petitioner was thus entitled to a change of venue, and transfer for all petitioners would avoid duplicative litigation.

 

430 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Westinghouse Electric Corp. v. Superior Court of Alameda County


S.F. No. 23376


Supreme Court of California


17 Cal. 3d 259; 551 P.2d 847; 131 Cal. Rptr. 231; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 293



April 27, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified May 6, 1976. Petitions for a rehearing were denied June 24 and July 21, 1976.



DISPOSITION: Let writs of mandate issue directing respondent court to order a change of venue to a neutral county in action No. 457291-8 and action No. 467571-0.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners various contractors filed a petition for a writ of mandate to challenge the judgment of respondent, the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied their motion to transfer venue pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 394 and to direct respondent to order a change in venue to transfer the matter to a neutral county, in an action for damages for providing allegedly defective equipment and faulty services.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, real party in interest, a multi-county agency, filed a suit against petitioners various contractors who were nonresidents of the current county, for providing allegedly defective equipment and faulty services. Petitioner filed a petition for a writ of mandate to challenge respondent superior court's denial of petitioners' motion to transfer venue under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 394. Respondent held § 394 did not apply to a multi-county agency and petitioners were ineligible under § 394 because each petitioner conducted business in the current county. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and held petitioners were entitled to a change of venue to a neutral location because plaintiff was the type of local agency within a certain county which had a potentially prejudicial advantage in a suit against the nonresident petitioners; petitioners were not doing business in the county because their activities in the county were not substantial enough to be reasonably viewed as being intimately identified with the affairs of the people of the community; and the right of a petitioner to transfer venue was not diminished by the illegibility of another petitioner to transfer venue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed respondent superior court, issued a writ of mandate, and held the petitioners were entitled to a transfer of venue to a neutral location under the applicable statute because plaintiff real party in interest was the type of local agency within a certain county which had a potentially prejudicial advantage in an action against nonresident petitioners and because petitioners were not doing business in the local county.

Dawson v. Los Altos Hills


S.F. No. 23341


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 676; 547 P.2d 1377; 129 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 249



April 8, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 261072, John D. Foley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied plaintiffs all relief in their action, under Cal. Sts. & High. Code §10400, for a declaration that a sewer assessment district and a resolution adopted prior to its formation were illegal and to enjoin defendant Town of Los Altos Hills and its agents from levying assessments proposed pursuant to the formation of the district.


OVERVIEW: Defendant Town of Los Altos Hills formed a special assessment district to pay for capacity rights to a sewer system used by a portion of the town. That part of town had not produced enough tax revenue to pay for a contract which obligated defendant to pay a fixed sum for capacity rights. After a public hearing, defendant levied assessments on properties in the district. Plaintiff property owners sought, under Cal. Sts. & High. Code §10400, a judicial declaration that the assessment district and its acts were illegal, and an injunction to prevent any further assessments. The trial court denied plaintiffs any relief, and plaintiffs appealed. The court affirmed and held that the assessment district was legal, the sewer system accrued benefits to the assessed properties, and the assessments were not disproportional to the benefits to plaintiffs' properties. Defendant had authority, under the Municipal Improvement Act of 1913, Cal. Sts. & High. Code § 10000 et seq., to create sewer assessment districts. As long as special benefit to plaintiffs' properties resulted from the expenditure of public funds, defendant could assess accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of relief to plaintiff property owners because defendant's formation of the assessment district was legal, the sewer system accrued benefits to the assessed properties, and the assessment was not disproportional to the those benefits.
