People v. Williams


Crim. No. 19098


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 663; 547 P.2d 1000; 128 Cal. Rptr. 888; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 247



April 2, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-32001, Gilbert Harelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted defendant of first-degree robbery, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 211, 211a.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree robbery under Cal. Penal Code §§211 & 211a. The preliminary hearing testimony of a witness that was considered an unavailable witness within the meaning of Cal. Evid. Code § 240(a)(5) was admitted by the trial court under the former testimony exception to the hearsay rule set forth in Cal. Evid. Code § 1291(a)(2). This testimony was not adverse to defendant. The court reversed the judgment because the testimony of a police detective regarding the witness' prior inconsistent statement that implicated defendant was not admissible under Cal. Evid. Code § 1235. The witness' statement was not inconsistent with testimony at the hearing in which the admissibility of his prior inconsistent statement arose. The court determined that it was reasonably probable that a result more favorable to defendant would have been reached in the absence of the error. Used as substantive evidence, the erroneously admitted evidence amounted to a confession.


OUTCOME: The judgment against defendant was reversed because the testimony of a police detective regarding a witness' prior inconsistent statement that implicated defendant was not admissible and a result more favorable to defendant would have been reached in the absence of the error.

 

433 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


National Geographic Soc. v. State Board of Equalization


S.F. No. 23295


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 637; 547 P.2d 458; 128 Cal. Rptr. 682; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 245



April 1, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 577928, Robert J. Drewes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter judgment for defendant Board of Equalization.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State Board of Equalization sought review of an order from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which entered judgment in favor of plaintiff out of state seller ordering that defendant refund use taxes to plaintiff under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6203 and Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6204.


OVERVIEW: In defendant State Board of Equalization's suit against plaintiff out of state seller for failure to pay use taxes on mail order sales under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6203 and Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6204, the trial court ordered defendant to refund use taxes already paid by plaintiff. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the use taxes did not violate the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and U.S. Const. art. I, §8(3) because there was a sufficient nexus between the state and plaintiff so as to the imposition of a use tax collection liability. The court held that where an out-of-state seller conducted a substantial mail order business with residents of a state imposing a use tax on such purchasers and the seller's connection with the taxing state was not exclusively by means of the instruments of interstate commerce, the slightest presence within such taxing state independent of any connection through interstate commerce permitted the state to impose on the seller the duty of collecting the use tax from such mail order purchasers and the liability for failure to do so..


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of plaintiff out of state seller in defendant State Board of Equalization's suit. Where seller conducted mail order business with state's residents and the seller's connection with the state was not exclusively by means of the instruments of interstate commerce, the slightest presence within the state permitted imposition on the seller the duty of collecting use tax and liability for failure to do so.

 

434 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


T. M. Cobb Co. v. County of Los Angeles


L.A. No. 30474


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 606; 547 P.2d 431; 128 Cal. Rptr. 655; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 243; 19 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 305



April 1, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 13104, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter a summary judgment in favor of plaintiff and against defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation sought review from Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered summary judgment in favor of defendant County of Los Angeles in action for recovery of personal property taxes. Plaintiff contended that the procedures for summary seizure and sale in respect to the collection of taxes on unsecured property, pursuant to former Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 2914- 2921, were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A third party company secured a loan and granted security interest in all of its personal property and fixtures. Upon default, plaintiff corporation purchased the collateral at a private sale. Before the sale, defendant County of Los Angeles had posted a notice of sale and seizure of the subject property and fixtures. Plaintiff initiated action against defendant for recovery of personal property taxes. The trial court entered summary judgment in favor of defendant, and plaintiff sought review. The court held that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 2914, was unconstitutional insofar as it authorized a sale of plaintiff's property and fixtures without affording him an administrative hearing prior to such sale. The court held that plaintiff acquired the personal property and fixtures at the private sale free and clear of any right of defendant because the bank's security interest was superior to the interest of defendant and defendant could not seize and sell the property and fixtures for delinquent taxes. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded with directions to enter summary judgment in favor of plaintiff against defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed summary judgment of the trial court and remanded to enter summary judgment in favor of plaintiff corporation against defendant County of Los Angeles. The court held that plaintiff acquired the personal property and fixtures at the private sale free and clear of any right of defendant and that defendant could not seize and sell the property and fixtures for delinquent taxes without affording plaintiff an administrative hearing.

 

435 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Bouquet


S.F. No. 23262


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 583; 546 P.2d 1371; 128 Cal. Rptr. 427; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 241



March 19, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. P 20128, Robert D. Carter, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment below and remand the case for proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged certain provisions of an interlocutory judgment dissolving the marriage and determining the property rights of the parties rendered in the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), contending that his earnings and accumulations after the date of separation were his separate property pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 5118.


OVERVIEW: Cal. Civ. Code § 5118 was amended during the separation of appellant husband and respondent wife to make the earnings and accumulations of a husband while spouses are separated, separate property. The trial court ruled that only the earnings and accumulations acquired by appellant after the effective date of the amendment to § 5118 were appellant's separate property, and appellant challenged the trial court judgment, contending that the amendment to § 5118 required retroactive effect. The court reversed the trial court's judgment, holding that the amendment to § 5118 required retroactive application and the retroactive application, while it did deprive respondent of her property, did not violate her constitutional right to due process because it was necessary to abrogate rights in marital property that derived from the patently unfair former law. Although legislative enactments are presumed to operate prospectively, the court determined that retroactive application was consistent with the legislative intent, as evidenced by the probable unconstitutionality of the former statute and a letter of legislative intent published in a Senate resolution after enactment of the amendment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded for proceedings consistent with its opinion, holding that the amendment making earnings and accumulations acquired by a husband during separation separate property required retroactive application and that such application did not constitute an unconstitutional deprivation of the respondent wife's property.

 

436 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Schwalbe v. Jones


S.F. No. 23072


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 514; 546 P.2d 1033; 128 Cal. Rptr. 321; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 237



March 12, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 373913, Lyle E. Cook, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff parents of decedent sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which was in favor of defendant husband of decedent on plaintiffs' claim for willful misconduct. Defendant's motion for nonsuit on plaintiff's negligence claim was granted on the ground that Cal. Veh. Code § 17158 precluded recovery on that theory.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the judgment in an action brought by plaintiff parents of decedent against defendant husband of decedent for negligence and willful misconduct concerning a car accident. The trial court granted defendant's motion for nonsuit on the negligence count on the ground that Cal. Veh. Code § 17158 precluded recovery and a verdict for defendant was returned on the count alleging willful misconduct. The court concluded that the motion for nonsuit on the negligence count was properly granted. The court found that the legislature had reasonably concluded that the owner of a motor vehicle, whether he drives it himself or selects another to act as his chauffeur, should not recover for injuries sustained by him due to the negligent operation of that vehicle. The court determined that the refusal to give jury instructions proffered by plaintiffs was not error because the proffered instructions simply made explicit what was clearly implicit in the instructions given. The court also determined that the trial court properly refused to read to the jury certain statutory provisions because to do so would have been seriously misleading.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against plaintiff parents of decedent. Defendant husband of decedent's motion for nonsuit on the negligence count was properly granted because the owner of a motor vehicle should not recover for injuries sustained by him due to the negligent operation of that vehicle. Jury instructions proffered by plaintiffs simply made explicit what was clearly implicit in the instructions given.

 

437 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Eu v. Chacon


S.F. No. 23420


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 465; 546 P.2d 289; 128 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 232



March 8, 1976 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue. This order is final forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state official appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which enjoined petitioner from interfering with the candidacies of non-attorney judges seeking to run for justice court judgeships.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state official was enjoined from interfering with the candidacies of non-attorney judges seeking reelection, which petitioner appealed on the basis that the non-attorney judges were not qualified pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 71701. The court held that as § 71701 required an attorney judge fill each justice court vacancy, the non-attorney judges did not qualify to run for reelection. The court found that the term "vacancy," as used in the statute, applied to vacancies arising in the course of a fixed term and those arising at the expiration of a fixed term even though the incumbent continues to hold office until the appointment of a successor. The court also held that its interpretation of § 71701 did not discriminate against the non-attorney judges, as there were no members of the supposedly favored class, which the non-attorney judges proposed. Finally, the court held that the need for a qualified judiciary operating at the entry level of the state court system was a sufficient compelling government interest furthering the requirements of § 71701.


OUTCOME: The order enjoining petitioner state official from interfering in the candidacies of non-attorney judges seeking reelection was discharged and a peremptory writ of mandate was issued precluding non-attorney judges from running for election. A newly enacted statute required that only attorney candidates were qualified to fill vacancies, including those created by virtue of reelection.

People ex rel. Busch v. Projection Room Theater


L.A. No. 30432


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 360; 546 P.2d 733; 128 Cal. Rptr. 229; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 227



Mar. 4, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] opinion vacated and reprinted (See 17 C.3d 42)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff law enforcement officers appealed the judgments of the trial court (California), which dismissed plaintiff's nuisance actions against defendant adult book stores in a consolidated action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff law enforcement officers sought injunctive and other relief against defendant adult bookstores for exhibiting obscene magazines and films. Plaintiffs alleged that defendants' operations constituted a public nuisance subject to abatement and regulation, pursuant to either the general public nuisance statutes of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 3479, 3480 or Cal. Penal Code §§ 370, 371 or under the Red Light Abatement Law, Cal. Penal Code § 11225 et seq. The trial court dismissed plaintiffs' action, and plaintiffs appealed. The court reversed, holding that the exhibition of obscene magazines and films was indecent conduct that properly constituted a public nuisance subject to abatement, that the public nuisance statutes did not violate the constitutional principle against prior restraint of presumptively protected materials, but that the term "lewdness" did not include the exhibition of obscene magazines or films in bookstores or theaters.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order dismissing plaintiff law enforcement officers' nuisance actions, holding that the exhibition of obscene magazines and films was indecent conduct properly constituting a public nuisance subject to abatement and that the public nuisance statutes did not violate the constitutional principle against prior restraint of presumptively protected materials.

Agric. Labor Relations Bd. v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 23349


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 392; 546 P.2d 687; 128 Cal. Rptr. 183; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 228; 91 L.R.R.M. 2657; 78 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P53,800



March 4, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On March 31, 1976, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner California Agricultural Labor Relations Board filed a writ of mandate to review the decision of respondents, the Superior Courts of Tulare and Fresno Counties (California), which issued various orders enjoining enforcement of Cal. Code Regs. tit. 8, pt. II, ch. 9, §§ 20900-20901, which permitted qualified access to agricultural property by farm labor organizers.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner agricultural labor board sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent trial courts to vacate orders enjoining regulations under Cal. Code Regs. tit. 8, pt. II, ch. 9, §§ 20900-20901 that were adopted by petitioner, which granted a qualified right of access to growers' premises by labor organizers. Growers, the real parties in interest, filed actions attacking the validity of the regulations and seeking to prevent their enforcement. Respondents enjoined enforcement of the access regulations, and petitioner sought a writ of mandate requiring respondents to vacate their orders. On appeal, the court granted petitioner's action for writ of mandate. The access regulation was constitutional as it did not deprive real parties in interest of their property rights without due process of law, or constitute a taking without compensation. Incidental damages to property from governmental activities were not considered a taking of property. Further, the regulation was within the scope of authority conferred under Cal. Gov't Code § 11373, and was reasonably necessary to effectuate the purpose of the regulation under Cal. Gov't Code § 11374.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner agricultural labor board's action for writ of mandate. The court held that an access regulation was constitutional because it did not violate due process, and did not constitute a taking of property rights without compensation. The regulation was otherwise valid because it was within the scope of authority conferred, and was reasonably necessary to effectuate the purpose of the statute.

 

440 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Scott


Crim. No. 18693


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 242; 546 P.2d 327; 128 Cal. Rptr. 39; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 219



February 24, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied March 24, 1976. McComb, J., Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 4760, Charles R. Best, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged with possession of LSD and marijuana, appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which, on remand, affirmed the decision of the appellate court vacating the trial court's motion to suppress order.


OVERVIEW: Police officers found contraband on defendant, who they volunteered to drive to his destination. The trial court, on remand, affirmed the decision of the appellate court vacating the trial court's motion to suppress order. The court reversed the trial court's judgment. The court held that it was not bound by the law of the case doctrine to follow the appellate court's ruling because its misapplication of the controlling law resulted in defendant's subsequent guilty plea and conviction, which otherwise would not have occurred. The pat-down search was impermissible because nothing in the evidence could have led the officers to believe that defendant was armed and dangerous. The court held that the officers should have informed defendant that he had a right to refuse the ride, and that if he accepted the ride, he would have been subject to a pat-down search for weapons. Because defendant was not presented with this option and did not consent to the search, the pat-down was an unreasonable invasion of defendant's privacy in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 13 and U.S. Const. amend. IV. Thus, the contraband seized as a result of the search was inadmissible in evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, which affirmed the appellate court's vacating of a motion to suppress order earlier granted by the trial court, because the contraband seized resulted from the officer's unlawful pat-down search of defendant for weapons.

Torrance v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L.A. No. 30507


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 195; 545 P.2d 1313; 127 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 216



February 20, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On March 19, 1976, the opinion and judgment were modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ of mandate is discharged and the petition for a peremptory writ is denied. Real party in interest shall recover its costs in an amount to be determined by the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A property owner, a real party in interest, challenged an order of the Court of Appeal for the Second Appellate District, Division Five (California), which granted petitioner city's alternative writ of mandate ordering the issuance of a peremptory writ to direct respondent trial court to vacate its order setting aside petitioner's abandonment of a condemnation action and take further action consistent with the appellate court's opinion.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city was granted an alternative writ of mandate and a peremptory writ of mandate ordering respondent trial court to vacate its order setting aside petitioner's abandonment of a condemnation action. On appeal, the court discharged the alternative writ of mandate, denied the petition for a peremptory writ, and directed respondent to allow a property owner, a real party in interest, to recover its costs as respondent's findings were not beyond its power to make given the evidence before it. The court held there were ample grounds for finding that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1255a(b) had been met, given that petitioner filed an eminent domain action to acquire strips of land running across two borders of a parcel; the property owner bought the parcel and proceeded to develop the tract minus the portion subject to condemnation only upon petitioner's repeated assurances that its eminent domain action would be prosecuted to final judgment; and petitioner then decided to abandon its eminent domain action and require that the strips be dedicated to petitioner as a condition of approval of the property owner's development plans.


OUTCOME: The court discharged petitioner city's alternative writ of mandate and denied the petition for a peremptory writ because respondent trial court had ample grounds for finding that a property owner, a real party in interest, had detrimentally relied upon the condemnation proceeding and could not be restored to its pre-proceeding position. The court ordered that the property owner be allowed to recover costs.

Bloom v. Municipal Court for Inglewood Judicial Dist.


L.A. No. 30258


Supreme Court of California


16 Cal. 3d 71; 545 P.2d 229; 127 Cal. Rptr. 317; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 209



February 6, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C34527, Ernest J. Zack, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff accused appealed from a judgment of the Los Angeles Superior Court (California) that denied his petition for a writ of prohibition to restrain defendant municipal court from continuing from proceeding on a complaint charging him in the statutory language with Cal. Penal Code § 311.2(a). Plaintiff contended that this statute was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff accused petitioned for a writ of prohibition to prevent defendant municipal court from proceeding on the complaint against plaintiff's alleged violations of an obscenity statute, Cal. Penal Code § 311.2(a). The court affirmed judgment that denied the writ of prohibition sought by plaintiff. The court held that the court of appeal had, based in part on Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1110, appellate jurisdiction over the superior court judgment that denied plaintiff's request for a writ of prohibition. The court held that § 311 was, as already judicially construed, sufficiently specific in its definition of what constituted obscene material. The court ruled that plaintiff could not allege error by the fact that the statute required respondent to carry a greater burden of proof than was imposed under the United States Constitution. The court relied upon a controlling precedent which determined that § 311 satisfied the requirement of specificity under the Miller test by prohibiting patently offensive depictions of hardcore sexual conduct. The court rejected plaintiff's argument that § 311 violated the due process clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that denied plaintiff accused's petition for a writ of prohibition to halt defendant municipal court from proceeding with the prosecution that charged plaintiff with violating the obscenity statute. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague because it specifically defined that patently offensive depictions of hardcore sexual conduct were prohibited.

 

443 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Podesto


Crim. No. 18616


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 921; 544 P.2d 1297; 127 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 198



January 27, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied February 26, 1976, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause, having served its purpose, is discharged, and the writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus seeking release pending appeal of his possession of marijuana for sale conviction. Petitioner claimed that all defendants except those in capital cases were entitled to release pending appeal as a matter of right.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of possession of marijuana for sale. He appealed his conviction on the basis of an allegedly illegal search and seizure. During the pendency of his appeal, he moved for an order of release on appeal. In arguing for release, counsel noted that petitioner had made all court appearances and that the conviction would likely be reversed on appeal. The trial court denied the motion and the lower appellate court denied his subsequent application for bail pending appeal. Petitioner then sought a writ of habeas corpus to obtain a release on appeal. The court denied his petition, holding that Cal. Const. art. I, § 12, which ensured to defendants in non-capital cases the availability of release on bail as a matter of right, only applied to preconviction bail. Further, the court held that Cal. Penal Code § 1272 did not violate the equal protection clauses of the federal or state constitutions. Finally, the court held that because the trial court did not give a statement as to its reasons for denying bail, it could not determine whether that court abused its discretion. Accordingly, petitioner was permitted to file a new application with the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's petition for writ of habeas corpus because he was not entitled to release pending appeal as a matter of right; however, because the trial court failed to explain why it denied petitioner's application, petitioner was afforded the right to file a new application for bail on appeal with the trial court.

In re Marriage of Brown


L.A. No. 30463


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 838; 544 P.2d 561; 126 Cal. Rptr. 633; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 192; 94 A.L.R.3d 164



January 16, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. FLW-4780, Henry Milton Busch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review from an interlocutory judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which declared that respondent husband's pension rights were not community property and were not subject to division by the court.


OVERVIEW: Respondent's employer maintained a noncontributory pension plan in which the rights of the employees depended upon their accumulation of points. The parties sought a dissolution of a marriage and the trial court, relying on the French rule, held that respondent's non-vested pension rights were not property, but a mere expectancy, and thus not a community asset subject to division. On appeal, the court overruled the French rule concluding that it erred in characterizing non-vested pension rights as expectancies and in denying the trial courts the authority to divide such rights as community property. The court concluded that pension rights, whether or not vested, represented a property interest. Therefore, to the extent that such rights derived from employment during coverture, they comprised a community asset subject to division in a dissolution proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and the cause was remanded for further proceedings.

Jasperson v. Jessica's Nail Clinic


No. B035656


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


216 Cal. App. 3d 1099; 265 Cal. Rptr. 301; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1315



December 20, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC109666, Lawrence C. Waddington, Judge.



DISPOSITION: No legal challenge to the ordinance having been supported, the judgment is reversed. Appellant to have his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which granted judgment to respondent in appellant's suit alleging respondent violated a city ordinance when it allegedly refused appellant service because he carried the AIDS virus.


OVERVIEW: Appellant brought suit against respondent, a nail clinic, after respondent allegedly refused him service because he had AIDS. Appellant argued that respondent violated a city ordinance prohibiting discrimination of persons with AIDS by any business establishments. The trial court entered judgment for respondent. On appeal, the court held that the ordinance involved did not adopt a classification that treated similarly situated groups in an unequal manner. Thus, the court held that the ordinance was not vulnerable to an equal protection challenge because it bore a rational relationship to a legitimate legislative purpose. The court declared that the ordinance addressed a real and pressing concern regarding the discrimination of those with the AIDS virus. Moreover, the court held that the ordinance served a public health function by encouraging those with AIDS to disclose their status to providers of personal services, and this latter group could then take precautions against transmission.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court that granted judgement to respondent in appellant's suit alleging violation of a city ordinance when respondent allegedly refused him service because he had AIDS. The court held that the primary purpose of the ordinance was to protect people with AIDS from discrimination by providers of personal services.

Tapp v. Superior Court


No. G008325


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


216 Cal. App. 3d 1030; 265 Cal. Rptr. 267; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1370



December 19, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 11, 1990, and petitioner's application for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 4, 1990. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is dissolved and the petition for peremptory writ is denied. Our previously issued temporary stay order is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged the order of the trial court (California), which allowed the prosecution of petitioner for rape, forced oral copulation, burglary, and assault when the trial court had twice previously dismissed the same charges.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged was rape, forced oral copulation, burglary, and assault. Six months later, the prosecution was granted a three month continuance. Petitioner sought extraordinary writ relief, and a peremptory writ was issued directing the trial court to set aside its order and to enter a new order denying the motion. The prosecutor was then unable to proceed as a witness was unavailable. The trial court dismissed the case. A new complaint alleging nearly identical offenses was filed the same day. A preliminary examination was held, and petitioner filed a motion to suppress evidence. The prosecutor argued that res judicata and collateral estoppel barred relitigation of the motion, which had been previously denied. The information was set aside because the trial court determined that the failure to hear the motion denied petitioner a substantial right. A third action was filed, and petitioner sought extraordinary writ relief, contending that the filing was barred because the charges had been twice dismissed pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1387. The court denied the petition because the statute was not vague and ambiguous and one prior dismissal was due to excusable neglect.


OUTCOME: Petitioner's application for peremptory writ was denied because the prosecution of petitioner for felony charges that had twice been dismissed previously was not based on a vague or ambiguous statute, and one of the prior dismissals was the result of excusable neglect.

 

447 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


California Fed. Savings & Loan Ass'n v. City of Los Angeles


No. B033596.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four.


227 Cal. App. 3d 284; 265 Cal. Rptr. 86; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1286



December 18, 1989.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted March 15, 1990 (S013951) (See 54 Cal. 3d 1)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 579296, Vernon G. Foster, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which, pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 23182, ordered appellant to refund moneys paid by respondent savings and loan for the appellant's business license tax. Appellant contended that § 22182 was an unconstitutional attempt by the legislature to regulate a municipal affair of a charter city.


OVERVIEW: Respondent savings and loan challenged appellant city's business license tax for violating Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 23182, which imposed a tax on net earnings of financial institutions in lieu of all other state, county, and municipal taxes and licenses. The superior court entered judgment in favor of respondent ordering appellant to refund the taxes paid because in an area of statewide concern, the state legislature could curb the taxing power of a charter city. Appellant sought review contending that its power to tax for revenue purposes could be limited only by an amendment to the constitution or to its charter. The court noted that the home rule doctrine under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5(a) granted chartered cities the authority to manage local affairs while restricting the state's power to interfere with municipal matters. Because the power to impose taxes to raise revenue was a municipal affair pursuant to a direct constitutional grant, the court reversed the judgment holding that a statewide concern analysis was irrelevant to the business license tax imposed by appellant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment that ordered appellant city to refund moneys paid by respondent savings and loan for its business license tax that was based on a state tax imposed in lieu of municipal taxes on financial institutions. The court held that taxing for revenue purposes was a direct constitutional grant to chartered cities and could be limited only by an amendment to the constitution or to appellant's charter.

In re Marriage of Mansell


No. F002872


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


217 Cal. App. 3d 219; 265 Cal. Rptr. 227; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1372



December 18, 1989 


NOTICE:  [**1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for partial publication. The portions of this opinion to be published follow.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 29, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Merced County, No. 55594, Robert M. Falasco, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's denial of the motion to vacate and modify the judgment herein is affirmed. Wife to receive costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States Supreme Court remanded the matter to the Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District, for proceedings not inconsistent with its opinion after the appellate court affirmed the denial of appellant husband's motion to modify a final judgment dividing his military retirement benefits with respondent wife.


OVERVIEW: Several years after appellant husband and respondent wife stipulated to a property settlement giving respondent half of appellant's gross military retirement benefits, including disability benefits, appellant moved the court to modify the court's final decree of dissolution adopting the agreement. In support, appellant argued that modification was justified under the subsequent United States Supreme Court's McCarty decision, which prevented state courts from treating military retirement pay as community property. After the appellate court affirmed the denial of appellant's motion, the matter was reversed by the United States Supreme Court and remanded to the appellate court for further proceedings. On remand, the court affirmed again, holding that the court did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's motion because the McCarty holding never indicated that state courts were divested of subject matter jurisdiction to divide military retirement benefits, that appellant agreed to the division, and that the McCarty holding was not retroactive.


OUTCOME: On remand, the court affirmed again because the court did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's motion because the McCarty holding never indicated that state courts were divested of subject matter jurisdiction to divide military retirement benefits, appellant agreed to the division, and the McCarty holding was not retroactive.

 

449 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Tate v. Saratoga Sav. & Loan Ass'n


No. H004532


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


216 Cal. App. 3d 843; 265 Cal. Rptr. 440; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1282



December 15, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 15, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Nos. 620871 and 619310, Leslie C. Nichols, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, and Tate and Lazares are to recover attorney fees and costs on appeal in an amount to be determined by the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant savings and loan challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which confirmed arbitration awards in favor of appellees joint venturers and awarded them attorney fees and costs. Appellant argued that the arbitration proceedings were so unfair the resulting award should have been vacated.


OVERVIEW: In a dispute over a joint venture agreement, appellant savings and loan sued appellees joint venturers for nonpayment of a promissory note and appellees petitioned the trial court to compel arbitration of the dispute. The trial court compelled arbitration of the matter and subsequently affirmed the arbitration award in appellees' favor. On appeal, the court considered three issues involving interpretation of the agreement. First, was the arbitration provision unconscionable and therefore unenforceable; second, were the arbitrators empowered to award punitive damages; and third, were awards of attorney fees and costs authorized? The court held that unconscionability of an arbitration provision or another contractual clause must be measured at the time the agreement was made and there was no inherent unconscionability in the parties' arbitration agreement. The court further held that the arbitrators' award of punitive damages was not, as a matter of law, in excess of the arbitrators' powers. The court also found that the arbitration agreement allowed an award of attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment on the basis that the parties' arbitration agreement was not unconscionable and allowed for an award of punitive damages and attorney fees.

Kennedy Wholesale, Inc. v. Board of Equalization


No. C006406.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


227 Cal. App. 3d 228; 265 Cal. Rptr. 195; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1276



December 14, 1989.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted March 1, 1990 (S013840) [See 53 Cal. 3d 245] 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 506474, Anthony DeCristoforo, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tobacco distributor challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which entered judgment in favor of defendant Board of Equalization thereby denying his request for a refund of taxes paid pursuant to Proposition 99, the Tobacco Initiative.


OVERVIEW: Proposition 99, the Tobacco Tax and Health Protection Act of 1988 (Act) was a statutory initiative measure that imposed additional taxes on distributor of tobacco product. The Act added Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 30121- 30130 and also added Cal. Const., § 12, art. XII B. to the California Constitution. After having to pay an increased tax pursuant to the Act, plaintiff tobacco distributor filed a claim for refund with defendant Board of Equalization, alleging that the initiative was unconstitutional. After defendant denied plaintiff's request, plaintiff filed an action pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code 30434, in the trial court for a refund of taxes paid, under protest. The trial court denied defendant's request. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision. The court held that the Act did not violate proposition 13, the single subject rule, applicable to initiatives. The court further found that the Act did not violate the equal protection clause of the United States or the California Constitutions. Finally, the court found that the Act did not infringe on the legislature's constitutional appropriations power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision denying plaintiff tobacco distributor's request for taxes paid under protest. The court held that the tax initiative that imposed the additional tax on distributors of tobacco products did not violated the United States or the California Constitutions. The court further found that the tax initiative did not violate proposition 13, the single subject rule, applicable to initiatives.

People v. Superior Court (Marks)


No. B039818.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven.


234 Cal. App. 3d 482; 264 Cal. Rptr. 910; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1277



December 14, 1989 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified December 19, 1989; Review Granted April 4, 1990 (S013832)


PRIOR HISTORY: This Opinion has been Reprinted as Modified for the Original Opinion of December 14, 1989, previously Reported at 227 Cal. App. 3d 200.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought a writ mandating respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to vacate its order precluding the prosecution of criminal defendant, real party in interest, for first degree murder with special circumstances and to order respondent to strike criminal defendant's pleas of former acquittal and once in jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant, real party in interest, was charged with crimes, including murder with two special circumstances. Respondent trial court ordered a special hearing to determine his competency to stand trial, but the hearing was not completed. Respondent resumed criminal proceedings, and the jury found criminal defendant guilty of murder and conspiracy to commit murder with special circumstances and returned a verdict of death. The supreme court reversed the judgment, and, on remand, respondent conducted a competency hearing and found criminal defendant mentally competent to stand trial. Criminal proceedings were resumed. Criminal defendant sought to enter pleas of former acquittal once in jeopardy, and respondent precluded his prosecution for first degree murder on the ground that his double jeopardy protections would be violated. Petitioner state sought a writ of mandate to force respondent to vacate its judgment. The court granted the writ and order the judgment vacated finding that respondent's decision was made without jurisdiction due to the failure to complete the competency hearing; thus, the judgment was void and jeopardy did not attach.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner state's request for a writ of mandate and ordered respondent trial court to set aside its order precluding prosecution of criminal defnendat, real party in interest, and to strike pleas of double jeopardy. Respondent was without jurisdiction to try the case; thus, the judgment was void for the court's failure to pursue a hearing where criminal defendant's competency to stand trial was in question.

Leonardini v. Shell Oil Co.


No. C000619


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


216 Cal. App. 3d 547; 264 Cal. Rptr. 883; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1270



December 12, 1989 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III and IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 29, 1990. Lucas, C. J., Panelli, J., and Eagleson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 310945, Lloyd Allan Phillips, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant oil company appealed from a judgment in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which awarded plaintiff safety activist damages in a malicious prosecution action. The lawsuit was based on defendant's prior action against plaintiff to enjoin the publication of a safety report.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff safety activist prepared and distributed a pamphlet on public hearings and issues regarding the use of polybutylene pipe for drinking water. Defendant oil company was the manufacturer of the resin found in the pipes. Defendant sought to enjoin trade libel and obtain declaratory relief against plaintiff and the laboratory involved in the testing. After defendant settled with the laboratory, its motion to dismiss plaintiff was granted. Plaintiff filed a lawsuit for malicious prosecution against defendant and was awarded damages in the trial court. The court affirmed the judgment and stated that injunctive relief against further dissemination of plaintiff's report constituted a prior restraint of publication and its issuance was barred by U.S. Const. amend. 1, and that statements made in the context of public debate on a matter of public health could not be enjoined. The action for declaratory relief lacked probable cause because defendant sought a scientific declaration concerning its product rather than a declaration of a legal right. The court found that no reasonable attorney would have believed that there was a legal basis for defendant's actions against plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of plaintiff safety activist because defendant oil company did not have probable cause to seek injunctive relief, as further dissemination of plaintiff's safety report constituted prior restrain of a publication, barred by the constitutional right to free speech. Defendant's action for declaratory relief also lacked probable cause and was not legally justifiable.

Oildale Mut. Water Co. v. N. of the River Mun. Water Dist.


No. F010888


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


215 Cal. App. 3d 1628; 264 Cal. Rptr. 544; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1207



November 28, 1989 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part I.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 183464, William A. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings on the California Constitution article XIII B issue. The parties shall bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant water company sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Kern County (California), which found that depreciation charges made by respondent water district in providing water to appellant under a contract was appropriate, and which held that appellant did not carry its burden of proving that respondent violated Cal. Const. art. XIII B.


OVERVIEW: Appellant water company sued respondent water district, a government entity, claiming that respondent overcharged for water provided under a service agreement. Appellant also sought a declaration that the charges were unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. XIII B, which precluded government entities from expending the proceeds of taxes in excess of an appropriations limit. The trial court found that charges made by respondent for costs relating to some facilities were inappropriate, but that the charge of depreciation to the operation and maintenance account on those facilities was appropriate. The trial court held that appellant did not carry its burden of proving that respondent violated the article. The court affirmed without comment the trial court's ruling that depreciation was properly included. The court held that policy and fairness dictated that respondent carry the burden of proving compliance with the article in order to insure accurate record keeping on the part of respondent. The court reversed the issue of the constitutional violation, because the trial court improperly allocated the burden of proof, and remanded for further proceedings on the issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed without comment the trial court's ruling that depreciation charges made by respondent water district to appellant water company was appropriate. The court reversed the trial court's judgment on the constitutional issue because the trial court incorrectly allocated the burden of proof to appellant.

City of Oceanside v. McKenna


No. D008264


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


215 Cal. App. 3d 1420; 264 Cal. Rptr. 275; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1201



November 22, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 14, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N36950, Jeffrey T. Miller, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant condominium owner challenged a summary judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) against him in the suit for injunctive and declaratory relief brought by respondents, city and community development commission, to enforce the restrictions upon appellant's renting or leasing his condominium. Appellant contended that the restrictions were being enforced in an unfair and discriminatory manner.


OVERVIEW: Appellant condominium owner challenged the trial court grant of summary judgment for respondents, city and community development commission, in their suit for injunctive and declaratory relief to enforce restrictions prohibiting appellant from renting his unit, which was part of a publicly subsidized condominium project. The unit deed authorized respondents to enforce the restrictions. The court affirmed the judgment for respondents holding that appellant did not present competent evidence showing the restrictions were unreasonable or enforced in an unfair and discriminatory manner. The court held that the restrictions were reasonable and enforceable under Cal. Civ. Code § 1354. The court determined that because the restrictions on use and alienation were justified by legitimate purposes of public policy to provide affordable housing, and were authorized by Cal. Health & Safety Code § 33437(a), they were not void under Cal. Civ. Code § 711. The court concluded that although there were no written standards for the restrictions' enforcement, they were enforced in a uniform and objective manner and not in any discriminatory basis in violation of Cal. Code Regs. tit. 10, § 2792.25(a).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed summary judgment for respondent city and community development commission holding that the restrictions against appellant condominium owner renting his unit were reasonable as they served a legitimate purpose to provide affordable housing in a publicly subsidized condominium project, and that appellant failed to present competent evidence showing the restrictions were enforced in an unfair and discriminatory manner.

 

455 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



McAdory v. Rogers


No. B028475


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


215 Cal. App. 3d 1273; 264 Cal. Rptr. 71; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1178



November 21, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C397559, Robert L. Roberson, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The matter is remanded to the trial court which is directed to amend the judgment to award Ms. McAdory $ 250,000 in noneconomic damages. Ms. McAdory shall recover her costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that, in plaintiff's medical malpractice suit against defendant doctor, reduced plaintiff's award of noneconomic damages to conform to the cap provided for in Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2. Plaintiff contended that the trial court erred in applying § 3333.2 after plaintiff's comparative fault was factored into the damage equation.


OVERVIEW: In her medical malpractice action against defendant doctor, plaintiff patient appealed a judgment that reduced plaintiff's award of noneconomic damages to conform to the cap on such damages as provided for in Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2. The cap was applied after the award, which exceeded the $ 250,000 cap, and was reduced by plaintiff's comparative fault. The court reversed the judgment. The court noted that it was constrained to adhere to the jury's determination that plaintiff's noneconomic damages were in an amount that exceeded the statutory cap. The court further observed, in apparent agreement with plaintiff, that a medical malpractice patient could, as did plaintiff, suffer more than $ 250,000 in noneconomic damages but was precluded from recovering any more than that amount. Thus, the court ruled, it was error to apply the cap after plaintiff's damages had been reduced by her comparative fault; the cap should have been applied before comparative fault was factored into the damages equation. The court reasoned that to permit such a reduction in plaintiff's recovery amounted to a punishment for her comparative fault that was not the policy behind comparative fault analysis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment that reduced plaintiff patient's noneconomic damages award to conform to the statutory cap for such damages in plaintiff's malpractice action against defendant doctor. The cap was no more than a limitation on plaintiff's recovery, and it should have been applied to plaintiff's award, which exceeded the cap, before the award was reduced by comparative fault. Plaintiff was entitled to the maximum award under the cap.

Wilkinson v. Times Mirror Corp.


No. A042625


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


215 Cal. App. 3d 1034; 264 Cal. Rptr. 194; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1168; 4 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1579



November 17, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 15, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 636361-3, Michael E. Ballachey, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a publishing company and its subsidiary, by a writ of supersedeas, appealed an order from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), issuing a preliminary junction that restrained defendants from conditioning the employment of plaintiff job applicants, and of any other member of the named class seeking employment with defendant subsidiary, upon submission of mandatory urinalysis testing for drugs and alcohol.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff job applicants obtained a preliminary injunction restraining defendants, a publishing company and its subsidiary, from conditioning employment of certain categories of job applicants upon submission to mandatory urinalysis testing for drugs and alcohol. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that Cal. Const. art. I, § 1 recognized a right to privacy and was intended to protect that right from both governmental and private intrusion. However, in the context of private action, the test was whether the conduct complained of so substantially burdened plaintiff's right of privacy that it was constitutionally unreasonable. Because employers were authorized to condition employment on results of a medical examination under Cal. Code Regs. tit. 2, § 7294.0(d) and Cal. Gov't. Code §§ 12920, 12921, 12926, and 12994, and a urinalysis was a routine part of any medical examination, job applicants should reasonably anticipate such testing. The testing was only slightly more intrusive than other aspects of the job-seeking process; the subject applicants were given advanced notice of the testing; and the procedures used minimized the intrusiveness of the drug-screening program.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order granting the preliminary injunction was reversed. While defendant subsidiary's preemployment drug and alcohol testing program did intrude upon a recognized expectation of privacy, it did not so substantially burden the right to privacy that it was constitutionally impermissible; nor was the program otherwise unlawful. Plaintiff job applicants had no likelihood of prevailing on the merits.

Selinger v. City Council


No. E005698


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


216 Cal. App. 3d 259; 264 Cal. Rptr. 499; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1251



November 13, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 1, 1989, and respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 25, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. SCV 239818, A. Rex Victor, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city council sought review of an order by the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which granted respondent developer's petition for a writ of mandate that required appellant to acknowledge that respondent's tentative tract map was deemed approved because appellant failed to act on the application within one year after the application was complete.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city council challenged an order granting respondent developer's petition for a writ of mandate. The writ of mandate required appellant to acknowledge that respondent's tentative tract map was deemed approved because appellant failed to act on the application within one year after the application was complete. The court reversed the order granting the writ of mandate because the moratorium, enacted after an application was complete, did not toll the time period for action on the application under Cal. Gov't Code § 65956. Additionally, the Permit Streamlining Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 65920 et seq., was unconstitutional insofar as it led to approval of applications for development without notice and a hearing to affected landowners. Cal. Gov't Code § 65858, allowing appellant to impose a development moratorium, was not reconciled with § 65956, which required appellant to act on an application within one year. An actual controversy existed between respondent and appellant in the lawsuit initiated by respondent, so the matter was ripe for judicial determination. Approval of tentative subdivision maps was unconstitutional unless adequate notice and a hearing were provided.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting respondent developer's petition for a writ of mandate requiring appellant city council to acknowledge that respondent's tract map was approved. The moratorium, enacted after respondent's application was complete, did not toll the time period for action on the application under statute, and the Permit Streamlining Act was unconstitutional as not providing for notice and a hearing to affected landowners.

In re Marriage of Joseph


No. A040057.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO

NOTICE:  [**1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which permitted appellee wife to seek attorney's fees in connection with a postjudgment child custody dispute between the parties. Appellee filed a cross-appeal from the trial court's denial of attorney's fees.

OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment permitting appellee wife to seek attorney's fees in connection with a postjudgment child custody dispute between the parties and denying appellee's claim for attorney's fees because appellee did not demonstrate a need for them. The court remanded the cause to the trial court to determine whether appellee was entitled to any appellate attorney's fees.

Minelian v. Manzella


No. B043724


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


215 Cal. App. 3d 457; 263 Cal. Rptr. 597; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1107



November 8, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 6, 1989, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 15, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court of Santa Monica Judicial District, No. 87C01814, Joseph W. Chandler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the municipal court is affirmed. Respondent to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlord sought review of an adverse judgment of the Municipal Court of Santa Monica Judicial District (California) in appellant's unlawful detainer lawsuit against respondent tenant.


OVERVIEW: Respondent tenant filed a petition with the rent control board to determine the base rent for her unit, after appellant landlord increased respondent's rent. The hearing examiner concluded that appellant was charging an excessive amount of rent. Respondent stopped paying rent, using the excess rent previously paid to cover future rent payments. Appellant landlord prepared a three-day notice to pay rent or quit. Appellant then filed an unlawful detainer complaint. The trial court ruled in favor of respondent. Appellant sought review, contending that Cal. Civ. Code § 1947.7 applied and that excess rent payments by respondent could be offset against rent owing only to the extent that the payments were made within three years prior to the three-day notice. The court affirmed the trial court judgment, holding that § 1947.7 was unavailable to appellant in light of the substantial evidence that appellant failed to substantially comply with the Santa Monica Rent Control Charter Amendment. The court also held that there was no statute of limitations to the right to an offset for unlawful excess rent already paid when raised as an affirmative defense to an unlawful detainer action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the municipal court's judgment that respondent tenant owed no rent, holding that the equitable nature of a tenant's right to an offset of rent meant that no statute of limitations applied when payment of excess rent was raised by respondent as an affirmative defense to appellant landlord's unlawful detainer action.

People v. Powers

Trans. May 16, 1991 to Ct. App., 1st Dist., Div. 1, with directions to vacate its decision and reconsider in light of City of West Hollywood V. Beverly Towers (1991) 52 Cal. 3d 1184. (See 2 Cal. App. 4th 330)

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed a decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) in so far as it limited the relief granted to the imposition of civil penalties and an injunction against future unfair business practice in an action against defendants, property owner and associated corporations, for violating San Francisco City Subdivision Code § 1385. The court affirmed the judgment in all other respects and remanded the case to the trial court.

 

461 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Manuel P.


No. D006455


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


215 Cal. App. 3d 48; 263 Cal. Rptr. 447; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1086



October 31, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. J145417, Judith McConnell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a Mexican national juvenile, challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which authorized a California juvenile court to transfer him to Mexican juvenile officials in Mexico, under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 738.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Mexican national juvenile was caught stealing in San Diego and made a ward of the juvenile court. Pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 738, appellant was returned to Mexican juvenile authorities. Appellant challenged the validity of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 738, contending that it was unconstitutional because it violated the U.S. Const. art. VI, Supremacy Clause and the Compact Clause, U.S. Const. art. I § 10. On appeal, the court affirmed the order returning appellant to juvenile authorities in Mexico, holding that § 738 did not require nor invite any form of agreement with any foreign power. The court reasoned that § 738 did nothing more than permit California to order the removal from its borders of alien minors who had conducted themselves so as to come within the jurisdiction of the juvenile court. The court concluded that there could be no doubt but that California has such power and that federal preemption did not apply. The court noted that appellant neither availed himself of a formal deportation process nor did he request the court to stay the enforcement of its order to return him to Mexico pending review of his appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the juvenile court, holding that the federal preemption did not apply to bar California from ordering nonresident juveniles returned to their country of origin, especially when the juvenile is first presented to the Immigration and Naturalization Service for processing through the federal deportation system.

 

462 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Feiock


No. G003512


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


215 Cal. App. 3d 141; 263 Cal. Rptr. 437; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1087



October 31, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 25, 1990. Mosk, J., and Kaufman, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the United States Supreme Court, petitioner sought reconsideration of his writ of habeas corpus application seeking relief from a judgment of contempt for failure to pay child support. Petitioner alleged that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1209.3, which required the trial court to presume prima facie evidence of contempt after proof of non-compliance with a court order, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was found in contempt for failure to pay child support and sentenced to a period of probation. Petitioner filed an application for habeas corpus relief, alleging that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1209.3, was unconstitutional because it presumed contempt based upon proof of non-compliance with a support order without considering petitioner's ability to pay. The court initially granted the application, and the prosecution filed an application for writ of certiorari with the United States Supreme Court, which was granted. The United States Supreme Court remanded the case back to the court. On remand, the court denied the application for habeas corpus. The court held that the prosecution was not required to show ability to pay as an element of the contempt, instead, inability to pay was a defense that petitioner had the burden of pleading and proving. The court held that the act of raising the defense did not put too harsh a burden on petitioner, and once the defense was raised, the burden would shift to the prosecution to rebut petitioner's claims and prove his ability to pay.


OUTCOME: After remand, the court denied petitioner's habeas corpus application because the presumptions raised by the civil procedure code were not unconstitutional, petitioner could raise his inability to pay as a defense to the contempt presumption, petitioner had the burden of pleading and proving his inability to pay, and, if petitioner successfully raised the defense, the burden would shift to the state to disprove petitioner's claim.

Topanga Ass'n for a Scenic Cmty. v. County of L.A.


No. B042669


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


214 Cal. App. 3d 1348; 263 Cal. Rptr. 214; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1049



October 19, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 1, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-703875, Kurt J. Lewin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, community and homeowner's associations, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellants' petition for writ of mandate seeking to require respondent Los Angeles County to set aside their approval of a residential development project conducted by respondent and real party in interest developer.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, community and homeowner's associations, challenged planning commission's decision to approve respondent and real party in interest developer's project. The county board of supervisors concurred in the approval. Appellants' petition for a writ of mandate pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5 seeking to have the project approvals set aside was denied. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court refused to reweigh the evidence, and found that there was sufficient evidence to support approval of the project. The presence of archaeological resources in the project area did not require a finding that the project would cause substantial environmental damage as prohibited by the California Environmental Quality Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq., and the Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 66410 et seq., because the archaeological resources were not unique. Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21083.2.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent superior court's denial of appellants', community and homeowner's associations, petition for writ of mandate, finding that there was sufficient evidence to support approval of respondent and real party in interest developer's project and that the presence of archaeological resources did not require a finding of substantial environmental damage where the archaeological resources were not unique.

 

464 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Niebauer


No. D009461


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


214 Cal. App. 3d 1278; 263 Cal. Rptr. 287; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1055



October 18, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the El Cajon Judicial District of San Diego County, No. KJ90354, Larrie R. Brainard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the municipal court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Municipal Court for the El Cajon Judicial District of San Diego County (California), which convicted defendant of driving with dark tinting material on the side windows of his truck in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 26708(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of driving with dark tinting material on the side windows of his truck under Cal. Veh. Code § 26708(a). A majority of the appellate department of the Superior Court affirmed the trial court's decision and certified the matter to the court to obtain uniformity of decision for what it perceived to be a direct conflict between its decision and a published decision in another county. The court requested briefs from the parties on the constitutionality of § 26708(a), on whether the rationale of the conflicting decision should be followed, and on whether there was sufficient evidence in the record to support defendant's conviction. The court affirmed the trial court's decision, holding that defendant had not shown any burden on interstate commerce and that § 26708(a) was not unconstitutionally applied to him. The court further held that there was sufficient evidence in the record from which any reasonable fact finder could have found that defendant was driving with tinting material applied to the windows of his truck in violation of § 26708(a).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision that convicted defendant of driving with dark tinting material on the side windows of his truck because defendant had not shown any burden on interstate commerce and the statute under which he was charged was not unconstitutional. The court held that there was sufficient evidence in the record to support defendant's conviction.

 

465 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Am. Acad. of Pediatrics v. Van De Kamp


No. A040911


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


214 Cal. App. 3d 831; 263 Cal. Rptr. 46; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1028



October 12, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 884574, Morton R. Colvin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state attorney general appealed the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) granting a preliminary injunction in favor of plaintiff health care providers preventing the implementation of Assemb. B. 2274, 1987-1988 Reg. Sess. (Cal. 1987), requiring unemancipated minors to obtain the consent of a parent, a guardian, or the juvenile court, before undergoing an abortion.


OVERVIEW: On appeal by defendant state attorney general, the court found that the trial court had not abused its discretion in granting a preliminary injunction, sought by plaintiff health care providers, preventing the implementation of Assemb. B. 2274, 1987-1988 Reg. Sess. (Cal. 1987), which required unemancipated minors seeking an abortion to obtain parental or court consent. It found that the trial court's determination that greater harm would result from implementing the law than from not implementing it was supported by substantial evidence and thus would not be overturned. Noting that the choice to have an abortion was protected under the privacy right of Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, and could not be intruded upon absent a compelling state interest, the court held that the privacy right did not distinguish between children and adults. It concluded that there was sufficient evidence for the trial court to find that plaintiffs could prevail at trial because plaintiffs had demonstrated that they could prove that the state interests cited by defendant, protecting minors from harm and preserving the parent/child relationship, were protected as well or better under existing law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of a preliminary injunction to plaintiff health care providers against defendant state attorney general, preventing the implementation of a law requiring minors seeking abortions to obtain parental or court consent. It held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in finding that harm would result from not granting the injunction and in finding that plaintiffs had shown a likelihood of prevailing at trial.

 

466 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Michael D.


No. G007908


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


214 Cal. App. 3d 1610; 264 Cal. Rptr. 476; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1318



October 6, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-134625, Hugh Michael Brenner, Judge, and Dennis J. Keough, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment as to the escape charge is reversed, and the matter is remanded for redisposition. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which convicted appellant of escape, based on his departure from his parents' home, while on home confinement.


OVERVIEW: The state alleged that appellant juvenile came under the provisions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, because he escaped from his parents' home, while on home confinement in violation of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 871. The trial court convicted appellant of escape. Appellant sought review of his conviction, as well as certain conditions of his probation. On review, the court reversed the escape conviction. The court held that appellant's departure from his parents' home did not constitute an escape within the meaning of § 871. The court explained that, under § 871, a person must be not only under the custody of a probation officer or a peace officer, he must also be in a county juvenile hall, or committed to a county juvenile home, ranch, camp or forestry camp, and must escape or attempt to escape from that place. The court rejected appellant's contention that certain conditions of his probation were unconstitutional, vague, and overbroad. The court held that the conditions of appellant's probation were reasonable under the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant's conviction for escape and remanded the case for redisposition. The court held that appellant's departure from his parents' home did not constitute an "escape," as defined by the statute. The court affirmed the judgment in all other respects.
