Fisher v. San Pedro Peninsula Hospital


No. B033017


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


214 Cal. App. 3d 590; 262 Cal. Rptr. 842; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1002; 54 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 584; 53 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P39,888



October 2, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 18, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SOC86235, James M. Sutton, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is reversed and remanded with directions to enter a new and different order dismissing the action against Dr. Brow, but granting plaintiffs leave to amend the first cause of action for sex discrimination and granting Dr. Fisher leave to amend his third cause of action for retaliation, against SPPH, based on SPPH's cancellation of Dr. Fisher's office lease. Appellants to recover costs on appeal from defendants and respondents San Pedro Peninsula Hospital and Barry Tischler. Defendant and respondent, Frank Brow, is to recover costs on appeal from the appellants.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed their complaint against appellees, doctors and hospital, for sexual discrimination under the California Fair Employment and Housing Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 12900 et seq., retaliation in employment, third party interference with business relations, and intentional infliction of emotional distress.


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife suffered sexual harassment from her employer, appellee gynecologist, and reported it to appellee hospital, who did nothing. She witnessed sexual harassment by appellee doctor to other employees, then quit. Appellant husband was a doctor from whom appellee pediatrician would not take referrals and whose lease was terminated by appellee hospital. Appellants filed an action against appellees doctors and hospital for sexual discrimination under the California Fair Employment and Housing Act (FEHA), Cal. Gov't Code § 12900 et seq., retaliation in employment, third party interference with business relations, and intentional infliction of emotional distress. The trial court dismissed the action. Appellants sought review and the court reversed and held that appellant wife should have been able to amend her complaint to allege environmental sexual harassment, that there was no retaliation cause of action against appellee pediatrician under FEHA, that appellee hospital was not liable for appellee doctors' behavior, that appellants did not allege that appellee doctors interfered with their business relations, and that there was no support for punitive damages.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal and held that appellants should have been permitted to amend the complaint to allege environmental sexual harassment, that appellee hospital was not liable for appellee doctors' behavior, but might have been liable for the lease termination, that there was no retaliation cause of action against appellee pediatrician, and that there was no interference with business relations nor support for punitive damages.

 

468 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Mautner v. Peralta


No. A043767


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


215 Cal. App. 3d 796; 263 Cal. Rptr. 535; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 1098



September 29, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco, No. 842026, Maxine M. Chesney and Lucy Kelly McCabe, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Wells Fargo to pay costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff veterans home sought review of the order of the Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco (California), which dismissed its claim against defendant bank in an action to recover funds improperly transferred to decedent's beneficiaries contrary to plaintiff's statutory lien under Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code §§ 1035.05 and 1035.1.


OVERVIEW: Decedent veteran died testate leaving his entire estate to defendants, his friends. Prior to his death, he took up residency at plaintiff veterans home. Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code §§ 1035.05 and 1035.1 operated to void decedent's will to the extent that it left property to nonrelatives, and substituted plaintiff as statutory heir. Defendant financial institution released funds of the decedent to defendant friends after being notified of plaintiff's lien and plaintiff sought to recover the money. The trial court dismissed the claim and found that the bank was absolutely immune from liability for the transfer because it was presented with an affidavit pursuant to Cal. Prob. Code § 630 governing administration of small estates. The court reversed and held the reasonable and practical construction of Cal. Prob. Code § 630 rendered its grant of immunity inapplicable where the holder has actual notice of a statutory heir's superior claim prior to receiving an affidavit pursuant to Cal. Prob. Code § 630 from a legatee. In the absence of immunity, the allegations of the complaint survived the demurrer.


OUTCOME: The judgment of dismissal of plaintiff veterans home claim was reversed because the bank did not have immunity from liability when it transferred funds in a decedent veteran's account to a beneficiary upon presentation of an affidavit pursuant to Cal. Prob. Code § 630 because it had actual notice of the superior, statutory claim of the veterans home pursuant to Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code §§ 1035.05 and 1035.1.

In re Marriage of Watt


No. A041437


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


214 Cal. App. 3d 340; 262 Cal. Rptr. 783; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 972



September 27, 1989 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part IV.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 92706, John A. DeRonde, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in part and reversed in part, with directions to conduct further proceedings consistent with this opinion. David to pay costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife challenged an order of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which denied her request for spousal support and reimbursement to the community of funds spent on appellant husband's education.


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife argued that the trial court abused its discretion in denying spousal support to her and erred in holding that the community made no reimbursable contributions to appellant husband's education. The court held that Cal. Civ. Code § 4801(a)(2) did not limit one spouse's contribution to the other's education to direct educational expenses, but required the trial court to also consider contributions towards ordinary living expenses. The court reversed and remanded because the trial court failed to take into account appellant husband's absence from the work force in determining the parties' pre-dissolution standard of living. The court affirmed the holding that Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.3 required reimbursement to the community only for expenses related to appellant husband's education, and not for appellant wife's contribution to the parties' living expenses.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the order denying appellant wife spousal support because the trial court failed to consider her contributions toward appellant husband's ordinary living expenses and his absence from the work force in determining the parties' pre-dissolution standard of living.

 

470 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cent. Valley Ch. 7th Step Found. v. Younger


No. A033285


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


214 Cal. App. 3d 145; 262 Cal. Rptr. 496; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 961



September 22, 1989 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976 and 976.1, this opinion is certified for partial publication. The portion to be published follows.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied October 26, 1989, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. 497394-6 and 524298-6, Jacqueline Taber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both plaintiffs and defendants below sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) that addressed the constitutionality of various policies and practices followed by state officials in disseminating arrest records to public employers and licensing entities.


OVERVIEW: This case addressed the constitutionality of policies and practices followed by state officials in disseminating arrest record information to public employers and licensing entities. Plaintiffs brought actions challenging a range of policies implemented by the defendants, various state officials. The trial court entered a judgment granting in part and denying in part plaintiffs' requests for declaratory and injunctive relief. On review, the court affirmed in most respects and concluded that dissemination of arrest records to authorized agencies for employment, licensing and certification purposes significantly affected plaintiffs' right to privacy guaranteed by the California Constitution. It also rejected defendants' contention that state officials were merely a conduit for information and had no duty whatsoever to verify, update, or censor information received before disseminating it. According to the court, because dissemination of arrest record information impinges on the fundamental privacy rights of Californians, defendants could disseminate such information only when it served a compelling state interest, such as for evaluating persons seeking employment as peace officers.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision granting in many respects plaintiffs' requests for declaratory and injunctive relief pertaining to defendant state officials' dissemination of arrest records was substantially affirmed, with certain modifications. The court concluded that only dissemination that would serve a compelling state interest was allowable.

 

471 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Sands v. Morongo Unified School Dist.


No. E005349.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two.


225 Cal. App. 3d 1385; 262 Cal. Rptr. 452; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 989



September 19, 1989.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Granted December 7, 1989 (S012721) (See 53 Cal. 3d 863)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. BCV 2279, LeRoy A. Simmons, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a school district, superintendent, and governing board, challenged an injunction granted by the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), prohibiting religious invocations and benedictions at high school graduation ceremonies, in respondents' suit under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a alleging that the practice violated the establishment clause of U.S. Const. amend. I and various provisions of the state constitution.


OVERVIEW: Respondents sued appellants, a school district and its governing body, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a, seeking to restrain appellants from including a religious invocation and benediction in high school graduation ceremonies on the grounds of violation of the establishment clause of U.S. Const. amend. I and portions of the state constitution. The trial court ruled in favor of respondents, and appellants sought review. The court reversed the judgment. It held that respondents, as residents and taxpayers, had standing to bring the action. It held that the invocations and benedictions did not violate the establishment clause where they had a legitimate secular purpose of solemnizing the occasion, where their religious effect was remote and incidental and not part of any program of calculated indoctrination, and where they did not foster excessive entanglement with religion since they were not funded by appellants and were not a daily part of the educational process. The court further held that they did not violate Cal. Const. art. XVI, § 5, and art. IX, § 8, because they did not have the substantial effect or promoting religion and the involvement of faculty was incidental.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, holding that respondents had standing to sue, but that invocations and benedictions did not violate the establishment clause where they had a legitimate secular purpose, where their primary effect was not to advance religion and their religious effect was incidental, and where they did not represent excessive entanglement with religion because they were not state funded and not part of the educational process.

Turner v. Architectural Design Group


No. H003735.


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


213 Cal. App. 3d 1457; 262 Cal. Rptr. 375; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 948



September 18, 1989 


NOTICE:   [**1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for review is DENIED November 30, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions is directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. H003735, Charles Gordon, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer sought review of a judgment entered by the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that granted respondent architect's motion for an order enforcing settlement under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 664.6. On appeal, appellant contended that the offer and acceptance made before the referee was not made before the court as required by that section.


OVERVIEW: Respondent architect filed suit to foreclose on a mechanic's lien for work it completed on a condominium complex under construction by appellant developer. At a mandatory settlement conference held before a referee, appellant offered to settle the matter for a cash amount and waiver of the lien. Respondent subsequently accepted that offer. After appellant failed to execute payment, respondent obtained a judgment that ordered enforcement of the settlement under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 664.6. On appeal, the court noted that Cal. Civ. Proc. § 664.6 required that the settlement offer had to be made "before the court" in order for enforcement under that section and that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 639 limited the powers of a referee. No provision of that section in turn authorized a referee to conduct settlement proceedings. The court thus concluded that the offer made before the referee did not transpire "before the court" within the meaning of Cal. Civ. Proc. § 664.6. As the statutory predicates for the enforcement of the agreement were not reached, the judgment entered in favor of respondent was reversed, and costs of appeal were awarded to appellant.


OUTCOME: The judgment entered in favor of respondent architect was reversed, and costs of appeal were awarded to appellant developer. The settlement conference before the referee wherein agreement was reached between parties was not a judicially supervised proceeding as required under the applicable statute, and therefore, the trial court erred when it enforced the parties' alleged settlement.

 

473 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



City of Rancho Cucamonga v. Warner Consulting Servs.


No. E004578


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


213 Cal. App. 3d 1338; 262 Cal. Rptr. 349; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 955



September 14, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
A petition for a rehearing was denied October 5, 1989, and respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 21, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. OCV 32988, George G. Grover, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant theater owners challenge the judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) which issued a permanent injunction prohibiting topless dancing at appellant's theater, in an action brought by respondent city to enjoin violations of local ordinances and a public nuisance.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city brought suit against appellant theater owners to enjoin violations of local ordinances and a public nuisance. Following trial, the court found that appellants had violated a local ordinance prohibiting topless dancing in premises licensed to sell alcoholic beverages and issued a permanent injunction. Appellants argued on appeal that the state had not delegated authority to respondent under U.S. Const. amend. XXI to regulate such conduct, that topless dancing was protected conduct under the state constitution, and as an establishment primarily devoted to theatrical entertainment, the theater in question was exempt from the local ordinance banning topless dancing. The court reversed the lower court's judgment and remanded the case for further determination. The state had not delegated to respondent its expanded powers under the U.S. Const. amend. XXI; thus, the respondent's authority to regulate conduct in establishments licensed by the state to sell alcoholic beverages derived solely from its general police power. Accordingly, the local ordinances could not be presumed valid under U.S. Const. amend. XXI.


OUTCOME: The judgment enjoining appellant theater owners from allowing topless dancing at their theater was reversed and the case was remanded for further determinations. The source of respondent city's power to regulate conduct in establishments licensed by the state to sell liquor derived solely from their general police powers rather than from any delegation of any of the state's constitutionally expanded powers.

 

474 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Quiroz


No. E005041.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two.


213 Cal. App. 3d 1001; 261 Cal. Rptr. 852; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 922



August 31, 1989 


NOTICE:   [**1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied November 16, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, Thomas E. Hollenhorst, Judge, No. 26117, and William R. Bailey, Jr., Judge, No. CR 26948.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California) which, after a jury trial, convicted defendant of violating Cal. Penal Code § 69. Defendant alleged that the trial court committed errors in instructing the jury on the § 69 charge.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged a trial court decision that convicted defendant, after a jury trial, of violating Cal. Penal Code § 69. Defendant contended that the trial court committed err in not giving a "unanimity" instruction to the jury as to the § 69 charge in light of the prosecution's failure to elect a specific § 69 offense upon which to rely in proving the charge, erred in not giving a clarification instruction as to the constitutionally allowable application of § 69, and erred in failing to properly instruct the jury regarding requisite intent necessary to convict under § 69. The court reversed. The trial court was required to giving an "unanimity" instruction in conformity with Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 17.01 because defendant was accused of but one offense and the evidence was susceptible of proving the commission of more than one offense. The prosecution failed to make an election regarding the specific offense. The trial court erred by not instructing the jury that the phrase "any threat" in § 69 applied only to threats of unlawful violence. The trial court also erred by failing to properly instruct the jury on the degree of intent necessary for a § 69 conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a trial court decision that convicted defendant, after a jury trial, of interfering with official duties. The trial court committed reversible error by not giving an "unanimity" instruction regarding the interference charge, by not giving a clarifying instruction regarding the constitutional application of the unlawful interference charge, and by not properly instructing the jury on the requisite degree of intent necessary.

 

475 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Hamilton v. Town of Los Gatos


No. H004004


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


213 Cal. App. 3d 1050; 261 Cal. Rptr. 888; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 896



August 31, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 634433, Thomas P. Hansen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we affirm the denial of the writ of mandate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, city council member, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent, town, to give him a copy of a tape recording of a closed session hearing he did not participate in due to a conflict of interest. Appellant based his claim on Cal. Gov't Code § 54956.9 and respondent opposed it based on Cal. Gov't Code § 87100.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, town, held a town meeting, including a closed session to discuss with its attorney a matter in litigation. Appellant, town council member, voluntarily left the council during the closed session because of a conflict of interest. Appellant subsequently asked the town clerk to permit him to listen to the tape of the closed session. She refused and appellant filed a petition for a writ of mandate to require the town to make the tape available to him. The superior court found that because appellant was disqualified from acting or participating in the discussion between the council and its attorney, he had no greater right to the tape than the public at large. The superior court also held that disclosure to appellant would constitute waiver of the attorney-client privilege. The court agreed and affirmed the superior court's judgment denying the writ. The court held that Cal. Gov't Code § 87100 prevented appellant from participating in the closed session and the superior court properly denied access to the tape of that hearing in order to forestall the appearance of possible improprieties.


OUTCOME: The court held that appellant, town council member, was prohibited from obtaining a tape recording of a closed session between the council and its attorney, where the council met to discuss litigation related to a matter in which appellant had a conflict of interest. Therefore, the court concluded that the superior court properly denied appellant access to the tape recording of the closed session, and affirmed the denial of the writ of mandate.

 

476 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Christina A.


Nos. H004140, H004145, H004147


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


213 Cal. App. 3d 1073; 261 Cal. Rptr. 903; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 895



August 31, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 16, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Nos. 88755, 88756 and 88757, Kristine M. McCarthy, Juvenile Court Referee.



DISPOSITION: The orders are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the dispositional and permanency planning orders of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which terminated her parental rights over her three children because Cal. Civ. Code § 232 and Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 361.5(b)(2) were unconstitutionally vague and respondent Santa Clara County did not present sufficient evidence to support the orders.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Santa Clara County removed appellant mother's three children from her custody because allegations of child abuse were substantiated. The trial court found that appellant was not fit for reunification with her children pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232 and Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 361.5(b)(2). The court stated that vagueness occurs when a legislature states its proscriptions in terms so indefinite that the line between innocent and condemned conduct became a matter of guesswork. The court found that appellant failed to examine whether there was a constitutionally protected interest at stake to which her claim of vagueness would apply and that there was no question of appellant's having sufficient notice of the proscribed conduct in § 361.5(b)(2) so as to avoid it. The court also found that there was no improper purpose underlying the statutes because they had a rational relationship to the purpose of ensuring the well being of children whose parents were incapable of caring for them. The court affirmed the orders because it found sufficient evidence of the findings in the testimony of two psychologists' opinion of appellant's mental state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dispositional and permanency planning orders of the trial court that removed custody of appellant mother's children in an action based on the recommendation of respondent Santa Clara County Department. The court held that the governing statute was not vague and there was sufficient evidence to find that appellant was incapable of caring for her children.

 

477 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Melissa G.


No. H004943


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


213 Cal. App. 3d 1082; 261 Cal. Rptr. 894; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 898



August 31, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey County, No. J25630, John M. Phillips, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of paternity is reversed, and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which disregarded blood tests that established a 99.1 percent probability that appellant was the father of the minor child and applied the presumption under Cal. Evid. Code § 621 to declare that mother's husband was the minor child's legal father.


OVERVIEW: Appellant father challenged a decision by the trial court that ordered blood tests to determine the paternity of a minor child and established a 99.1 percent probability that appellant was the father of the minor child. The child was born eight days before her mother separated from her husband. The trial court applied Cal. Evid. Code § 621, disregarded the results of the blood tests, and determined that husband was the child's father. Appellant challenged the parternity order. On appeal the court reversed the order because the trial court's application of the presumption of § 621 was unconstitutional. Section 621 provided that when a wife was cohabiting with her husband, who was not impotent or sterile, the wife's child was presumed to be a child of the marriage. The minor child had no available mother, the man the court determined was her legal father was a stranger to her, and her most important psychological relationship was with a sibling to whom she was presumed biologically unrelated. As a result, the court concluded the statute's classification did not serve the interests it was designed to protect. Accordingly, the court held that application of § 621 was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order disregarding paternity tests and determining that mother's husband was the minor child's legal father. The trial court's application of a statutory presumption of paternity was unconstitutional where the statute's classification did not serve the interests it was designed to protect because application would lead to a custody situation that was contrary to the child's best interests.

 

478 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


George v. International Society for Krishna Consciousness


No. D007153.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


213 Cal. App. 3d 729; 262 Cal. Rptr. 217; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 894



August 30, 1989 


NOTICE:   [**1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As modified September 29, 1989; Appellants' petitions for review DENIED November 30, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976 Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 277565, James A. Jackman, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, religious organization, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that awarded respondents, mother and daughter, compensatory and punitive damages in their action for false imprisonment, intentional infliction of emotional distress, libel, and wrongful death after respondent daughter ran away to live in appellant's commune.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, religious organization, challenged the judgment in favor of respondents, mother and daughter, in respondents' action seeking damages that occurred when respondent daughter ran away to join appellant, and appellant's leaders assisted her in her efforts to remain hidden from her parents. The court reversed the judgment with respect to respondent daughter, but affirmed the award in favor of respondent mother. The court found that respondent daughter was not falsely imprisoned because she was of sufficient age to consent and no force or physical threat of force was used against her, nor was she inflicted with emotional distress because no force or fraud was used against her. The court found that appellant's official position letter was false and defamatory with respect to respondent mother because it implied that she beat her daughter. The court also found that appellant intentionally inflicted emotional distress on respondent mother because its leaders assisted respondent daughter in her efforts to run away, lied about their involvement, and refused to disclose respondent daughter's whereabouts. The court found that the punitive damage award was not excessive.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment insofar as it awarded damages to respondent daughter for false imprisonment, intentional infliction of emotional distress, and libel, finding that respondent daughter was not forcibly detained by appellant religious organization. The court affirmed the judgment with respect to respondent mother, finding that appellant intentionally caused her emotional distress by helping respondent daughter to run away.

 

479 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Sundance Saloon v. City of San Diego


No. D008383


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


213 Cal. App. 3d 807; 261 Cal. Rptr. 841; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 892



August 30, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 598854, James R. Milliken, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the motion for preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant cabaret challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied its request for a preliminary injunction to enjoin the enforcement of appellee city's ordinance, which required appellant to close between the hours of 2 a.m. and 6 a.m.


OVERVIEW: Appellant cabaret filed a complaint pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 to enjoin the enforcement of appellee city's ordinance requiring cabarets, with certain exceptions, to close between the hours of 2 a.m. and 6 a.m. Appellant argued that providing entertainment was an activity protected by the free speech guaranties of both the federal and state constitutions. Appellant also filed a motion for a temporary injunction to enjoin the enforcement of the ordinance pending the resolution of its § 1983 action. The trial court denied the motion. The court of appeals affirmed the denial. The court of appeals held that the trial court properly concluded that appellant made an inadequate showing of harm if the injunction was denied because the impingement on appellant's constitutional rights was minimal and the overall effect on expression was slight. The court further held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in determining it was unlikely appellant would prevail at trial.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the trial court's order denying appellant cabaret's motion for a preliminary injunction to enjoin enforcement of a city ordinance requiring appellant to close at certain times. The trial court properly concluded that appellant made an inadequate showing of harm if the injunction was denied and did not abuse its discretion in determining it was unlikely appellant would prevail at trial.

 

480 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Dyson v. State Personnel Bd.


No. C003975


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


213 Cal. App. 3d 711; 262 Cal. Rptr. 112; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 886



August 30, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 16, 1989. Lucas, C. J., and Kaufman, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 348909, Joseph A. DeCristoforo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The case is remanded to the trial court with the direction to issue a peremptory writ of mandate compelling the State Personnel Board to set aside Dyson's dismissal as an employee of the Department of Youth Authority's Preston School of Industry. Appellant shall recover his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee challenged an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which affirmed his dismissal as a youth counselor with appellee state agency and argued that evidence seized by appellee from appellant's home should have been excluded from the administrative proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Items were seized from appellant employee's home and held by appellee state agency pursuant to a search for evidence that appellant had committed the crime of theft. The search was initiated, directed, and participated in by appellee's chief of security, acting under his authority as a peace officer. The evidence was turned over by the agency to police authorities for use in a criminal proceeding initiated on the complaint of appellee. The evidence was excluded at trial and the criminal prosecution was dismissed on grounds that the search violated appellant's constitutional right to privacy. Appellant argued that the property should also have been excluded as evidence in the administrative hearing regarding his employment and sought review of the trial court's affirmance of his dismissal. The court reversed appellant's dismissal, holding that the exclusionary rule was available in the circumstances of this case as the unconstitutional search could not have a tighter nexus with appellee, the agency that sought to profit from it. The court further held that appellee was collaterally estopped to deny the invalidity of the search for and seizure of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment to affirm appellee state agency's dismissal of appellant employee from his job as a youth counselor. Unconstitutionally seized evidence should have been excluded in the administrative proceeding that resulted in appellant's dismissal.

 

481 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



W. Am. Ins. Co. v. Chalk


No. D009860


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


213 Cal. App. 3d 825; 261 Cal. Rptr. 837; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 887



August 30, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 22, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 435338, Robert E. Thomas, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant uninsured motorist sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which entered a judgment in favor of plaintiff insurer on its complaint seeking subrogation for uninsured motorists benefits paid to plaintiff's insureds. Defendant claimed that the extended statute of limitations under Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.2(g) did not apply.


OVERVIEW: Defendant uninsured motorist hit another car, injuring three persons and damaging their car. The injured persons were covered by plaintiff insurer. One party settled her claim with plaintiff under Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.2(i) within one year of the accident. The other two parties preserved their right to pursue their uninsured motorist claims under §11580.2(i) by filing suit against plaintiff within one year of the accident, and later settled their claims with plaintiff. Plaintiff sued defendant for subrogation for the amounts plaintiff paid to the injured parties. Defendant contended that the three-year statute of limitations under Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.2(g) did not allow plaintiff to prosecute claims of its insureds to which it became subrogated where the one year statute of limitations applicable to the insureds' personal claims against defendant had expired. The trial court found in favor of plaintiff. The appellate court affirmed and held that the extended limitations period of § 11580.2(g) was intended to protect plaintiff's subrogation rights. The court held that § 11580.2(g) served a legitimate state purpose and was constitutionally valid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for plaintiff insurer in its subrogation suit against defendant uninsured motorist. The court held that the subrogation statute allowed plaintiff to seek subrogation from defendant for benefits paid to plaintiff's insureds, whom defendant injured in an accident, within three years of paying those benefits, despite expiration of the one year statute of limitations for insureds' claims against defendant.

Leach v. City of San Marcos


No. D007281


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


213 Cal. App. 3d 648; 261 Cal. Rptr. 805; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 883



August 29, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N30177, Lawrence Kapiloff, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied appellant's motion for a preliminary injunction that sought to prevent respondent city from adopting a redevelopment plan under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 33000.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer filed a complaint that challenged the validity of a redevelopment plan adopted by respondent city. Appellant filed a motion for a preliminary injunction that sought to prevent respondent from taking any further action to implement the disputed plan. Although appellant presented evidence which suggested that the plan failed to conform with the Community Redevelopment Law, he presented no evidence that an injunction was necessary to prevent irreparable harm pending a trial on the merits of his claims. Cal. Health & Safety Code § 33000. The trial court denied his motion. The court affirmed the order that denied appellant's preliminary injunction because appellant failed to demonstrate that he would likely suffer irreparable harm if the plan was adopted pending a trial on the merits of his claims. While the plan would eventually impose a larger burden on taxpayers outside the plan area, appellant failed to suggest how much of a burden he would suffer, or how the burden would affect him; thus, appellant was not entitled to an injunction since harm could not be presumed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied appellant's motion for a preliminary injunction because appellant presented no evidence that an injunction was necessary to prevent irreparable harm pending a trial on the merits of his claims, and harmed was not presumed.

Sokolow v. County of San Mateo


No. A039117


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


213 Cal. App. 3d 231; 261 Cal. Rptr. 520; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 856; 62 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P42,404



August 17, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 21, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 288559, John G. Schwartz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying appellants' motion for attorney fees and costs is reversed, and the case is remanded to the trial court to make an award of attorney fees and costs consistent with this opinion. Respondents shall pay appellants' costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, plaintiffs in a sexual discrimination case against respondent, county and mounted patrol unit, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County, California. The order denied appellants' motion for attorney fees and costs associated with the discrimination lawsuit, under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, plaintiffs in sexual discrimination case moved for an award of attorney fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5, against respondents county and mounted patrol unit. Appellants had been only partially successful in their complaint to have women admitted to the county mounted patrol unit in that the unit was ordered to either cease operating as an arm of the county sheriff's department, and thus in violation of the equal protection clause of the constitution, or admit women members. Respondents chose to cut ties with the sheriff's department. The trial court held that appellants were not the prevailing party and denied the motion for fees. The court reversed and held that petitioners did prevail as the alternative relief they requested was granted. The court held that while the federal and state statutes provided somewhat different standards for the award of attorney fees, they each involved the threshold determination of whether or not the party seeking an award of fees was the prevailing party.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's denial of appellants' motion for attorney fees and held that the prevailing party was entitled to fees under state and federal rules. The court held that appellants had prevailed in their lawsuit even thought they did not obtain the exact results they hoped for.

 

484 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Violet C.


No. B036972


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


213 Cal. App. 3d 86; 261 Cal. Rptr. 470; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 834



August 15, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Ventura County, No. PS 16372, Roland Short, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant regional social services center sought review of the grant of habeas corpus by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), releasing respondent developmentally disabled adult from a state hospital because she was improperly committed under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6500. Appellant contended that its petition for respondent's involuntary placement was provided by an alternative and nonstatutory vehicle found in recent case law.


OVERVIEW: Appellant regional social services center sought review of a grant of habeas corpus by the lower court to release respondent developmentally disabled adult from a state hospital because she was improperly committed under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6500. Appellant claimed that respondent's involuntary placement, even though she was nonviolent and objected to the commitment, was provided for in recent case law with an alternative and nonstatutory vehicle for judicially committing developmentally disabled adults to a state hospital. The court found that the only proper party to bring a petition for involuntary commitment was one legally authorized to seek commitment as provided in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 4825, and that in the case of a developmentally disabled adult, a showing that he was a danger to himself or others was required by § 6500. The court found nothing in the record that indicated the petition was brought by one legally authorized to seek respondent's commitment; therefore, the procedure used to commit her to a state hospital was improper, her placement was illegal, and the order granting respondent's habeas corpus writ was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order granting respondent developmentally disabled adult's habeas corpus writ was affirmed because appellant regional social services center was not legally authorized to seek respondent's commitment and the placement was illegal. The court found that respondent lacked authority to commit to a state hospital a nonviolent developmentally disabled adult who was unable to care for herself properly.

 

485 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



C. E. Buggy v. Occupational Safety & Health Appeals Bd.


No. C004865


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


213 Cal. App. 3d 1150; 261 Cal. Rptr. 915; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 926; 1989 OSHD (CCH) P28,713



August 11, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 356346, Allen P. Fields, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff subcontractor appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate to defendant Occupational Safety and Health Appeals Board challenging citations for violation of Cal. Lab. Code §§ 1635(b)(7) and (b)(14). Plaintiff alleged that the regulations were unconstitutionally vague and ambiguous and that no substantial evidence supported either citation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff subcontractor was cited by the California Division of Occupational Safety and Health for unsafe working conditions. After its administrative appeals were denied, plaintiff appealed to the trial court, which affirmed the citations. On further appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that persons of common intelligence would necessarily conclude Cal. Lab. Code § 1635(b)(7) required decking to be secured before an employee worked upon it. A less restrictive meaning or application would have seriously endangered employees and conflicted with the mandate of the Occupational Safety and Health Act, Cal. Lab. Code § 6300 et seq. Substantial evidence negated the existence of a "well-devised employer safety program." Plaintiff's practice was insufficiently distinct from the improper procedure engaged in by the injured employees. This situation was within plaintiff's control, and plaintiff was properly precluded from asserting the "independent employee act" affirmative defense. The court held that plaintiff did not maintain "immediate, competent supervision" while its two employees were working over 15 feet above the ground as required.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying the writ of mandate was affirmed on the grounds that the regulations plaintiff subcontractor challenged were not vague and ambiguous and the evidence negated the affirmative defense of "independent employee act." Plaintiff did not maintain "immediate, competent supervision" of the employees.

 

486 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Landry


No. H003859


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


212 Cal. App. 3d 1428; 261 Cal. Rptr. 254; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 822



August 10, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 114533, Robert Michael Foley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Santa Clara County Superior Court (California), that convicted him of second degree murder, arguing he was prejudiced by improper jury instructions regarding second degree felony murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his second degree murder conviction, arguing he was prejudiced by an improper jury instruction regarding second degree felony murder. The court held that the jury instruction on felony murder was erroneously overbroad because it allowed the jury to use an inapplicable underlying felony. A second degree felony murder jury instruction could not be given when based on a felony that was an integral part of the homicide and that was included in fact with the homicide. The court found that although defendant committed three assaults with a deadly weapon, and that one of the felonious assaults could not have been used as the felony underlying felony murder, the jury instructions failed to differentiate between the assaults. As the court could not determine whether the jury relied on an improper or proper felony to convict defendant of felony murder, and as it was clear that the jury could have relied on a felony murder theory in convicting defendant, the imprecise jury instruction prejudiced defendant. Defendant's conviction was thus reversed because there was reasonable doubt as to whether defendant would have been convicted under proper instructions.


OUTCOME: Order convicting defendant of second degree murder was reversed because the jury instructions on second degree felony murder allowed the jury to consider inapplicable underlying felonies, and it was not clear beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant would have been convicted under proper jury instructions.

Franchise Tax Bd. v. Superior Court


No. B042653


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


212 Cal. App. 3d 1343; 261 Cal. Rptr. 236; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 817



August 9, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 26, 1989. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged, and the petition for writ of prohibition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner franchise tax board sought a writ of prohibition to require respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), to sustain petitioner's demurrer to real party in interest taxpayers' complaint for determination of nonresidency.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner franchise tax board issued notice of additional tax. Real party in interest taxpayers protested, claiming to have been residents of different states in the years subject to the tax. Petitioner's determination was affirmed. Real parties in interest filed a complaint in respondent trial court for a determination that they were non-residents. Petitioner demurred on the ground that the action was barred by the anti-injunction provision, Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 32. Respondent overruled the demurrer, and petitioner sought a writ of prohibition. The court denied the petition and discharged the order to show cause, holding that respondent's statutorily authorized action entailed no unconstitutional issuance of court process to prevent or enjoin collection of a tax. The court found that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 19081 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1060.5 did not authorize respondent to prevent or enjoin collection of any tax. Respondent was permitted only to determine the fact of a taxpayer's residence. The court determined that during the litigation, it was only the tax statute, not respondent's acts, that prevented the collection of the disputed tax.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the order to show cause and denied petitioner franchise tax board's request for writ of prohibition. The court held that respondent trial court's statutorily authorized determination of residence entailed no unconstitutional issuance of court process to prevent or enjoin collection of a tax.

Horwath v. City of E. Palo Alto


No. A040661


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


212 Cal. App. 3d 766; 261 Cal. Rptr. 108; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 766



July 28, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 315151, Clarence B. Knight, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owners of rental property sought review of the judgment rendered by the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) that held that appellants' writ of mandate, challenging enforcement of rent rollback, was barred by laches.


OVERVIEW: Appellant owners of rental property petitioned for a writ of mandate in connection with appellee city's enforcement of rent rollback. Appellants asserted that the ordinance was invalid because the city attorney did not explain its impact on existing law. Appellee contended that the statute of limitations and laches barred appellants' claims. The trial court held that that Cal. Elec. The court concluded that the election contest remedy was not available to appellants because they failed to prove they were electors of East Palo Alto under Cal. Elec. Code § 20021. Further appellants' due process challenge was unpersuasive because appellants failed to meet their burden of showing unfairness in the election process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against appellant owners of rental property that challenged enforcement of an election concerning rent rollbacks because appellants failed to meet their burden of showing unfairness in the election process.

 

490 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Rossco Holdings v. Cal.


No. B035188


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


212 Cal. App. 3d 642; 260 Cal. Rptr. 736; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 763



July 26, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of appellants Rossco Holdings Incorporated for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 2, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 662732, Miriam A. Vogel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified by sustaining without leave to amend respondents' demurrer to the ninth through twelfth causes of action on the ground Quaker has waived the right to attack the permit conditions by complying with them and, as so modified, the judgment is affirmed. Respondents to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners brought suit against defendants state and state environmental agency in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), alleging inverse condemnation, violation of their U.S. Const. amend. V rights, and violation of their civil rights under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. Defendants demurred, and the trial court entered judgment for defendants. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs owned land designated as coastal zone on certain state maps and subject to certain regulations. Plaintiffs brought suit against defendants state and state coastal commission, alleging that their property was not properly in the coastal zone, and alleging violations of their rights under U.S. Const. amend. V and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrers to the complaint, and plaintiffs appealed. The court affirmed. The land's location in the coastal zone on the state maps was controlling over descriptive language in the statute defining the coastal zone, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30103(a). The court held that plaintiffs failed to show that the available procedure of asserting the invalidity of permit conditions through a petition for writ of administrative mandate was not a fair forum. The court held that one plaintiff had waived objection to development conditions by complying with them, and failing to exhaust its administrative remedies by filing an action for administrative mandamus before or with its claim for inverse condemnation. The court held that the state and its agencies were not persons for the purpose of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by sustaining defendants' demurrer to certain causes of action on the ground plaintiff waived the right to attack the permit conditions by complying with them and, as modified, affirmed the judgment. The court held that the coastal zone boundary was established by the maps referred to in the Coastal Act, and that the state and its agencies were not persons for the purpose of federal civil rights suits.

Bradley v. State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co.


No. B038592


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


212 Cal. App. 3d 404; 260 Cal. Rptr. 470; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 736



July 20, 1989

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C693747, Aurelio Munoz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying appellant's motion to compel arbitration is reversed and the superior court is ordered to enter a new and different order granting appellant's petition to compel arbitration. Appellant to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insured sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied his petition to compel arbitration regarding the uninsured motorist accident provision in the policy issued to appellant by respondent insurance company.


OVERVIEW: Appellant insured had a uninsured motorist policy with respondent insurance company. After an accident, appellant attempted to collect under the policy and the parties could not agree on a settlement. Appellant filed suit and a petition to compel arbitration. The trial court dismissed the petition. The trial court found that appellant had not complied with the notice requirement of Cal. Ins. Code §11580.2(i)(1) because appellant failed to give respondent notice of the suit against the uninsured driver within one year of the accident. The court reversed and found that Cal. Ins. Code §11580.23 should have been retroactively applied and that respondent did not have a vested right or contractual right in the notice provision of the former code section.


OUTCOME: Decision of the trial court that denied appellant insured's petition to compel arbitration regarding the uninsured motorist accident provision in the policy issued to appellant by respondent insurance company was reversed and remanded. The court found that respondent was not entitled to notice within one year of appellant's suit against the uninsured motorist and that arbitration should have been ordered.

Peters v. Superior Court


No. B041354


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


212 Cal. App. 3d 218; 260 Cal. Rptr. 426; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 738



July 19, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real parties in interest for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 12, 1989.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondeat superior court to forthwith vacate its March 30, 1989, order in Los Angeles Superior Court case No. SCC018254 denying petitioner's motion for preference pursuant to section 36, subdivision (b) and to enter a new and different order granting said motion. Costs on appeal are awarded to petitioners.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner minor sought a writ of mandate ordering respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to grant him trial setting preference pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 36(b), (f) in his medical malpractice action against Los Angeles County, the real party in interest.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner minor filed a medical malpractice action against real party in interest, Los Angeles County. Petitioner then filed a motion for trial setting preference pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 36(b), (f). The trial court denied the motion and petitioner filed for a writ of mandate. The court granted the motion and issued the writ ordering the trial court to vacate its denial and enter a new order granting the motion. The court found that rules of interpretation compelled the conclusion that preference under § 36(b) was mandatory. In addition, contrary to the arguments of the real party in interest, § 36(b) did not violate separation of powers doctrine or equal protection. As for due process, the court found that the real party in interest had no standing to raise that claim and declined to consider it.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate for the trial court to vacate its order denying petitioner minor's motion for preference and to enter a new and different order granting said motion. The court found that the statutory provision was mandatory, did not violate separation of powers or equal protection, and real party in interest had no standing to raise a due process claim.
