In re Debeque


No. D009431


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


212 Cal. App. 3d 241; 260 Cal. Rptr. 441; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 728



July 19, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Petitioner's application for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 31, 1989. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Because DeBeque is not presently entitled to the relief he requests, the order to show cause is discharged and his petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied. The stay previously ordered by this court pending resolution of DeBeque's petition will be vacated 14 days after finality of this writ denial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed for writ of habeas corpus, challenging the trial court's requirement that he register as a sex offender as a condition of his probation, after he pleaded guilty to one misdemeanor count of annoying or molesting children under Cal. Penal Code § 647.56.


OVERVIEW: After his arrest for masturbating in front of two young boys, petitioner pleaded guilty to one misdemeanor count of annoying or molesting children under Cal. Penal Code § 647.6. Defendant was granted probation on the condition that he register as a sex offender. Petitioner filed for a writ of habeas corpus challenging the registration requirement. The court denied the writ, finding that the registration requirement was not cruel and unusual punishment. Cal. Penal Code § 647.6 was enacted to protect children from sexual offenders, and violation of § 647.6 indicated an abnormal sexual interest with respect to children. Lack of physical contact was not dispositive. The fact that petitioner sought out young children in the neighborhood two days in a row, touched their belongings to get their attention while he masturbated, and admitted to having an addiction to exhibitionism, supported the finding that he was likely to repeat the incident. There was not sufficient evidence of disparity in punishments in the same jurisdiction, or other jurisdictions. The court concluded that the registration requirement was not out of proportion to petitioner's offense and did not shock the conscience.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's writ of habeas corpus and found that the requirement that petitioner register as a sex offender was not unconstitutional as cruel and unusual punishment or out of proportion to the offense. The nature of petitioner's offense involving young children and the likelihood that the incident would be repeated supported the registration requirement.

 

494 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Lane v. I.U.O.E. Stationary Eng'rs


No. C005488


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


212 Cal. App. 3d 164; 260 Cal. Rptr. 634; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 718



July 18, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 28, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 347964, Anthony DeCristoforo, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the superior court with directions to enter a new order granting plaintiff 30 days in which to amend the complaint in a manner consistent with this opinion. Costs shall be awarded to plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that dismissed his second amended complaint after its demurrer was sustained without leave to amend in an action by plaintiff against defendant union for breach of contract.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee's complaint alleged that defendant union negligently breached its contract by inadequately representing him before, during and after disciplinary proceedings against him. The trial court ruled that the union owed plaintiff no duty and dismissed his complaint after its demurrer was sustained without leave to amend. The issue on appeal was whether a labor union, voluntarily representing one of its members in a disciplinary hearing before a municipal civil service board, had a duty of care and, if so, the nature of such duty. After review, the court concluded that the union owed plaintiff a duty akin to that of fair representation and such a duty would be breached if the union acted arbitrarily, discriminatorily or in bad faith. The court found that the trial court erred in finding no duty and that the complaint either already stated or could have been amended to state a claim for the breach of its duty to provide the equivalent of fair representation. Because the trial court improperly sustained the demurrer without leave to amend, its judgment was reversed and the case remanded with directions to enter an order granting plaintiff time to amend his complaint.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that dismissed plaintiff employee's complaint without leave to amend was reversed and the case remanded to the superior court with directions to enter a new order granting plaintiff 30 days in which to amend his complaint because the court erred in finding that defendant union owed no duty of care to plaintiff.

 

495 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Sebago, Inc. v. City of Alameda


No. A041693


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


211 Cal. App. 3d 1372; 259 Cal. Rptr. 918; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 685; 16 Media L. Rep. 2377



June 30, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 23, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 621894-4, Joseph Karesh, Judge. +


+ Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which held unconstitutional appellant's ordinance restricting the sale of appellee's sex-related tabloid from city newsracks.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city argued trial court error in ruling its ordinance unconstitutional because the ordinance, which restricted the newsrack locations for selling appellee's sex-related tabloid, was legitimately aimed at bolstering the effectiveness of existing legislation on adult entertainment activity and at restricting access of minors to adult newspapers. The court disagreed, noting first that the latter targeted tabloid "speech" and was, thus, content-based and impermissible. Moreover, the ordinance, also aimed at preventing urban blight, was not content-neutral. No independent studies demonstrated that newsracks, as opposed to bookstores and theaters, had a secondary and negative impact on neighborhoods; nor was this content-based ordinance justified by a compelling state interest, as it was not narrowly drawn and limited to obscenity or matters deemed harmful to minors by statute, but applicable even to benign publications. Because the relationship between ordinance and legitimate state interest was tenuous, the ordinance was constitutionally impermissible. Appellate attorney fees were justified when fees for a successful prosecution had already been awarded appellee.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed holding unconstitutional appellant city's ordinance restricting to certain locations sales of appellee's sex-related tabloid from city newsracks, as ordinance was neither a content-neutral attempt to prevent urban blight nor a content-based effort that was justified by compelling state interest or narrowly drawn and limited to matters deemed harmful to minors by statute.

 

496 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Woods v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


No. D008020


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


211 Cal. App. 3d 1263; 259 Cal. Rptr. 885; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 676



June 28, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 586815, Franklin J. Mitchell, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent California Department of Motor Vehicles suspended appellant's driver's license pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 16070 for failure to establish proof of financial responsibility. Appellant sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that denied a writ of mandate to set aside the suspension.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of a judgment that denied a writ of mandate to compel respondent California Department of Motor Vehicles to set aside the suspension of his driver's license, pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 16070, for failure to establish proof of financial responsibility, on the basis that respondent's failure to comply with the time requirements of Cal. Veh. Code § 16075(b) invalidated his suspension. The court affirmed the judgment on the basis that, generally, requirements relating to the time within which an act had to be done were directory rather than mandatory or jurisdictional, unless a contrary intent was clearly expressed. The court stated that the consistent legislative intent of Cal. Veh. Code § 16075 was to address the problem of uninsured motorists by speedily removing them from the road, not to make a mandatory time period for a hearing, and that the statute imposed no penalty on respondent for failure to meet the statute's time requirements. The court further held that because appellant failed to demonstrate he was prejudiced in any way by the delay, he would not be entitled to any interpretation of the statute to relieve him from his suspension.


OUTCOME: The judgment that denied a writ of mandate was affirmed because the failure of respondent California Department of Motor Vehicles to conduct a hearing pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 16075(b) within 30 days did not invalidate the suspension where no prejudice was established.

 

497 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Daniels v. Tergeson


No. F011029


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


211 Cal. App. 3d 1204; 259 Cal. Rptr. 879; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 654



June 27, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Tuolumne County, No. 29086, Richard L. McMechan, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the election of respondent to the office of fourth district supervisor is nullified. ( Pierce v. Harrold (1982) 138 Cal.App.3d 415, 418-419 [188 Cal.Rptr. 458]; Gov. Code, § 1770, subd. (j).) Costs to appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contestant sought review of a judgment, entered by the Superior Court of Tuolumne County (California), in favor of respondent candidate in appellant's action challenging respondent's election to the position of district supervisor, based upon respondent's failure to meet the registration requirements of Cal. Gov't Code § 25041.


OVERVIEW: Respondent candidate received a winning percentage of the votes cast for the position of district supervisor. Appellant contestant, a registered voter, challenged the election on the ground that respondent was not a registered voter in the district at least 30 days before the deadline, as required by Cal. Gov't Code § 25041. The trial court refused to invalidate the election based on the failure to comply with the requirements of § 25041. The court reversed the judgment. In so ruling, the court held that strict compliance with § 25041 was required, and the fact that respondent missed the deadline for registering by two days rendered him ineligible to run for office. In reaching the conclusion that strict compliance with § 25041 was required, the court relied on the use of the term "shall" in reference to the registration requirement indicating that the provision was mandatory. Moreover, applying strict scrutiny test, the court found the registration requirement in § 25041 to be constitutional because it was narrowly designed to promote the state's interest in regulating the election process to insure only qualified residents were put before the electorate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of respondent candidate in appellant contestant's action challenging respondent's election to the position of district supervisor. The registration requirement in the statute governing the election process must be strictly complied with, and respondent failed to strictly comply with the statute. The court ordered the election of respondent to be nullified.

 

498 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Garcia


No. D008206


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


211 Cal. App. 3d 1096; 260 Cal. Rptr. 71; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 655



June 26, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 11, 1989, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 28, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR92914, Napoleon A. Jones, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment and sentence are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant inmate sought review of an order by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) which entered a judgment for conviction for the offense of unlawful possession of heroin for sale and purchase for the purposes of sale and sentenced him to an eight-year term.


OVERVIEW: Defendant inmate was arrested on an outstanding warrant, and a search incident to his arrest produced tar heroin in his pocket. Appellant contended that the sentence imposed upon his conviction was an illegal sentence, violative of the double-the-base-term provision of Cal. Penal Code §1170.1(g) and violative of the U.S. and California Constitutions. The court found that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11370.2(a) was enacted to add additional penalties where the person found to be in possession of drugs had also possessed drugs for sale in the past and where that person had suffered a prior conviction for possession for sale. The court found that a reading that § 1170(g) precluded imposition of the three-year sentence enhancement under § 11370.2(a) would negate its stated purpose. This would also have been inconsistent with the legislative intent. Accordingly, the judgment and sentence was affirmed because the plain language of § 11370.2(a) meant that a person in defendant's situation should have an additional three-year enhancement added to any term of imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment of conviction for possession for sale and sentence was affirmed because the sentence imposed did not violate a double-the-base-term provision or the U.S. and California Constitutions when the legislative intent was to punish the person who possessed drugs for sale and had suffered a prior conviction for possession for sale.

 

499 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Ladanio


No. H004575


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


211 Cal. App. 3d 1114; 260 Cal. Rptr. 12; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 661



June 26, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 12, 1989. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 116103, Thomas Charles Hastings, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant minor appealed his conviction for attempted first degree murder in the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), alleging that the trial court erred in sentencing him to state prison instead of committing him directly to the California Youth Authority.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a 17 year old minor, was convicted of attempted first degree murder. Defendant appealed, alleging that his sentence of life with the possibility of parole was indeterminate and that therefore he was eligible for commitment to the California Youth Authority (CYA). The court disagreed, noting that the sentence was clearly within the reach of Cal. Welf. & Inst. § 1731.5, which excluded from CYA commitment of juveniles sentenced to imprisonment for life; nowhere did the statute distinguish between those who were or were not eligible for parole. Defendant also argued his sentence violated the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment under U.S. Const. amend VIII and Cal. Const. art I, § 17 because defendants convicted of first degree murder, rather than attempted murder, had a sentencing advantage in being eligible for commitment to CYA. The court noted disparities in sentencing, but held defendant's sentence was constitutional. First, eligibility for CYA commitment did not guarantee that a defendant would be committed there. Also, defendants ineligible for CYA commitment could still be housed there and participate in its programs until they reached 25 years of age.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence for attempted murder was affirmed because the trial court properly found defendant ineligible for commitment to the California Youth Authority and because his sentence of life with the possibility of parole did not violate the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment.

 

500 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Angel P.


No. G006310.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three.


211 Cal. App. 3d 1030; 259 Cal. Rptr. 838; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 652



June 23, 1989 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review denied October 26, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-121098, Gilbert N. Mueller, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that sustained a petition under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 because appellant had obstructed police officers in the performance of their duties under Cal. Penal Code § 148.


OVERVIEW: An undercover police officer was attempting to make an undercover drug deal, when appellant juvenile yelled "Narco, narco" and the drug dealer fled. Appellant was arrested and eventually found in juvenile proceedings to have obstructed the work of a police officer in violation of Cal. Penal. Code § 148. Appellant challenged § 148 on the grounds that the statute was unconstitutionally overbroad. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that § 148 was not unconstitutionally broad on its face, when it was interpreted to require specific intent on the part of the perpetrator. The court held that § 148 was to be strictly construed to apply only to verbal and nonverbal conduct which had the effect of physically restricting, delaying, or obstructing a peace officer in the performance of official duties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that sustained the petition at issue because appellant juvenile had obstructed police officers in the performance of their duties. The court held that the statute setting forth the offense of obstruction was not constitutionally overbroad when strictly construed and there was specific intent on the part of the perpetrator.

 

501 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Martin


No. B024374


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


211 Cal. App. 3d 699; 259 Cal. Rptr. 770; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 626; 86 A.L.R.4th 383



June 20, 1989 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for partial publication. The portions of this opinion to be deleted from publication are identified as those portions between double brackets, e.g. [[/]].


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 27, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR20233, Frederick A. Jones, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant chemical company executive sought review of a conviction from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) for knowingly disposing of hazardous waste or knowingly causing others to dispose of hazardous waste in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25189.5(b). Appellant contended that the act was unconstitutional for vagueness, was an unlawful delegation of legislative authority, and was superseded by federal law.


OVERVIEW: Appellant chemical company executive was convicted on charges that he knowingly disposed of hazardous waste or knowingly caused others to dispose of hazardous waste in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25189.5(b). On appeal, he argued that the statute was unconstitutionally vague, was an unlawful delegation of legislative authority, and was superseded by federal law. The court affirmed the judgment of conviction. The court found that § 25189.5(b) was not unconstitutionally vague because appellant could, from the terms of the statute, reasonably understand the conduct that was prohibited. Because the legislature, not an administrative agency, created the criminal sanction and fixed the penalties, there was not an unlawful delegation of legislative authority. Further, because § 25189.5(b) was more stringent and regulated empty containers, which were exempt from federal regulation, the state law was not superseded by federal law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction. The court held that the act was constitutional, that it was not vague or ambiguous, that it did not unlawfully delegate legislative authority, and that it was not superseded by federal law.

 

502 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Crawford v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


No. B036621


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


213 Cal. App. 3d 156; 259 Cal. Rptr. 414; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 609; 54 Cal. Comp. Cases 198



June 13, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 12, 1989, and petitioner's application for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 31, 1989. Broussard, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ of prohibition is discharged, and the petition for a peremptory writ of prohibition or in the alternative for a writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner psychiatrist sought a writ of prohibition to restrain further contempt proceedings against him by respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (WCAB). The matter was before the court on a transfer order from the California Supreme Court directing the court to issue an alternative writ requiring respondent WCAB to either cease and desist or to show cause why a peremptory writ should not issue.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (WCAB) charged petitioner psychiatrist with contempt for the willful violation of Cal. Code Regs. tit. 8, § 10606, Workers' Comp. App. Bd. R. 10606, in connection with reports he submitted in workers' compensation cases over a two-year period. Petitioner's application for a writ of prohibition restraining further proceedings was denied. However, the state supreme court issued a transfer order directing the court to issue an alternative writ requiring respondent WCAB to cease and desist or to show cause why a preemptory writ should not issue. After a hearing, the court discharged the writ and denied relief to petitioner. The court held that the judicial power vested in respondent WCAB pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code §§ 111, 134 included the power to issue all necessary process in proceedings for contempt. The affidavits and documents in support of the order to show cause established a prima facie showing of contempt sufficient to justify its issuance under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1209. The record did not support assertion of a statute of limitations defense under Cal. Penal Code § 802 or Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.


OUTCOME: The alternative writ of prohibition was discharged, and the petition for a peremptory writ of prohibition or, in the alternative, for a writ of mandate was denied. Respondent Worker's Compensation Appeals Board was authorized by statute to issue all necessary process in proceedings for contempt. Respondent established a prima facie showing of contempt sufficient to justify issuance of the order to show cause served upon petitioner psychiatrist.

 

503 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Grand Jury of San Diego County ex rel. Miller v. Superior Court


No. D009903.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One.


211 Cal. App. 3d 740; 259 Cal. Rptr. 404; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 603



June 13, 1989 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Modification of Opinion on Denial of Rehearing July 12, 1989; Review denied September 28, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976 Cal. Rules of Ct.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a grand jury engaged in a murder investigation, requested, by writ of mandate, review of an order made by respondent, the Superior Court of the County of San Diego (California), that ruled that all information in the possession of an investigator for the murder suspect's defense attorney, relating to the case, was subject to the attorney-client privilege.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner grand jury was engaged in a murder investigation. The prime suspect in the murder was represented by an attorney, who retained the assistance of private investigator The grand jury learned that the private investigator knew of the identity of an eyewitness to an argument between the suspect and the victim before her murder, and it subpoenaed the investigator to disclose the information. The trial court ruled that all information in the private investigator's possession relating to the case was subject to the attorney-client privilege. The grand jury petitioned for writ of mandate. The court sustained the decision of the trial court denying the interrogation, but not solely on the attorney-client privilege, but because of the pre-indictment nature of the case. It held that the right to counsel under the U.S. Const. amend. VI, unimpeded by interference of the prosecution, together with the privilege against self-incrimination, under U.S. Const. amend. V, precluded the questioning sought by the grand jury. It held that the investigator's work product was privileged. The court denied the writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The denied petitioner's writ of mandate. The court held that the preclusion against interference with the representation of counsel was applicable equally to pre-indictment as to post-indictment investigations. It held that the private investigator, being an agent of the attorney, could not be forced to give testimony.

 

504 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Lusardi Constr. Co. v. Aubry


No. D007559


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


2 Cal. App. 4th 1135; 259 Cal. Rptr. 250; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 565; 116 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P56,328



June 6, 1989, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted September 27, 1989 (S011121)
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 211 Cal. App. 3d 265 and 224 Cal. App. 3d 336 and 231 Cal. App. 3d 1167.)] (See 1 Cal. 4th 976)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 579903, Vincent P. Di Figlia, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, agency and officials, appealed a summary judgment rendered by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) in favor of plaintiff contractor, that determined that plaintiff was not bound by the prevailing wage law requirements on a project. Defendants argued that a public agency was unable to avoid the wage law provisions by contracting to purchase a completed project from a private third party rather than contracting directly.


OVERVIEW: A private corporation entered into a contract with a defendant agency to purchase a new hospital facility. The corporation then entered into a contract with plaintiff contractor to construct the building. Plaintiff was initially told that the project was a private project. However, six months after plaintiff commenced performance, defendant agency informed plaintiff that it considered the project a public works requiring compliance with prevailing wage law, Cal. Lab. Code § 1720 et seq. Thereafter, plaintiff filed suit for declaratory relief, and the trial court entered summary judgment in plaintiff's favor. On appeal, defendants, agency and officials, argued that a public agency was unable to avoid the wage law provisions by contracting to purchase the completed project from a private third party rather than contracting directly. The court affirmed. The court ruled that the trial court's determination that defendant's adjudication was accomplished without adherence to due process was justified because no notice and hearing procedure was utilized. Moreover, defendant agency was estopped from denying that the project was private because plaintiff relied on that representation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment rendered in favor of plaintiff contractor. The court ruled that the trial court correctly determined that the prevailing wage law provisions were inapplicable to plaintiff's project because defendant agency did not adhere to due process notice and hearing requirements.

 

505 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Washington


No. A039608


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


211 Cal. App. 3d 207; 259 Cal. Rptr. 307; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 562



June 6, 1989 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rules 976 and 976.1, California Rules of Court, the Statement of the Case, part I and the Disposition of this opinion are certified for publication.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 21, 1989. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J. were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Napa County, No. 6519, Herbert W. Walker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Napa County (California) which convicted defendant of rape and simple assault. The trial court in a separate action enhanced the convictions for a prior serious felony conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought to exclude for purposes of impeachment evidence of three prior felonies. The trial court deferred the matter, although it stated it would rule before trial. However, a new judge determined that he would not rule until after defendant testified. Defendant did not testify and he was found guilty of rape and simple assault. The trial court enhanced his convictions for having a prior serious felony conviction. The court of appeal affirmed. Postponing a ruling on the motion to exclude the use of prior convictions to impeach until after defendant testified was unusual because it impinged upon his right to testify or not to testify. The threat of impeachment was a coercive force that would compel a defendant to testify against his wishes. However, based on controlling case law, the court found that the trial court was allowed to wait. That law found that a trial court must have known the precise nature of a defendant's testimony in order to balance its probative value against prejudicial effect. Such knowledge was unknowable if a defendant, as in this case, did not testify.


OUTCOME: The court of appeal affirmed the conviction of defendant for rape and simple assault. The trial court had the authority to wait until after defendant testified before ruling on a motion to exclude for purposes of impeachment evidence of prior convictions. The trial court could do this because it could not have known the precise nature of defendant's testimony in order to balance its probative value against prejudicial effect.

 

506 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Building Industry Assn. v. Superior Court


Nos. D009161, D009135


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


211 Cal. App. 3d 277; 259 Cal. Rptr. 325; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 564



June 6, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 27, 1989, and petitioner's application for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 17, 1989. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Writs denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner developers sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied their motion for summary judgment against real party in interest, city. Petitioners urged that city's policy of development quotas was inconsistent with the public welfare and sought a writ of mandate against it to compel the trial court to enjoin city from enforcing its policy.


OVERVIEW: City, the real party in interest, adopted a policy by which the city would have control over the rate of development of residential property, setting development quotas on a year to year basis. Petitioner developers sought to compel the trial court to enjoin city from enforcing its new policy. Petitioners urged that the policy had no relation to the public welfare and was an arbitrary and capricious exercise of police power. The trial court denied petitioners' motion for summary judgment, holding that city had shown that there were still substantial issues of material fact in dispute, and petitioner's sought a writ of mandate to overturn the ruling. Petitioners put forward 12 asserted conflicts with city's public welfare, and after examination of each, the court held that none of the alleged conflicts could be resolved without reference to a factual issue in dispute. The court therefore concluded that the trial court properly denied petitioners' motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: As each of the 12 alleged conflicts between real party in interests city's development policy and the public welfare could not be resolved without reference to a disputed issue of fact, the court held that the denial of petitioner developers' motion for summary judgment was proper. The decision of the trial court was affirmed and petitioners' writs of mandate were denied.

 

507 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Bennett v. Bodily


No. B031878


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


211 Cal. App. 3d 133; 259 Cal. Rptr. 199; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 551



June 1, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 17, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C545717, Norvell F. Woods, Jr., and Dion G. Morrow, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and the matter remanded to the superior court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. Appellant is awarded costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff uninsured motorist appealed from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which nullified an arbitration award to plaintiff and entered judgment in favor of the negligent defendant. On appeal, plaintiff argued that the trial court's action amounted to an impermissible denial of due process.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff uninsured motorist was involved in an automobile collision and obtained an arbitration award as recompense for personal injuries caused by the negligent driving of defendant. Alleging personal injuries arising from the same collision to himself, defendant sought and received money from his insurer under his uninsured motorist protection. Plaintiff was without notice and had no access or involvement in those proceedings. The trial court thereafter nullified the arbitration award and entered judgment in favor of defendant and plaintiff appealed. The court determined that 1983 Cal. Stat. ch. 1252, § 10, failed to provide any procedure to protect plaintiff's interest in the arbitration award. The court concluded that ch. 1252 could not reach those remedial or deterrent objectives by allowing an unbridled private power to assault the uninsured motorist's protected property rights in direct violation of the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. Therefore, the court reversed the judgment and remanded the case.


OUTCOME: Judgment nullifying the arbitration award to plaintiff uninsured motorist was reversed and the matter was remanded to the superior court for further proceedings consistent with the opinion. The statute at issue violated plaintiff's rights to due process of law as guaranteed by the Constitutions of California and of the United States.

 

508 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Mandicino v. Maggard


No. A037969


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


210 Cal. App. 3d 1413; 258 Cal. Rptr. 917; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 544



May 30, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 29, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 284130, Richard Patsey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment awarding attorney fees is reversed. Cater is awarded her costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city clerk sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which granted respondent initiative sponsor's request for a peremptory writ of mandate concerning misleading statements in ballot arguments, granted respondent's motion for attorney fees pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5, and ordered appellant to pay the attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Respondent initiative sponsor filed a petition for a writ of mandate under Cal. Elec. Code § 5025 against appellant city clerk. Respondent challenged as false and misleading six ballot arguments submitted by appellant in opposition to Measure C in the June 1986 municipal election. The trial court granted respondent's peremptory writ of mandate, granted her motion for attorney fees under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5, the private attorney general statute, and ordered appellant to pay the attorney fees. The court reversed the award of attorney fees to respondent because the trial court abused its discretion in awarding them under § 1021.5. The court stated that de minimis modifications to opinion statements in the ballot argument failed to confer a significant benefit upon voters. The trial court's modifications were minor editorial changes to the language of appellant's argument. The meaning of the argument or its implications were not substantially altered. The changes were different from the deletions sought by respondent. In addition, the court found that the modifications were not required by § 5025 and therefore did not result in a significant public benefit.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the award of attorney fees to respondent initiative sponsor because the trial court abused its discretion in awarding attorney fees under the statutory provision where de minimis modifications to opinion statements in a ballot argument failed to confer a significant benefit upon voters. The modifications were not required by a statutory provision and therefore did not result in a significant public benefit.

Tahoe-Sierra Pres. Council v. State Water Res. Control Bd.


No. C000386


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


210 Cal. App. 3d 1421; 259 Cal. Rptr. 132; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 595



May 30, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 28, 1989, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 21, 1989. Panelli, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Placer County, No. 58789, Richard A. Sims and George Yonehiro, Judges.



DISPOSITION: As to plaintiffs' claims of takings by unreasonable overregulation the judgment is modified to one of dismissal on the ground that the claim is not ripe for the reasons given in this opinion. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed. The parties shall recover their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Placer County (California), which granted judgment on the pleadings to respondent state water board in appellants' action concerning a plan respondent adopted to prevent increased surface runoff of water carrying soil products into a lake.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners sued respondent state water board, alleging that respondent's plan to prevent increased surface runoff was unconstitutional and resulted in a regulatory taking of their property without just compensation. Appellants sought review after the lower court granted judgment on the pleadings to respondent. The appellate court affirmed the judgment, finding that the plan was constitutional. However, the court modified the judgment to dismiss the taking claim on the ground that it was not ripe for review because appellants failed to seek just compensation or an administrative determination. The court found that Cal. Water Code § 13170 permitted respondent to adopt a water quality control plan and that the Federal Water Pollution Control Act did not preclude it from utilizing broader authority than mandated to regulate nonpoint sources of pollution. As such, the court found that respondent's waste discharge permit system was constitutional. Because the plan enabled each landowner to show that his building proposal, though in excess of that permitted, did not increase water discharge, the court found that appellants were not denied due process.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment to one of dismissal with respect to appellant landowners' claim that a plan by respondent state water board amounted to a regulatory taking because appellants did not seek a determination from respondent or just compensation, both prerequisites to alleging a taking. The court affirmed the remainder of the judgment, holding that the plan did not exceed respondent's statutory and constitutional authority.

People v. Sewell

Ctr. for Public Interest Law v. Fair Political Practices Com


No. D008786


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


210 Cal. App. 3d 1476; 259 Cal. Rptr. 21; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 532



May 30, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' application for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 17, 1989.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ denied; alternative writ is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public interest law firm requested a writ of mandamus to set aside a formal opinion of respondent, the Fair Political Practice Commission, that certain provisions of the Campaign Spending Act of 1986 were ineffective because they conflicted with the Campaign Contribution Limits Without Taxpayer Financing Amendments to the Political Reform Act, which received more votes.


OVERVIEW: The people adopted two initiative measures, proposition 68, the Campaign Spending Act of 1986, and proposition 73, the Campaign Contribution Limits Without Taxpayer Financing Amendments to the Political Reform Act, which addressed campaign finance reform. These measures contained provisions that conflicted with each other, and a formal opinion was requested to determine whether any of the provisions of proposition 68, which received the lesser number of votes, survived. After a public hearing respondent, the Fair Political Practices Commission, issued an opinion that the provisions of proposition 68 did not survive. Petitioner, a public interest law firm, requested a writ of mandamus seeking to overturn respondent's finding. The court denied petitioner's request and affirmed respondent's decision. The court reviewed both initiatives and concluded that that an irreconcilable conflict existed between them. The court applied the constitutional standard and found that proposition 73, which received the higher number of votes, prevailed.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner public interest law firm's request for a writ of mandamus seeking to overturn a finding by respondent, the Fair Political Practice Commission, than of two conflicting initiative measures, the one that received fewer votes did not survive. The court found that the conflicts between the two initiatives were irreconcilable and the measure that received the higher number of affirmative votes prevailed.

First English Evangelical Lutheran Church v. County of L.A.


No. B003702


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


210 Cal. App. 3d 1353; 258 Cal. Rptr. 893; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 527; 19 ELR 21329



May 26, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 23, 1989, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 25, 1989. Lucas, C. J., Panelli, J., and Kaufman, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C273634, Albert D. Matthews, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment dismissing the cause of action for inverse condemnation based on enactment of ordinance No. 11,855 is affirmed for the reasons recited in this opinion. In all other respects the opinion this court filed on June 25, 1985, and in which remittitur issued on November 4, 1985, remains in full force and effect. Accordingly, the case is remanded for further proceedings consistent with that opinion as to the cause of action for inverse condemnation based on cloud seeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant church challenged a ruling of the California appellate court, which dismissed appellant's action against appellee, the County Flood Control District, for inverse condemnation under Cal. Gov't Code § 835. The Supreme Court of California then heard the same action on appeal and the case was now back before the appellate court on remand for further proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Appellant church owned a church camp. When a storm dumped several inches and flooded the area, many buildings on certain portions of appellant's camp were destroyed. Appellee, the County Flood Control District, passed a regulatory interim ordinance which prohibited temporarily certain uses of appellant's land which was previously flooded until studies were performed on any likelihood of reoccurrence. The ordinance prohibited the reconstruction of buildings only on certain portions of appellant's land, and allowed other use of that land. Appellant filed an inverse condemnation action and claimed an unconstitutional taking had occurred under Cal.Const. art. I, § 19. Particularly, appellant claimed appellee was liable under Cal. Govt. Code § 835. This § 835 action was dismissed by the trial and appellate court, and the Supreme Court of California ruled that compensatory damages could be awarded if a taking occurred, but remanded to the appellate court the issue of whether there was a taking. The appellate court held that no taking occurred where the regulatory action was temporary, intended to advance public safety interests, and allowed certain uses of appellant's land.


OUTCOME: The California appellate court, who had this case before them on remand from the Supreme Court of California, dismissed the claim at issue and held that appellant church did not suffer an unconstitutional taking where regulatory actions taken by appellee, the County Flood Control District, were temporary, intended to protect public health and safety, and did not restrict all uses of appellant's land.

Brian T. v. Pac. Bell


No. A041423


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


210 Cal. App. 3d 894; 258 Cal. Rptr. 707; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 512



May 22, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 17, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. CH128655-7, Michael Ballachey and James F. Perley, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, parents of minors and organizations formed to protect children, challenged an order of the Alameda County Superior Court (California), which denied injunctive relief and granted the demurrer of respondents, a telephone company and 976 vendors.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, parents of minors, filed suit for nuisance and unlawful business practices in disseminating sexually explicit messages to minors through 976 telephone numbers; and appellants, organizations formed to protect joined the suit, sought injunctive relief. The trial court granted demurrers to the jurisdiction filed by respondents, a telephone company and 976 vendors, and denied injunctive relief. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the Public Utility Commission had regulatory power over telephone services, including the power to block 976 numbers, that review of commission orders could only be sought by mandamus to the supreme court, and that the telephone company was absolved from tort liability because it acted in accordance with a commission order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed because a challenge to Public Utilities Commission orders, in this case over 976 telephone service, could only be brought by mandamus to the California Supreme Court and telephone companies were absolved from tort liability if they acted under commission orders.

Cal. Coastal Com v. Superior Court


No. D009001


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


210 Cal. App. 3d 1488; 258 Cal. Rptr. 567; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 484



May 18, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 9, 1989.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ issue directing the superior court to vacate its order overruling defendants' demurrer to the complaint and to enter a new order sustaining that demurrer. The parties to bear their respective costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state challenged the decision of the trial court (California), which denied respondent's demurrer in real party in interest individuals proceedings for inverse condemnation seeking $ 1 million in damages alleging the permit condition requiring dedication of a public access easement amounted to an unconstitutional taking of private property without compensation.


OVERVIEW: The court issued a peremptory writ directing the trial court to vacate its order overruling respondent's demurrer and to enter a new order sustaining the demurrer. The court found that real party in interest individual never sought mandate relief. The court held that the validity of the permit condition was at issue in the prior administrative proceeding, and because real party failed to contest that validity by the means provided for judicial review, he was now estopped to relitigate the same issue in the context of an inverse condemnation action. The court held that respondent commission quite clearly had subject matter jurisdiction and the authority to impose permit conditions reasonably related to any burdens on the public beach created by the construction of real party's residence. The court found that the fact that respondent incorrectly analyzed the relationship between the burdens and the condition it sought to impose did not mean it acted in excess of its jurisdiction. The court held that if real party believed respondent was wrong, he had a remedy by way of judicial review and Having failed to avail himself of that recourse, he had no basis for complaint.


OUTCOME: The court issued a preemptory writ directing the trial court to vacate its order overruling respondent's demurrer to the complaint and to enter a new order sustaining that demurrer because real party in interest individual was collaterally estopped from relitigating the issue.

People v. Superior Court (moraza)


No. H005639


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


210 Cal. App. 3d 592; 258 Cal. Rptr. 499; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 482



May 15, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real party in interest for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 24, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Health & Saf. Code, § 11488.4, subd. (h)) seeking review of the trial court's order.



DISPOSITION: Real party in interest has been notified that a peremptory writ in the first instance could be issued here, and he has filed opposition. The peremptory writ of mandate will issue in the first instance. ( Code Civ. Proc., § 1088; Palma v. U.S. Industrial Fasteners, Inc. (1984) 36 Cal.3d 171, 177-182 [203 Cal.Rptr. 626, 681 P.2d 893].) Let a writ of mandate issue as prayed, directing respondent court to vacate its order dated March 10, 1989, and to enter a new and different order denying real party's motion for return of the forfeited currency. Our temporary stay of execution dated April 7, 1989, shall remain in effect until this opinion becomes final.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people petitioned for a statutory mandate, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11488.4(h), seeking review of the decision of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California) in a forfeiture proceeding that granted real party in interest claimant's motion to return the seized property of currency.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest claimant successfully moved for the return of currency seized during his arrest on a domestic matter, arguing that a precondition of a forfeiture was a drug-related arrest and further that there must have been probable cause at the seizure of property for forfeiture. The people sought a writ of mandate to overturn the lower court's order. The court found that the government had the burden of proving by clear and convincing evidence, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11488.4(i)(4), that the property was subject to forfeiture within Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11470. The court concluded that state law did not require a drug arrest to sustain a forfeiture. The court ruled that the issues of constitutionality and of statutory probable cause for the forfeiture were satisfied, because the initial seizure was part of a legitimate search for a weapon followed by a protective inventory search, and there was probable cause to believe the money was the fruit of narcotics transactions in light of all the evidence. Petition was granted, instructing the trial court to vacate its order and to deny real party in interest's request for return of the currency.


OUTCOME: The court granted the people's request for a writ of mandate, and ordered the trial court to vacate its previous order and to issue a new order denying the return of the forfeited currency to real party in interest claimant because both forfeiture requirements of constitutionality and of statutory probable cause were satisfied.

 

516 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Anderson


No. D007231


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


210 Cal. App. 3d 414; 258 Cal. Rptr. 482; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 453



May 11, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 10, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CRN12180, Don Martinson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which entered a judgment of conviction after a jury found appellant guilty of two counts of receiving stolen property. Appellant challenged the trial court's instruction as to the evidentiary effect of his possession of recently stolen property, contending that the instruction unconstitutionally shifted the burdens of proof.


OVERVIEW: After a jury trial, appellant was convicted of two counts of receiving stolen property and challenged his conviction, contending that Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 2.15 assumed the property was "recently" stolen and deprived the jury of the opportunity to decide that fact, that the evidence did not warrant giving a bracketed portion of the instruction referring to "false statements," and that the instruction was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the trial court's order, holding that it assumed the jury determined whether the property was "recently" stolen before it considered the inference of guilt permitted by the instruction, and that the jury reasonably could have concluded that appellant's possession of a stolen car within one month of the theft lead to an inference that he knew of the car's "recently" stolen nature. Evidence of appellant's intentionally false statement regarding the property's ownership warranted reference to the "false statements" portion of the instruction. The court held that the instruction did not compel the jury to find guilty knowledge based on slight corroborative evidence, and that there was no constitutionally suspect presumption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting appellant of two counts of receiving stolen property, because the jury instruction at issue did not deprive the jury of an opportunity to decide an essential fact, the trial court did not err in giving certain portions of the instruction which were supported by sufficient evidence, and the instruction did not create a constitutionally suspect presumption of guilty knowledge.

 

517 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Curl v. Superior Court


No. F011191.


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


223 Cal. App. 3d 1058; 258 Cal. Rptr. 308; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 442



May 5, 1989.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 31, 1989.
Review granted August 17, 1989 (S010655) Order grt. review was modified September 21, 1989: Pursuant to rule 29.2(b), Cal. Rules of Ct., review is limited to the following issues discussed in the Ct. App. opinion: (1) whether the constitutional validity of a prior murder conviction underlying a prior murder-special circumstance can be challenged by pretrial motion; and (2) what is the standard of proof by which the People must establish the constitutional validity of such a prior conviction.


PRIOR HISTORY: This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of May 5, 1989, previously reported at 210 Cal. App. 3d 1310.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, charged with murder and a special circumstance allegation for a prior murder conviction, sought a writ of mandate and prohibition from a decision of respondent Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which found by clear and convincing evidence, following petitioner's motion to strike the special circumstance allegation, under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(2), that a prior second degree murder conviction was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged with murder with a special circumstance allegation, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(2), for a previous murder conviction. Petitioner filed a motion to strike the special circumstance alleging that he had been under the influence of drugs and not properly advised of his rights when he pled to the prior murder charge. Respondent superior court denied the motion, finding by clear and convincing evidence that the prior conviction was constitutional. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate against respondent, and the court denied the for writ because respondent used a higher standard of proof than was required due to the fact that the parties did not waive jury trial on the allegation. The court held that petitioner's motion was the equivalent of a Cal. Penal Code § 995 motion requiring the pretrial challenge to be based upon reasonable cause to bind over for trial on the special circumstance allegation. The court held that the actual determination of a prior conviction's constitutional validity was a jury question to be proven beyond a reasonable doubt, of which such proof could consist of custom and habit evidence, under Cal. Evid. Code § 1105.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ of mandate because respondent superior court found by clear and convincing evidence that petitioner's prior conviction was constitutional. The court held that, unless both sides waived the right to jury trial on the special circumstance allegation, a challenge was to be decided based upon whether or not reasonable cause, a lesser standard, had been shown to bind petitioner over for trial on the special circumstance.

San Franciscans for Reasonable Growth v. City and County of San Francisco


No. A035010


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


209 Cal. App. 3d 1502; 258 Cal. Rptr. 267; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 427



May 2, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 801050, Roy L. Wonder, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs challenged an order from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), discharging its peremptory writ of administrative mandamus which directed defendant City and County of San Francisco and its administrative bodies to prepare a supplemental environmental impact report in compliance with the California Environmental Quality Act and state guidelines, Cal. Code Regs. tit. 14, § 15000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The lower court entered an order discharging its peremptory writ of administrative mandamus which directed defendant City and County of San Francisco and its administrative bodies to prepare a supplemental environmental impact report (EIR) in compliance with the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA) and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 14, § 15000 et seq., on a certain project. In addition to requiring the supplemental EIR, the writ had commanded defendant to reconsider its prior approval of the project. Defendant reaffirmed its earlier resolution approving the project without imposing any additional mitigation measures. On appeal, at issue was whether defendant abused its discretion in reapproving the project by failing to consider additional mitigation measures in the areas of child care, open space, and housing. The court found project-specific or cumulative impacts on the availability of child care programs were not environmental impacts and, therefore, defendant had no duty under CEQA to condition project reapproval on exactions from the project sponsor in this area. The court concluded that defendant discharged its duty to begin anew the analytic process and affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment below in favor of defendant City and County of San Francisco and its administrative bodies on the grounds that defendant discharged its duty to begin anew the analytic process.

Interinsurance Exch. of the Auto. Club of S. Cal. v. Spectrum Inv. Corp.


No. B029357


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


209 Cal. App. 3d 1243; 258 Cal. Rptr. 43; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 398



April 26, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied July 27, 1989. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insurer challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that entered a declaratory judgment in favor of defendant rental company.


OVERVIEW: Defendant rented a vehicle to plaintiff's insured and she was involved in a car accident. Claims were filed against both plaintiff and defendant. Plaintiff filed an action to determine who had the duty to defend and indemnify insured. The trial court found plaintiff was responsible. Plaintiff appealed that decision claiming that Cal. Ins. Ins. Code § 11580.9(d). The court agreed with defendant, holding that plaintiff was responsible for the defense and indemnification of its insured.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment holding that plaintiff insurer was responsible for the defense and indemnification of its insured because the cash deposit did not describe or rate the vehicle as owned by defendant rental company, and insured was not liable as a permissive user under insurance statute regulating uninsured motorists.

 

520 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Gene R. Smith Corp. v. Terry's Tractor


No. D007928


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


209 Cal. App. 3d 951; 257 Cal. Rptr. 598; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 359



April 18, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied July 13, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N30755, Jeffrey T. Miller, Judge.



DISPOSITION: (See fn. 1.) The judgment is affirmed. 1



1 Our disposition reflects our rejection of Smith's argument that the court erred in entering judgment after granting the Light Gallery's motion for judgment on the pleadings and his request that we vacate the judgment. The entry of a judgment implementing a court's order occurs routinely for a number of valid reasons. Smith's implicit quarrel with the judgment here is that it removes all the defendants when only a single defendant brought the motion. Although in certain circumstances the trial court's broad sweep might be improper we conclude the action here was a prudent and economical use of judicial resources. Lacking subject matter jurisdiction over one defendant the court lacked subject matter jurisdiction over all.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which dismissed plaintiff's claim alleging wrongful and malicious prosecution of an involuntary bankruptcy by defendants, because the claim was preempted by federal law.


OVERVIEW: After plaintiff allegedly failed to pay debts to defendants, defendants unsuccessfully moved to have plaintiff placed in involuntary bankruptcy. Plaintiff then brought action in the state court, alleging malicious prosecution and abuse of process by defendants. The court dismissed the action, holding that the exclusive jurisdiction with respect to bankruptcy proceedings rested in the federal courts. By allowing plaintiff to proceed in the state court in an action relating to bankruptcy, the court would be subverting the exclusive jurisdiction of the federal courts. Plaintiff's argument that limiting his remedy to a bankruptcy forum would deprive him of a jury trial was rejected, because there was no constitutional right to a jury trial in matters arising in bankruptcy.


OUTCOME: Judgment dismissing plaintiff's claim was affirmed, because the state court did not have jurisdiction over cases relating to bankruptcy.

 

521 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Liebig v. Superior Court


No. A041742


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


209 Cal. App. 3d 828; 257 Cal. Rptr. 574; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 346



April 14, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied June 22, 1989.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner grandfather sought review by extraordinary writ of an order from the respondent superior court (California), which after retransfer by the state supreme court, permitted a suit by plaintiff granddaughter that sought tort damages for sexual molestation. The suit was filed within the three-year extension of time under Cal. Code of Civ. Proc. § 340.1. Petitioner claimed a deprivation of his vested right to the repose of the action.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner grandfather was sued by his granddaughter, who sought tort damages after she reached majority, for sexual molestation when she was between the ages of five and 14. Respondent granddaughter filed her suit under Cal. Code of Civ. Proc. § 340.1, which created a three year extension of the time limit that would otherwise have time-barred her action. Petitioner argued that the extension deprived him of a vested right to the repose of the cause of action under the previous one-year period. The state supreme court granted review and retransferred the matter with directions to issue an alternative writ. On review, the court again denied the writ, finding that enlargement of limitations operated prospectively unless a statute expressly provided otherwise, which § 340.1 did. The legislature had the power to retroactively extend a civil statute of limitations to revive a cause of action time-barred under a former limitations period. Vested rights were not immune from retroactive laws when an important state interest was at stake such as the availability of tort relief to plaintiffs who had been the victims of sexual abuse while a minor.


OUTCOME: The alternate writ was discharged and peremptory writ denied because vested rights were not immune from retroactive laws when an important state interest was at stake such as the availability of tort relief to plaintiffs who had been the victim of sexual abuse while a minor.
