People v. Workman


No. F008490


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


209 Cal. App. 3d 687; 257 Cal. Rptr. 753; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 330



April 11, 1989 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part II.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 9, 1989, and Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was denied July 20, 1989. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 30820, Robert T. Baca, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Six defendants convicted on numerous thefts arising from an oil stealing scheme appealed the judgments of conviction of the Superior Court of Kern County (California). The trial court denied their motions to dismiss that had challenged the state's refiling of the complaint and the second magistrate's failure to give collateral estoppel effect to the first magistrate's orders on the suppression motions.


OVERVIEW: Six defendants were charged with theft in an oil-stealing scheme. A preliminary examination commenced on a 25-count complaint. The magistrate granted motions to suppress that were made by defendant A, oil company representative, and defendant B, distributor. The case was dismissed without prejudice on the state's motion. The state's second complaint alleged 70 counts based on the same transactions. Defendants challenged the propriety of the second filing under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(d), and requested application of collateral estoppel. The second magistrate rejected the arguments, denied renewed motions to suppress, and held defendants to answer. Thereafter, defendants A and B moved to dismiss plaintiff 's information filed in the trial court. The trial court allowed all defendants to join the motions, which were denied. No contest plea agreement was reached. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgments of conviction, concluding that Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(j) applied, the second filing and issue relitigation were proper, and the trial court erred in finding that other defendants had standing to challenge the search of defendant B's business. Sentencing errors were rejected.


OUTCOME: The judgments were affirmed. Other defendants did not have standing to challenge denial of the suppression motions in the search of one defendant's business; it was error to have joined all defendants in the motion. Another defendant, an oil company representative, had standing to object to the search of his home, but his challenge was without merit because state law allowed for refiling of the complaint when he had not been held to answer.

 

523 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Hunter v. City of Whittier


No. B029923


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


209 Cal. App. 3d 588; 257 Cal. Rptr. 559; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 325; 66 Rad. Reg. 2d (P 8;F) 1110



April 10, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA001044, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the preliminary injunction granted by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in favor of respondent satellite disk owners, which restrained appellant from enforcing the ordinance that regulated satellite television receiving antennas in a residential zone.


OVERVIEW: Respondent satellite disk owners had placed disks on the roofs of their homes. Appellant city subsequently enacted an ordinance that regulated the antennas in a residential zone. The ordinance required an owner to obtain a conditional use permit. Respondents applied for permits. Appellant conditioned the issuance of the permits on relocation of the antennas to the ground and in a way that the antennas would be screened from street and surrounding property views. Administrative appeals were exhausted. Respondents filed a class action suit to restrain enforcement of the ordinance and for damages. Respondents argued the ordinance was a violation of 47 C.F.R. § 25.104. The trial court granted a preliminary injunction that restrained appellant's enforcement of the ordinance, which was affirmed on appeal. The court determined that appellant's ordinance did not enact specific screening or placement requirements for the antennas which gave unduly broad discretion to administrative officials in light of the Federal Communication Commission's requirements that aesthetic objectives had to be clearly defined and take into account respondents' federal rights.


OUTCOME: The preliminary injunction in favor of respondent satellite disk owners was affirmed. The court determined that appellant city's antenna use ordinance lacked the "clearly defined " objectives necessary to avoid federal preemption in its general standards for issuance of a conditional use permit.

 

524 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Burrell v. City of L.A.


No. B027696


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


209 Cal. App. 3d 568; 257 Cal. Rptr. 427; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 324



April 10, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
As Modified April 25, 1989. Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied July 20, 1989. Kennard, J., did not Participate therein. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C603176, Jerry K. Fields and Warren H. Deering, Judges; No. C633835, Kurt J. Lewin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of the trial courts in these cases declaring Los Angeles City Charter section 112 unconstitutional are reversed. Burrell's "cross-appeal" seeking backpay during the pendency of this appeal is not properly before this court as it concerns a matter not decided by the trial court. Each party to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city challenged adverse judgments entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in suits by respondent employees challenging their termination, arguing that the trial court erred in concluding that respondents' due process rights were violated by the termination procedures.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city terminated the employment of respondents, who each brought suit challenging the termination as wrongful. In each suit, the trial court entered judgments favorable to the respondents. Petitioner sought appellate review, and the matters were consolidated before the appellate court. On review, the court noted that petitioner conceded that L.A., Cal., City Charter § 12 precluded the discharge of permanent employees except for written cause. As such, the issue for determination was whether the particular termination procedures employed satisfied due process requirements of a full and fair disciplinary hearing. In reversing the judgments for respondents, the appellate court concluded that respondents were afforded a hearing and an opportunity to refute the charges before an administrative committee. The appellate court rejected respondents' argument that a panel reviewing a departmental disciplinary decision must be able to override the department's decision of what it believes to be an appropriate penalty; rather, due process was satisfied where the reviewing body determined whether sufficient evidence upheld the findings of misconduct.


OUTCOME: The trial court judgments for respondent employees were reversed, as no due process violation occurred in the employment termination proceedings where respondents were afforded a hearing and opportunity to refuse the misconduct charges before an administrative committee and an opportunity to challenge the decision to a reviewing body, which determined whether the findings of misconduct were supported by sufficient evidence.

 

525 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Wrather Port Properties v. County of L.A.


No. B033091


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


209 Cal. App. 3d 517; 257 Cal. Rptr. 266; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 321



April 7, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C638180, Leon Savitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a city and county, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which ordered them to refund excess taxes collected on the basis of a revaluation of real property leased to respondent lessee.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city leased a ship and appurtenant real property to respondent lessee. At the time the lease was executed, 40 years was the maximum term permitted under appellant city's charter for the lease of appellant city's real property. Both parties were aware that a charter amendment would appear on the ballot one month later which, if approved, would extend the maximum term to 66 years. The parties provided in the lease that the term of the lease would be extended if the charter amendment was approved. Voters approved the charter amendment. Appellant county's tax assessor valued the lease three years later but revalued it two years later on the basis that the automatic increase in the lease's term from 40 to 66 years was a reappraisal event. Respondent was granted a refund of the additional taxes it paid. The judgment was affirmed because the automatic extension of the term of possession under the provision of the original lease did not constitute a second change in ownership within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2. Indeed, the evidence showed the assessor had from the beginning interpreted the parties' intent to be to create a 66-year lease, not a 40-year lease.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment ordering appellants, a city and county, to refund excess taxes collected from respondent lessee because the extension of the lease between respondent and appellant city did not constitute a change in ownership.

 

526 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Acuson Corp. v. Aloka Co.


No. H004601.


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


209 Cal. App. 3d 425; 257 Cal. Rptr. 368; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 317; 10 U.S.P.Q.2D (BNA) 1814



April 5, 1989  
April 5, 1989


NOTICE:   [**1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modification of Opinion on Denial of Rehearing May 3, 1989; Review denied June 22, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. Vi, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 639713, Leslie C. Nichols, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) granting a preliminary injunction preventing defendant from using any information it learned of plaintiff's product through its examination of the product.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the trial court granting plaintiff injunctive relief preventing defendant from using any information learned through its examination of plaintiff's product. The court reversed and held that the product had already been sold on the open market and was not a trade secret. The uncontradicted evidence showed that plaintiff sold the product to the public. In addition, the court rejected the contention that defendant breached or participated in a breach of the software licensing agreement because the preliminary injunction did not refer to the software and the license did not prevent a buyer from operating the machine, disassembling its hardware, studying its functions, and comparing it with other machines. Moreover, the internal padlocks could not protect the secrecy of a machine because it had been disclosed to the public and nothing that an examination of the machine discloses can be a trade secret. In addition, the law did not prohibit reverse engineering, but rather encouraged such efforts by giving to the competitor who invests substantial resources the opportunity to hold in legally protected confidence the results of its labor.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and held that the injunction was improper because the product had been sold in the open market, and thus, was not a trade secret. In addition, there was no evidence that defendant breached any agreement with plaintiff requiring it not to examine the product. Moreover, the law did not prohibit reverse engineering, but rather encouraged such efforts.

City of Glendale v. George


No. B032383


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


208 Cal. App. 3d 1394; 256 Cal. Rptr. 742; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 264



March 27, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NCC 19141G, Joseph R. Kalin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: (See fn. 8.) The order is affirmed as to paragraphs 3(a), 3(b), 3(c) and 3(f) of the underlying judgment, and reversed as to paragraphs 3(d) and 3(e) which are ordered stricken from the judgment. 8 Each side to bear its own costs on appeal.



8 A partial reversal is permissible in this case because the underlying judgment contains distinct and severable provisions. (Cf. Hasson v. Ford Motor Co. (1977) 19 Cal.3d 530, 552 [138 Cal.Rptr. 705, 564 P.2d 857, 99 A.L.R.3d 158]; In re Marriage of Steinberg (1977) 66 Cal.App.3d 815, 820 [136 Cal.Rptr. 299]; 9 Witkin, Cal. CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their motion to vacate a consent judgment that they had entered into with respondent city regarding the use of their residence as a "Santa's Village."


OVERVIEW: Appellant residents operated a "Santa's Village" from their home, which respondent city determined to be a nuisance. The parties entered into a consent judgment, which limited appellants' use of the property. Appellants filed a motion to set aside the consent judgment, claiming that it violated their rights under U.S. Const. amend. I to freedom of speech and association, which the trial court denied. The court initially determined that appellants were free to challenge the consent judgment because there was no clear and compelling evidence that appellants had waived their fundamental rights. The court affirmed the denial to vacate the judgment regarding restrictions on the decoration of appellants' residence and the time period during which they could be displayed because it found that the content neutral restrictions were reasonable. It reversed the denial regarding restrictions on the advertising of the residence, which it found was a prior restraint on speech, because it held that the restrictions were not narrowly drawn to protect respondent's interests. The court also struck down as vague the restriction on who could enter appellants' property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which denied appellant residents' motion to vacate a consent judgment they entered into with respondent city insofar as the judgment contained only time, place, and manner restrictions on appellants' First Amendment rights. The court reversed the order as to portions of the judgment, which constituted a content-based prior restriction on appellants' freedom of speech and that were vague.

 

528 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



ABC Plumbing & Heating Co. v. Vernon Savings & Loan Assn.


No. D007787


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


208 Cal. App. 3d 1370; 257 Cal. Rptr. 139; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 253



March 24, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 577472, Edward T. Butler, Judge. *


* Retired Associate Justice of the Court of Appeal sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. ABC awarded costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff subcontractor appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), dismissing a complaint for breach of contract, to foreclose a mechanic's lien, and to enforce a stop notice against defendant construction lender, for lack of subject matter jurisdiction and failure to exhaust administrative remedies.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff subcontractor filed a lawsuit against defendant construction lender on state law claims for breach of contract, to foreclose a mechanic's lien, and to enforce a stop notice against defendant. After the lawsuit was commenced, defendant was declared insolvent and the Federal Savings & Loan Insurance Corporation (FSLIC) was appointed receiver. The trial court dismissed the complaint for lack of jurisdiction. The court reversed. The court concluded that FSLIC did not have exclusive adjudicatory powers over creditors' claims, and that plaintiff could not be denied access to the state court for adjudication of its claims simply because defendant was declared insolvent after the state court assumed jurisdiction. The court held that plaintiff's claims did not involve loans, real estate transactions, and securities law issues in which the FSLIC had special expertise. The court concluded that plaintiff was not required to exhaust its administrative remedies because no adequate administrative remedy was available.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded because the Federal Savings & Loan Insurance Corporation (FSLIC) was not vested with exclusive jurisdiction over plaintiff subcontractor's state law claims arising from breach of contract against defendant construction lender after defendant was declared insolvent and FSLIC was appointed receiver. Exhaustion of administrative remedies was not required because no administrative remedy was available.

Guinnane v. San Francisco City Planning Com


No. A040760


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


209 Cal. App. 3d 732; 257 Cal. Rptr. 742; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 377



March 23, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied June 7, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 876131, Lucy Kelly McCabe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. 8



8 In light of our determination, we find it unnecessary to reach respondents' remaining arguments relating to the statute of limitations and exhaustion of administrative remedies.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which upheld the decision of respondents, the city planning commission and board of permit appeals, to deny appellant a building permit to construct a house that was not in character with the surrounding single family dwellings, as the proposed house was significantly larger than neighboring houses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owner sought a building permit for the construction of a home on four residential building lots. Respondents, the city planning commission and the board of permit appeals, denied appellant's application, on the basis that the house, significantly larger than the surrounding homes, was not in character with the neighborhood. Appellant sought review in the superior court, which affirmed respondents' denial of the permit. Appellant then challenged the judgment of the superior court. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that respondents were fully authorized to exercise independent discretion in deciding whether to approve the permit application under San Francisco Municipal Code part III, art. 1, § 26, and the San Francisco Charter, § 3.651. Further, the court concluded that such review was not confined to a determination whether the applicant had complied with the city's zoning ordinances and building codes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court, concluding that respondents, planning commission and board of permit appeals were authorized to exercise independent discretionary review of a building permit application, and that denial of the permit was supported by the evidence on the record.

1st Street Books v. Marin Community College Dist.


No. A040727.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four.


208 Cal. App. 3d 1275; 256 Cal. Rptr. 833; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 250



March 23, 1989 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted June 22, 1989 (S009983).
Trans. January 18, 1990 to Ct. App., 1st Dist., Div. 4, with directions to vacate its opn. and reconsider in light of chapter 1340, Statutes of 1989. The Injunction entered by Superior Court, County of Marin, on 10-27-87, is suspended during the pendency of the appeal. ( Code Civ. Proc., § 923.)
Subsequent opinion was not certified for Publication.
Review granted, Depublished by 1st Street Books v. Marin Community College Dist., 260 Cal. Rptr. 182, 775 P.2d 507, 1989 Cal. LEXIS 3824 (Cal., 1989)
Withdrawn by publisher


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant college and intervenor chain bookstore appealed the order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which granted summary judgment to plaintiffs, privately owned bookstores, and granted a permanent injunction that prohibited defendant and intervenor from operating a full scale bookstore on campus property. Defendant and intervenor claimed that Cal. Educ. Code § 81676 was unconstitutional as applied by the trial court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant college contracted with intervenor chain bookstore to manage its campus bookstores. The trial court granted summary judgment to plaintiffs, privately owned bookstores, on plaintiffs' request for a permanent injunction and restrained defendant and intervenor from selling any books, except textbooks necessary for college course work, because it was beyond defendant's authority to operate a full scale bookstore on campus under Cal. Educ. Code § 81676. The court affirmed order and held that § 81676 included language of limitation because it covered the sale of textbooks, not books in general, and the usual and ordinary meaning of textbooks was books used in the study of a given subject. Defendant and intervenor also argued that the trial court's application of § 81676 made them suppressors of U.S. Const. amend. I rights. The court held, however, that defendant's and intervenor's bookstores were not public forums in spite of being open to the public, thus, § 81676 as applied was not unconstitutional. Finally, the court held that injunctive relief was appropriate because it would be extremely difficult to ascertain the amount of compensation that would afford adequate relief.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs, privately owned bookstores, and the injunction issued against defendant college and intervenor chain bookstore because of the plain language of the statutory law and because campus bookstores were not public forums; thus, the statutory limitations placed upon the operation of the bookstores did not offend either state or federal constitutions.

 

531 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Acuff v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Bd.


No. C000334


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


208 Cal. App. 3d 1038; 256 Cal. Rptr. 513; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 217



March 17, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for review by the Supreme Court was denied June 1, 1989. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: We conclude there is no legal reason to disturb the Board's exercise of discretion in applying Unemployment Insurance Code section 1262 to the circumstances of this case. The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is denied. The alternative writ, having served its purpose, is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employees sought a writ of administrative mandate directing respondent Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board (California) to set aside the decision denying unemployment compensation benefits under Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1262. Petitioners contended that the trade dispute disqualification provision did not apply because they left work involuntarily after their employer implemented a wage reduction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employees sought a writ of administrative mandate directing respondent California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board to set aside its decision denying compensation benefits under Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1262, the trade dispute disqualification provision. The court denied the petition. The court found that petitioners were ineligible for benefits because they left work voluntarily over a trade dispute, a wage reduction implemented by their employer after their collective bargaining agreement expired. The court found that the wage reduction was an issue of the trade dispute expressly tendered in the negotiations that had reached an impasse. The court found that the implementation of the wage reduction did not sever the causal relationship between the dispute and petitioners' decision to leave work, and that petitioners went on strike because they chose to do so. The court held that petitioners' ineligibility for benefits did not conflict with federal and state guaranteed rights to organize and to engage in concerted activity for the purpose of collective bargaining.


OUTCOME: The court denied the request for a writ of mandate against respondent California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board. The court held that petitioner employees were disqualified from eligibility for compensation benefits during the period they were on strike, because they voluntarily left work over a trade dispute, a wage reduction implemented after their collective bargaining agreement expired.

Laborers' Int'l Union Etc v. El Dorado Landscape Co.

Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney challenged an order of respondent Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which imposed sanctions against him.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorney argued that respondent Superior Court of San Diego County's fast track rules to implement the Trial Court Reduction Act of 1986 (Act), Cal. The court affirmed because there was nothing in the record to show that appellant was not given notice and the right to a hearing before sanctions were imposed against him.

533 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



UA Communications v. City of Montclair


No. E005085


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


209 Cal. App. 3d 245; 257 Cal. Rptr. 124; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 302



March 10, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified March 31, 1989. Respondents' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 24, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. OCV39705, William P. Hyde, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the trial court is directed to enter judgment declaring Ordinance No. 86-630 unconstitutional as applied to plaintiffs and enjoining its enforcement with respect to them.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant theater corporations challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which entered declaratory and injunctive relief in favor of respondent city in appellants' action to test the constitutionality of a city admissions tax.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city passed an ordinance, Montclair Municipal Code § 3-5.504, that imposed a 6 percent tax on the price of an admission ticket to any event. Appellant theater corporations filed a complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief on the ground that the admissions tax impermissibly burdened their rights under the U.S. Const. amend. I without adequate justification. The trial court held that the tax was constitutional and entered judgment for respondent. On appeal, the court reversed because only a very small percentage of businesses in the city paid the tax and because 90 percent of the admissions tax burdened only four businesses. Here, appellants' uncontradicted evidence demonstrated that the tax was not targeted to all businesses but only to those engaged in protected speech. The court held that a statute challenged under U.S. Const. amend. I must be tested by its operation and effect and found that the burden of the tax fell disproportionately upon businesses engaged in protected speech.


OUTCOME: The court agreed with appellant theater corporations that an admissions tax imposed by respondent city was unconstitutional as violative of appellant's First Amendment rights to free speech because it fell disproportionately upon businesses engaged in protected speech. The court directed the trial court to declare the city ordinance unconstitutional.

 

534 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Schopflin v. Dole


No. A041589.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four.


208 Cal. App. 3d 617; 256 Cal. Rptr. 314; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 395



March 7, 1989 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Request for an order directing transfer denied May 18, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art IV, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 159508, Laurence K. Sawyer, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county controller challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California) that declared a statutory initiative concerning local tax ordinances to be unconstitutional for violation of Cal. Const. art. II, § 9(a), arguing that the proposition was a valid initiative authorized by Cal. Const. art. II, § 8, rather than an invalid referendum.


OVERVIEW: County voters approved Proposition 62, a statutory initiative providing that a local ordinance imposing general taxes after the proposition's effective date was valid only if adopted by a two-thirds vote of the legislative body. Appellee county brought suit seeking a declaration that the proposition was unconstitutional for violating the referendum provisions of Cal. Const. art. II, § 9(a). The trial court entered judgment declaring the initiative unconstitutional, and appellant county controller sought review, arguing that the voter approval requirement for local tax enactments was an initiative authorized by Cal. Const. art. II, § 8, rather than an invalid referendum. In affirming, the appellate court concluded that the future tax provisions amounted to a constitutionally proscribed referendum. Referendums were directed to enacted statutes or ordinances that had not yet taken effect, and the state constitution expressly provided that the referendum powers reserved to the people did not extend to tax levies. County voters had no referendum power to repeal a tax ordinance, and they could not do so indirectly by labeling their attempt an initiative rather than a referendum.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed, as the state constitution mandated that referendum powers reserved to the people did not extend to tax levies. Where the statutory initiative affected the validity of recently enacted local taxing ordinances, it was a constitutionally proscribed referendum and thus invalid.

Chico Feminist Women's Health Center v. Scully


No. C000584


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


208 Cal. App. 3d 230; 256 Cal. Rptr. 194; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 156



February 28, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified March 3, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 89695, Loyd H. Mulkey, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: As modified above, the order of April 25, 1986, is affirmed. To the extent defendants attempt to appeal from the February 27, 1986, order the appeal is dismissed. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff health center sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Butte County (California), denying its motion to amend a preliminary injunction by obtaining an additional injunctive order to enjoin all picketing activity by defendants anti-abortion protesters on Saturdays when abortions were performed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted plaintiff health center a preliminary injunction against defendant anti-abortion citizen protesters. One of plaintiff's clients was recognized by a defendant who revealed her identity and purpose. As a result, plaintiff filed a motion to amend the preliminary injunction seeking an additional injunctive order enjoining defendants from picketing from any vantage point on Saturdays, when abortions were performed. Plaintiff claimed defendants were violating its clients right to privacy. The trial court refused the requested order but amended the preliminary injunction by enjoining defendants from identifying or disclosing the identity of anyone entering or leaving the center. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court judgment, holding that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in refusing to enter the injunctive order because plaintiff's claim of privacy was based on mere recognition on public streets and its clients had no reasonable expectation of anonymity on public sidewalks and streets. The court held that plaintiff failed to show that defendants had ample alternatives for communicating to their target audience in a public forum.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court order that refused plaintiff health center's request to enjoin defendant anti-abortion protesters from picketing on Saturdays because plaintiff's clients had no reasonable expectation of anonymity on public streets. The court modified the trial court injunction to more certainly identify those it applied to, and dismissed defendant's challenge to the original preliminary injunction as an untimely appeal.

Conservatorship of Jones


No. D007568


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


208 Cal. App. 3d 292; 256 Cal. Rptr. 415; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 148



February 28, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied May 17, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. MH73405, J. Perry Langford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) which denied his request to end his conservatorship pursuant to the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code. § 5000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Before appellant was released from prison, a conservatorship was established and he was placed at a state hospital. The trial court granted his conservator's request to renew the conservatorship under the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act (LPS Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000 et seq. Appellant's petition for rehearing to terminate the conservatorship was denied, and he sought review, alleging that corrections custody qualified as a third party assistance and that the trial court improperly considered his future disability. The court affirmed. After reviewing statutes governing parolees and the purpose of the LPS Act, it found no rationale in the act or in case law to justify casting corrections in the third party assistance role suggested by appellant. It held that the trial court had not relied on the possibility of future disability in denying the request to end the conservatorship.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying appellant's request to terminate a conservatorship because the trial court properly found that the corrections department did not qualify as a responsible third person providing for appellant's needs under the involuntary commitment act and appellant advanced no other grounds for terminating the commitment.

People v. Snyder


No. H003890


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


208 Cal. App. 3d 1141; 256 Cal. Rptr. 601; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 245



February 28, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 115118, Robert Michael Foley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant inmate appealed from a judgment in the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that convicted him of escape with force and violence in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4532(b), alleging his right to a jury trial was violated and that the trial court abused its discretion in rejecting his guilty plea.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant inmate of escape with force and violence in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4532(b). Defendant appealed, alleging his right to a jury trial was violated because the information, jury instructions, and verdict form did not present force as an element of forcible escape but as a separate allegation to be found true. He claimed because that procedure required the jury to render a special verdict from which the trial court drew the legal conclusion of guilt, it violated his right to a general jury verdict of guilt. The court affirmed, holding that by finding defendant guilty of simple escape and that he used force, the jury applied legal principles to the facts, leaving the court to make operative the jury's verdict. Thus, there was no interference with defendant's constitutional right to a jury trial. Defendant also argued that the trial court erred in refusing to accept his Alford plea. The court noted that defendant made statements to the court raising the question of whether any plea would be voluntary; therefore, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in refusing to accept the plea.


OUTCOME: Defendant inmate's conviction for escape with force and violence was affirmed because his right to a jury trial was not violated and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in rejecting his Alford plea.

 

538 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Redwood Empire Publ'g Co. v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. A038555


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


207 Cal. App. 3d 1334; 255 Cal. Rptr. 514; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 111; 16 Media L. Rep. 1257



February 16, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 834325, Frank W. Shaw, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, publisher and printers, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), that upheld respondent State Board of Equalization's denial of appellants' refund for sales tax under the Sales and Use Tax Law, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6362, and rejected appellants' U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV attack on Cal. Code Regs. tit. 18, § 1590 (1975) (formerly Cal. Administrative Code tit. 18, § 1590).


OVERVIEW: After exhausting their administrative remedies before respondent State Board of Equalization, appellant, publisher and printers, sought a refund of sales tax collected on printing charges on grounds that appellant publisher's advertising guide was a newspaper or periodical under the Sales and Use Tax Law, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6362 and that Cal. Code Regs. tit. 18, § 1590 (1975) (formerly Cal. Administrative Code tit. 18, § 1590), which denied the exemption to publications devoted primarily to advertising, violated U.S. Const. amend I and XIV. The trial court upheld the denial of the refund, and rejected the constitutional attacks on the validity of § 1590. Appellants sought review. The appellate court found that the distinction in § 1590 was content-neutral. Moreover, commercial speech was accorded less protection than noncommercial speech. The appellate court affirmed the trial court judgment because respondent was permitted to treat commercial speech differently than noncommercial speech in § 1590 and the differential tax treatment accorded advertising guides was rationally related to a legitimate state interest and did not violate equal protection of the law.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment because respondent State Board of Equalization's denial of a sales tax refund to appellant, publisher and printers, was not an impermissible infringement on First Amendment liberties and the differential tax treatment accorded advertising guides was rationally related to a legitimate state interest and did not violate equal protection of the law.

 

539 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Danielle W.


No. B029922


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


207 Cal. App. 3d 1227; 255 Cal. Rptr. 344; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 101



February 10, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied May 3, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. J963877, Theodore D. Piatt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which delegated all control over visitation of appellant's minor children to respondent Los Angeles County Department of Children's Services.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother challenged the propriety of the juvenile court's order, which delegated all visitation control of her minor children in the care of respondent Los Angeles County Department of Children's Services (Department). Appellant argued that the order constituted a delegation of judicial power in violation of Cal. Const. art. III, § 3, that the order denied appellant due process, and that the order was not supported by substantial evidence. The court affirmed and held that respondent, which was acting as the probation officer, acted as an arm of a court in the best interests of the minors. The court held that a nonjudicial board or officer may be authorized to perform quasi-judicial powers to determine facts and exercise discretion. The court held that there was no due process violation because respondent was charged with the burden of representing the state in seeking the best interests of the children. The court held that there was ample evidence to support the order because the children felt neglected, unloved, and angry at appellant. The court held that forced contact would not be beneficial to the children.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order of visitation and delegating all control of appellant mother's minor children to respondent Los Angeles County Department of Children's Services (Department). The court held that appellant's due process rights were not violated because the order did not constitute punishment of appellant but was protection of the minor children's psychological well being.

 

540 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Fineberg v. Harney & Moore


No. B032887


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


207 Cal. App. 3d 1049; 255 Cal. Rptr. 299; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 87



February 7, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 3, 1989, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 26, 1989. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 585214, Robert Fainer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Respondents are to bear costs on this appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff client appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which rendered a judgment in favor of defendant attorney and held plaintiff waived the protection of the Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6146, in an action under § 6146 to recover excess contingent fees paid pursuant to a contingent fee agreement.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff client retained defendant attorney to institute a medical malpractice action and the parties executed a contingent fee agreement. As a requirement for defendant's representation, plaintiff waived the protection of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6146, which placed a limit on contingency fee agreements. After the malpractice suit was settled and costs deducted, defendant took 40 percent pursuant to the agreement and refused plaintiff's request for a refund of the fee that exceeded that permitted under § 6146. Plaintiff filed a suit against defendant for violation of § 6146. Defendant argued that the prescribed fee limitations precluded retention by injured persons of adequate counsel because it was not economically feasible. The trial court held plaintiff had waived protection of the statute. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and held that plaintiff had not and could not waive protection of § 6146 because such waiver was precluded by Cal. Civ. Code § 3513, whereby the legislature precluded contingency fee agreements providing for rates in excess of the limits set in the statute. The court found no deprivation of the right to counsel by reason of the statutory provision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendant attorney, and held that plaintiff client was entitled to recover excess fees paid because he was prohibited by statute from waiving the provisions of the statute governing contingency agreements.

 

541 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



City of Stanton v. Cox


No. G005603


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


207 Cal. App. 3d 1557; 255 Cal. Rptr. 682; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 131



January 31, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 454543, Leonard H. McBride, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. Respondents are entitled to costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), granted in favor of defendant bookstore and shopping center manager that denied plaintiff's motion to amend and request to permanently enjoin operation of bookstore's business which sold sexually explicit literature and materials. Plaintiff contended that the court abused its discretion in not permitting the amendment and incorrectly applied the law.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city sought review of a judgment granted in favor of defendant bookstore and shopping center manager that denied plaintiff's motion to amend and request to permanently enjoin operation of bookstore's business which sold sexually explicit literature and materials. Plaintiff contended that the court abused its discretion in not permitting the amendment and incorrectly applied the law. The court on review found that a party who waited 18 months before attempting to amend, and then did so only after trial had commenced, and who offered no excuse for the failure, could hardly complain when the request to amend was denied. The court held that it could not say that trial judge abused his discretion. The court also held that the trial court correctly determined that the Renton test had to be applied to plaintiff's zoning scheme. The court concluded that given the standard the trial court had to apply to the evidence, the court could not disagree with the findings. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment granted in favor of defendant bookstore and shopping center manager that denied plaintiff city's motion to amend and request to permanently enjoin operation of bookstore's business was affirmed. The court held that it could not say that trial judge abused his discretion or that it disagreed with the trial court's findings given the standard the trial court applied to the evidence.

 

542 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Bryte v. City of La Mesa


No. D007764


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


207 Cal. App. 3d 687; 255 Cal. Rptr. 64; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 62



January 27, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 17, 1989 and February 22, 1989, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied May 18, 1989. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 572803, Edward T. Butler, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The judgment holding the statute to be constitutional is reversed. The case is remanded with instructions to the trial court to enter judgment holding the statute unconstitutional and for such further proceedings as may be consistent with this opinion. On remand, the superior court may entertain motions for costs or other ancillary relief as may be consistent with our determination that the plaintiff should prevail in her assertion of unconstitutionality of the statute.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mental patient sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of San Diego (California), which held that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 8102 was constitutional. The trial court dismissed plaintiff's constitutional claim after entering an order for the return of plaintiff's weapons that were confiscated by defendant police department pursuant to the statute.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff mental patient filed an action against defendant police department in which she sought to regain weapons confiscated pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 8102. Plaintiff also sought to have the statute declared unconstitutional. The trial court ordered the return of plaintiff's property and dismissed plaintiff's constitutional claim. Upon review, the court reversed. The court held that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 8102 was unconstitutional because it did not provide plaintiff with a sufficient remedy to regain possession of her property to satisfy due process requirements under U.S. Const. amend. XIV; Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. The court reasoned that the statute did not contain the proper provisions for a hearing because it failed to provide adequate notice of time and place. The court found the statute's provision that provided a property owner could retain his or her property by filing an action in a superior court was not sufficient notice of a hearing because it required the owner to institute the action rather than the department. The court held that a statute permitting peremptory seizure of property required a provision within its own ambit for administrative review.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that held that a statute that confiscated weapons for detained mental patients was constitutional. The court held that because the statute did not provide plaintiff mental patient with a hearing for her to regain possession of her property back from defendant police department it violated her due process rights, and was unconstitutional.

 

543 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Azzarella


No. E004149


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


207 Cal. App. 3d 1240; 254 Cal. Rptr. 922; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 80



January 27, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for review by the Supreme Court was denied May 18, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. M-15009, Robert J. Timlin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent county appealed the grant of a petition for writ of habeas corpus in favor of petitioner mental patient in the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), in an action in which petitioner was certified under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5250.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner mental patient was brought to a hospital for detention and treatment and was certified for an additional 14 days pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5250. Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus for judicial review. Respondent county filed a motion for petitioner to assume the burden of proof or for a reduction of the burden of proof standard. The trial court decided that respondent had the burden of justifying certification by clear and convincing evidence. The trial court released petitioner after granting his petition. On appeal, respondent contended that the trial court erred in its application of the burden of proof. The court affirmed the trial court's decision but stated that the burden of proof, by a preponderance of the evidence, was the correct standard in evaluation of the lawfulness of detention in a habeas corpus proceeding. The burden rested on respondent. Although the trial court utilized the stricter standard of proof, the correct standard would not have resulted in a different conclusion. Respondent failed to present any evidence that, as a result of petitioner's mental disorder, he was incapacitated or unable to provide for his basic needs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court because respondent county failed to show, by a preponderance of the evidence, that the disorder of petitioner mental patient rendered him unable to carry out transactions necessary for survival.

 

544 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Okrand v. City of L.A.


No. B026035


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


207 Cal. App. 3d 566; 254 Cal. Rptr. 913; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 48



January 26, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied May 18, 1989. Kennard, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C577925, Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that the display of an unlit menorah in the city hall rotunda was constitutional and granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, city and administrator, and dismissed the lawsuit filed by appellant for injunctive and declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, city and administrator, allowed an unlit menorah to be displayed in the city hall rotunda near a decorated Christmas tree, and appellant taxpayer filed a lawsuit for injunctive and declaratory relief. The trial court granted respondents' summary judgment motion and dismissed the matter. The court affirmed because under the three-part test, the display did not violate the establishment clause of U.S. Const. amend. I. The court found that the display had a secular purpose of providing cultural and educational development, did not have an inherent religious significance like other objects, and did not show excessive entanglement between religion and government in the context of the rotunda's regular use for historical, cultural, and artistic displays. The court found that the display did not violate the state constitution under the "preference" test because there was no lack of comparable recognition of other religious symbols, and the menorah did not focus attention of a religious doctrine or portray a central tenet of religion. The court found that the unlit menorah was more of a museum piece than a religious symbol.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, city and administrator, because the display of an unlit menorah in the city hall rotunda had a secular purpose that did not advance or inhibit religion, nor create entanglement of respondent city with religion, and, therefore, did not violate the establishment clauses of the federal or state constitutions.

Cal. Trout v. State Water Res. Control Bd.


No. C000713


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


207 Cal. App. 3d 585; 255 Cal. Rptr. 184; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 63



January 26, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 22, 1989, and the petition of real parties in interest for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 26, 1989. Lucas, C. J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 336712 and 336715, Lloyd Allan Phillips, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of dismissal of the petitions for writs of mandate are reversed. The trial court shall issue appropriate writs, commanding the Water Board to exercise its ministerial duty to attach the conditions required by section 5946 to licenses 10191 and 10192 as that section is construed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff environmental interest groups appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) which dismissed petitions for writs of mandate against defendant State Water Resources Control Board to rescind two licenses, issued respondents, city and its department or power and water, which confirmed rights to the appropriation of water.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff environmental interest groups challenged the denial of their petitions for writs of mandate against defendant State Water Resources Control Board regarding two licenses it issued to respondents, city its department of water and power, which confirmed rights to the appropriation of water. The court reversed the denial of the writs of mandate and held that Cal. Fish & Game Code § 5946 was a specific legislative rule concerning the public trust and defendant had no authority to disregard that rule. The court held that the provisions of § 5946 which applied to a license for the appropriation of water by means of diversion from dams was limited only by the date of issuance of the license, and that a licensee did not gain right to the appropriation of water unless it was able to put the full amount of authorized water to beneficial use, as required by water law. The court held that the legislature had the constitutional power to make reasonable determinations of the priority of water uses, and that § 5946 applied to licenses which authorized the appropriation of all of the available water from a stream.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of dismissal of the petitions for writs of mandate. The court ordered the trial court to issue appropriate writs against defendant State Water Resources Control Board, commanding defendant to exercise its ministerial duty to attach the statutory conditions required to the licenses at issue, because defendant had no authority to disregard a specific legislative rule concerning the public trust.

 

546 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



N. Bay Reg'l Ctr. v. Sherry S.


No. A039381


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


207 Cal. App. 3d 449; 256 Cal. Rptr. 129; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 50



January 25, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 26, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Napa County, No. M-87182, Herbert W. Walker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that the order appealed from is flawed for failure to comply with the prescribed procedure for admission to a state hospital of a nondangerous, nonobjecting, developmentally disabled adult. On remand this procedure must be followed or a record must be developed demonstrating that the procedure is inadequate. In the meantime it is not necessary that Sherry be immediately released from the state hospital. (See In re Hop, supra, 29 Cal.3d at p. 94.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mentally disabled adult challenged the order of the Superior Court of Napa County (California), which granted the petition of respondent regional center to admit appellant into a state hospital although she was not represented by a parent, guardian or conservator.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted respondent regional center's petition to admit appellant mentally disabled adult into a state hospital over the objection of appellant's attorney that respondent was not authorized by statute to bring such a petition. Although the case was moot by the time it reached the court on appeal, the court chose to hear the matter because the case presented a significant issue that was likely to arise again. The court reversed the trial court's order, holding that respondent had not followed the proper procedure. The court reviewed Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 4825, 4653, and 4803 and determined that a nonconsenting developmentally disabled adult could be admitted to a state hospital on the application of a parent or conservator, combined with a referral from a regional center and a certificate of nonobjection. If no parent or conservator was available for this purpose, the Director of Developmental Services could be appointed conservator and should delegate to the regional center his or her duties to admit the conservatee.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, holding that the required procedure for admission to a state hospital of a nondangerous, nonobjecting, developmentally disabled adult who was not represented by a parent, guardian, or conservator was to appoint the Director of Developmental Services as guardian or conservator who could delegate to the regional center his or her duties to admit the conservatee to a state hospital.

 

547 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Perez


No. B031537


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


207 Cal. App. 3d 431; 254 Cal. Rptr. 869; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 37



January 24, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A796995, David A. Workman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his judgment of conviction entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), for vehicular burglary under Cal. Penal Code § 459, asserting that the trial court erred in admitting testimony from his first trial when the witness was unavailable.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted, during his second trial, of vehicular burglary under Cal. Penal Code § 459. Defendant challenged his conviction and asserted that the reading of the car owner's testimony to the jury was error because the prosecution failed to sustain its burden to show due diligence in procuring her presence at trial. On appeal the court affirmed the conviction and held that Cal. Penal Code § 1328d specifically provided for service of process by mail and that having the witness on-call was proper under Cal. Penal Code § 1331.5. The court found that when the witness was unavailable the lower court was not required to consider a continuance because the provisions of § 1328d concerning such a continuance are triggered only upon the request of a party, and the prosecution did not request a continuance. The court held that the trial court did not abuse it's discretion and that the prosecution met its burden to show the exercise of due diligence to obtain the witnesses presence at trial.


OUTCOME: The court upheld defendant's conviction for vehicular burglary because the testimony of a witness from defendant's first trial, read to the jury because of her unavailability, was proper. The court found that service of process on the witness by mail and having her on-call to testify met the prosecutions requirement of due diligence.

People v. Valencia


No. G005001


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


207 Cal. App. 3d 1042; 255 Cal. Rptr. 180; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 95



January 23, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-62184, Luis A. Cardenas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which sentenced defendant to two years for robbery and to two consecutive five-year terms for prior serious felony convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to robbery and was sentenced to two years on the robbery conviction and two consecutive five-year terms for prior serious felony convictions. Defendant challenged the validity of the enhancement to his sentence for robbery based on the prior convictions. On appeal the court affirmed the sentence and held that Cal. Penal Code § 1385(b) did not unconstitutionally infringe upon the power of the judiciary by prohibiting the striking of prior Cal. Penal Code § 667 felonies. The court held that § 1385(b) did not violates the spirit of the Determinate Sentencing Act by imposing a sentence disproportionate to the crime because the actual sentence imposed for the armed robbery charge was the low term of two years. The court found that charging defendant with, and sentencing him under, the more severe of two overlapping enhancement statutes did not violate his equal protection rights. The court found that defendant did not receive a longer sentence than his codefendant because the claimed disproportionality arose from application of the recidivist statutes and not from any decision based on relative culpability for the robbery offense.


OUTCOME: The court upheld defendant's sentence because the enhancement provisions for previous serious felony convictions were not unconstitutional and did not impose on defendant a sentence disproportionate to the crime or to the sentence received by his codefendant.
