Green v. Mt. Diablo Hospital Dist.


No. A039971


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


207 Cal. App. 3d 63; 254 Cal. Rptr. 689; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 12



January 11, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of appellant Green for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 29, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 295777, Ellen Sickles James, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), that denied his petition to compel arbitration of disputes arising from an employment contract and a buy-out agreement between himself and appellant hospital.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee and the old board of hospital directors entered into a contract that required immediate termination of appellant employee and provided for payments to appellant employee. The contract contained an arbitration clause and provided for attorney fees to the prevailing party. Subsequently, appellant new board voted to rescind that agreement, and appellant employee filed suit. Respondent taxpayers filed a complaint for declaratory relief, that original contract expired and that the amendment to the contract and the buyout agreement were void and unconstitutional. Appellant employee filed a petition to compel arbitration. The trial court denied the petition pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1281.2(b), (c). On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that respondents' allegations of appellant employee's violations of the Cal. Health & Safety Code, the Brown Act, and the California Constitution went to the making the contract and that if the contract was illegal the arbitration clause was unenforceable. The court disagreed with appellant employee's argument that there was contradicted evidence, which prevented the court from making such a finding.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment finding that arbitration could not be compelled where respondent taxpayers asserted contracts between appellant employee and hospital were void for illegality because the entire contract would then fail including the arbitration and attorney fees provision. The court further concluded there had been no final determination regarding the rights of the parties.

 

550 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Sweet


No. B037255


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


207 Cal. App. 3d 78; 254 Cal. Rptr. 567; 1989 Cal. App. LEXIS 9



January 11, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 19, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Santa Barbara Judicial District of Santa Barbara County, Nos. 279930 and 278440, Joseph Lodge, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders appealed from are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, who were each convicted of drunk driving under Cal. Veh. Code § 23152 in the Municipal Court for the Santa Barbara Judicial District of Santa Barbara County (California), challenged their enhanced sentences, arguing that the use of their prior convictions within the past seven years under Cal. Veh. Code §§ 23165, 23170, and 23175, rather than the past five years under former Cal. Veh. Code § 23102(d), was barred.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the trial court's order enhancing the sentences of appellants, who were each convicted of drunk driving under Cal. Veh. Code § 23152. Noting that the last time appellants pleaded guilty to drunk driving, Cal. Veh. Code § 23102(d) provided for enhanced punishment of a person previously convicted of drunk driving within the past five years, the court held that Cal. Veh. Code §§ 23165, 23170, and 23175, extending the period to seven years, were not ex post facto laws. All three statutes, it noted, were in force before appellants' present convictions and apprised them of the consequences of a new violation. It also rejected their arguments that that the prior convictions expired once the five years passed, that their earlier plea bargains amounted to a promise that the convictions would be used for only five years, and that their not being informed that the law could change violated their due process rights. Finally, it rejected appellants' arguments that the seven-year statutes were being applied retroactively, finding that this would be the case only if the statutes had been enacted after their offenses and before sentencing.


OUTCOME: Finding that enhancement statutes enacted after appellants' previous drunk driving offenses but before their present offenses were not ex post facto laws, the court affirmed the trial court's order enhancing their sentences, also rejecting appellants' arguments that their due process rights were violated, that the statutes had been applied retroactively, that their plea bargains had been violated, and that their prior offenses had expired.

 

551 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Axness v. Superior Court


No. A040921


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


206 Cal. App. 3d 1489; 255 Cal. Rptr. 896; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1230



December 30, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real party in interest Department of Motor Vehicles for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 16, 1989. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 294037, Ellen Sickles James, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the petition is reversed, and the trial court is directed to issue a writ of mandate commanding the department to rescind its order suspending appellant's driver's license.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which denied his petition for writ of mandate to prevent the Department of Motor Vehicles from suspending his driver's license based on a second out of state conviction for driving under the influence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged a judgment denying his petition for writ of mandate to prevent the California Department of Motor Vehicles (department) from suspending his driver's license based on two convictions for driving under the influence. Appellant claimed that the second conviction, which occurred in Minnesota, was unconstitutional because the Minnesota record was silent with respect to appellant's express waiver of his constitutional right of confrontation. The court found that such a waiver could not be presumed from a silent record. The court therefore reversed the judgment and directed the trial court to issue a writ of mandate commanding the department to rescind its suspension order. The court further explained that before the department may suspend appellant's driving privileges on the basis of an out-of-state conviction, appellant must be allowed to challenge the constitutionality of the conviction.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the court directed the trial court to issue a writ of mandate to rescind the order suspending appellant's driver's license. The court determined that before appellant's driving privileges could be suspended for two driving under the influence convictions, appellant must be allowed to challenge the constitutionality of one of the convictions, which occurred out of state.

 

552 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Lesara


No. F005447


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


206 Cal. App. 3d 1304; 254 Cal. Rptr. 417; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1218



December 28, 1988 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of the Facts and parts I, III and IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 20, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 27479, Henry E. Bianchi, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed. The matter is remanded to the trial court for resentencing in its entirety in accordance with our analysis and conclusions set forth in parts III and IV of this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction on numerous charges including oral copulation by force, rape, and sodomy in the Superior Court of Kern County (California), alleging the application of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 198(b), requiring jurors to have sufficient knowledge of the English language, deprived him his right to an impartial jury under Cal. Const. art. I, § 16.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted appellant of numerous charges including rape, oral copulation by force, sodomy, and assault by means of force. Appellant sought review, alleging the application of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code 198(b), requiring jurors to have sufficient knowledge of the English language, deprived him his right to an impartial jury under Cal. Const. art. I, § 16, in that his jury was not one drawn from a representative cross-section of the community. The court disagreed and affirmed the convictions. The court noted that to prove deprivation of an impartial jury, appellant was required to show that non-English-speaking citizens were a distinctive group in the community, but that appellant failed to provide any proof for that assertion. There was no showing that non-English-speaking citizens acted as a class or shared a common perspective. Furthermore, appellant's assertion that such jurors should not be excluded and should have been provided with personal interpreters placed too heavy a burden on the court and on other jurors.


OUTCOME: Appellant's convictions were affirmed because the statute at issue requiring the exclusion of potential jurors who did not possess sufficient knowledge of English was not unconstitutional, and appellant was not denied his right to a jury comprising a representative cross-section of the community. The matter was remanded for resentencing in its entirety.

 

553 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Adamson v. Dep't of Social Services


No. A041273


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


207 Cal. App. 3d 14; 254 Cal. Rptr. 667; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1236



December 27, 1988 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part II.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied January 18, 1989, and appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 16, 1989. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 322747, Allan J. Bollhoffer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant child day care center owner sought review from a Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) decision that denied appellant's Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5 writ of administrative mandate petition that challenged respondent Department of Social Services' revocation of appellant's license to operate a child day care center after children were allegedly molested on and off the premises of appellant's child day care center.


OVERVIEW: Appellant day care center owner challenged a trial court's denial of appellant's Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5 writ of administrative mandate petition that challenged respondent Department of Social Services' (Department) revocation of appellant's child day care center license. License revocation was based on alleged child molestation both on and off the day care center premises. The court affirmed the trial court decision. Based on the competent testimony of a child witness, there was substantial evidence that molestation occurred on-site. The Department was authorized to revoke appellant's license on the basis of child molestation accusations that did not occur on-site because there was substantial evidence that misconduct was related to the operation or maintenance of a child day care center. Such alleged off-site misconduct was inimical to the health, morals, welfare, or safety of an individual who received services for the child day care center. The alleged off-site molestation was relevant to the issue of license revocation. Revocation of appellant's license did not improperly interfere with appellant's right to marry a suspected child molester under the rational basis test.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a trial court's denial of appellant child day care center owner's writ of administrative mandate petition. Respondent Department of Social Services' revocation of appellant's child day care license was properly based on allegations of both on-site and off-site molestation. Pursuant to the rational basis test, respondent's revocation of appellant's license did not improperly interfere with her marriage to alleged child molester.

 

554 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Centennial Ins. Co. v. Murat


No. B033361.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


206 Cal. App. 3d 1199; 253 Cal. Rptr. 914; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1203



December 23, 1988 


NOTICE:  [**1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As modified January 20, 1989; Review denied March 16, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. IV, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C651692, Dzintra Janavs, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insured challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), prohibiting appellant's attorney from representing her in a certain capacity. The court initially dismissed appellant's appeal. The California Supreme Court granted appellant's petition for review and then transferred the cause to the court with directions to vacate the order dismissing the appeal.


OVERVIEW: Respondent insurance company filed an action for declaratory relief alleging that appellant insured's continued employment of inexperienced counsel to represent her in an underlying action was a breach of the implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing and her duty to cooperate with her insurer. The trial court held in favor of respondent, and appellant sought review. The court held that an order, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 2860(c), that terminated counsel's participation as counsel paid by the insurer was directly appealable as the equivalent of an injunction disqualifying current counsel from acting in a Cumis capacity. The court further held that the provisions of Cal. Civ. Code § 2860 were not unconstitutional on due process, equal protection or separation of powers grounds. Cal. Civ. Code § 2860(c) applied to Cumis counsel retained by an insured prior to the effective date of § 2860(c) because it was a codification of existing law and was procedural in nature.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court that prohibited appellant's attorney from representing her in a certain capacity. The court vacated its own order granting a stay of the trial court's order. Respondent was to recover its costs on appeal. The court held that the trial court's order was directly appealable and that the statutory section at issue could be applied to cases pending prior to its effective date.

 

555 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Samaro


No. H003350.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One.


206 Cal. App. 3d 1131; 254 Cal. Rptr. 128; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1210



December 22, 1988 


NOTICE:  [**1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied March 30, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 110372, Lawrence F. Terry, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied his motion to suppress filed pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 1538.5. Defendant pled guilty to possession of a concealable firearm by a convicted felon, possession of cocaine, possession of phencyclidine for sale, and a prior prison term.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of a concealable firearm by a convicted felon, various drug offenses, and a prior prison term. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, which the trial court denied. Defendant appealed the denial of his motion to suppress, arguing that the warrantless search of his car was not a lawful parole search. The court reversed and held that the officer possessed reasonable suspicion to conduct the parole search. However, defendant was entitled to suppression of the evidence seized because the officer did not possess probable cause to believe that contraband or evidence of a crime would be found in defendant's vehicle trunk. Moreover, the court found that because the vehicle was already impounded, the officer had ample time to attempt to secure a warrant, but he chose to rely on the parole condition as his authority for the search. Further, the prosecution failed to establish that the search was necessary for effective parole supervision, that it was related to legitimate parole administration rather than ordinary law enforcement, or even that the parole agent had prior notice of the search.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of defendant's motion to suppress. The court held that reasonable suspicion existed to justify a parole search, but the officer did not possess probable cause to justify the search of defendant's trunk. Further, the court found that neither the good faith exception to the exclusionary rule nor the inevitable discovery doctrine applied.

 

556 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Wilcox v. Ford


No. B029677


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


206 Cal. App. 3d 1170; 254 Cal. Rptr. 138; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1193



December 22, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 402774, Paul A. Turner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondent to recover costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), granting a dismissal in favor of respondent for failure to have brought an action to trial with five years as required under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 583.310.


OVERVIEW: Appellant filed a complaint after being involved in an automobile accident that occurred on May 9, 1981. The complaint was filed March 5, 1982. The matter was arbitrated, resulting in an award to appellant. Respondent moved for a trial de novo and the case was set for trial on July 25, 1986. On that date, respondent's attorney appeared and, at appellant's behest, sought to continue the trial to November 14, 1986. Respondent's attorney was to give notice to appellant of the new trial date but neglected to do so. Neither attorney appeared for trial and respondent moved for dismissal under the five-year statute. Appellant sought relief under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 473 on grounds of surprise, inadvertence and excusable neglect, as well as on grounds of estoppel and waiver. The trial court granted respondent's motion for dismissal. A motion for reconsideration was denied. The court affirmed the dismissal, holding that failure to monitor dates crucial to his lawsuit did not constitute a cause beyond his control so as to trigger application of the impossible, impracticable, or futile exceptions of § 473.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal because the failure to appear at trial or know the date of trial constituted lack of diligence, did not involve circumstances beyond appellant's control, and did not rise to the level of impossibility, impracticability, or futility.

 

557 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



County of Humboldt v. Harris


No. A040799


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


206 Cal. App. 3d 857; 254 Cal. Rptr. 49; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1183



December 19, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Humboldt County, No. 07703, John E. Buffington, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed. Respondent is to bear costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Humboldt County (California), which granted respondent nonresident's motion to quash service of process in a paternity action.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county brought a paternity action against respondent who was a resident of Nevada. Respondent nonresident made a special appearance by moving to quash the service of summons on the grounds of lack of personal jurisdiction and inconvenient forum. The trial court granted the motion, holding that the suit should be commenced in Nevada. Appellant contended the trial court erred inasmuch as the conception, birth, and rearing of the minor in California constituted sufficient grounds to maintain personal jurisdiction over respondent pursuant to the specific statutory authority found in Cal. Civ. Code § 7007 and under general due process principles. The court reversed the judgment, finding that section 7007 satisfied due process requirements and lawfully subjected respondent to the jurisdiction of California. The court found that respondent's sexual act resulting in the birth of a child imposed a substantial burden upon the mother and upon the state because the mother was impecunious. Therefore, the court held that it was reasonable the state should exercise judicial jurisdiction over respondent.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment that granted respondent nonresident's motion to quash service of process in a paternity action. The court held that pursuant to statute, the conception, birth, and rearing of a minor child in the jurisdiction provided sufficient due process grounds to maintain personal jurisdiction over respondent.

Baskin v. City of Berkeley Rent Stabilization Bd.


No. A037728.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


206 Cal. App. 3d 708; 253 Cal. Rptr. 791; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1173



December 16, 1988 


NOTICE:  [**1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted March 16, 1989 (S008743).
Pursuant to Rule 29.4(c), California Rules of Court, the above-entitled review is DISMISSED and cause remanded to the Court of Appeal, First Appellate District, Division One January 31, 1990


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 608998-2, Henry Ramsey, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlord challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied his writ of administrative mandamus and affirmed respondent board's decision to lower the tenants' rental rate and assess a penalty against appellant in the form of reimbursement to the tenants of overpaid rent.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landlord rented a family residence to tenants and increased their rental rate over the next few years. Thereafter, the tenants filed a petition with respondent board claiming that appellant charged excessive rent and had not registered the property as required by local ordinance. The tenants sought a rate adjustment and reimbursement. Following a hearing, in which appellant testified that his costs exceeded the rent charged, respondent set a new rental rate, concluded that tenants overpaid appellant, and authorized the tenants to withhold the rent until the overcharged amount was satisfied. Appellant challenged respondent's decision and petitioned for a writ of administrative mandamus, which the trial court denied. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed the judgment. First, the court held respondent did not exercise, nor did the underlying statute authorize, judicial power contrary to the Cal. Const. art. VI, § 1. Further, the court held that appellant was not denied a fair return on his investment by respondent's decision. The court reasoned respondent acted in accordance with the ordinance in denying appellant's introduction of evidence regarding his expenses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment denying appellant landlord's writ for administrative mandamus and affirming respondent board's decision to reduce the tenants' rent and the tenants' entitlement to reimbursement of overpaid rent. The court held that respondent acted within its capacity and only acted in its ministerial capacity when determining the rental reduction and penalty.

 

559 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Aikens


Nos. B027710, B036550


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


207 Cal. App. 3d 209; 254 Cal. Rptr. 30; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1192



December 16, 1988 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rules 976 and 976.1 of the California Rules of Court, the introductory paragraph, the Factual Synopsis, contention 1 of the Discussion and the concurring and dissenting opinion are certified for publication.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied January 11, 1989. Johnson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 29, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A571330, Henry P. Nelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that convicted him of two counts of assault with a deadly weapon. After the jury returned a verdict on one count, the trial court substituted a juror and gave an instruction, Cal. Jury Instructions, Civil No. 17.51, to the jury. Cal. Penal Code § 1089 dealt with substituting alternate jurors.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of assault with a deadly weapon. After the jury returned a verdict on one of the counts, the trial court substituted an alternate juror for one of the jurors that had deliberated. Defendant appealed and argued that after a jury rendered a verdict, the trial court could not excuse a juror, substitute an alternate, and allow the changed jury to deliberate for other verdicts. The court affirmed. The court held that Cal. Penal Code § 1089 allowed the substitution of a juror at any time before or after final submission of a case to the jury. The court held further that it was constitutional under both the U.S. and California constitutions to substitute jurors after deliberations began. However, an instruction had to be given to the jury to begin fresh deliberations and not resume old ones. Per federal precedent, failure to give such an instruction was an error. Here, the trial court had given the proper instruction by using Cal. Jury Instructions, Civil No. 17.51. The court also gave weight to the case not being close on the facts and identification being the only issue.


OUTCOME: Ruling against defendant, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held that it was constitutional under both the federal and state constitutions to substitute jurors after deliberations began. It was error to fail to give the appropriate instruction to the jury after substitution, but the trial court had given the appropriate instruction.

Bakanauskas v. Urdan


No. A039697


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


206 Cal. App. 3d 621; 253 Cal. Rptr. 764; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1172



December 14, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 613804-2, Donald P. McCullum, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tenant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent property owner, denying appellant's claim that her eviction violated Berkeley, Cal., Mun. Code § 13.76.130A.9.


OVERVIEW: Appellant tenant vacated her apartment after respondent property owner instituted eviction proceedings. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondent. Appellant sought review, contending that her eviction violated Berkeley, Cal., Mun. Code § 13.76.130A.9. Appellant also contended that sale of the rental property, coupled with the tenants-in-common agreement entitling them to exclusive occupancy of a particular unit, constituted the creation of a "community apartment project" in violation of Berkeley, Cal., Mun. Code § 21.52.070. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the evidence supported the finding that there were no comparable units for occupancy, and that the deed did not create any separate interests in the property or any right to exclusive occupancy of either unit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which granted respondent property owner's motion for summary judgment against appellant tenant. The court affirmed summary judgment because there were no comparable units for occupancy, and there was no community apartment project created.

 

561 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Smith


No. A042035


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


206 Cal. App. 3d 599; 253 Cal. Rptr. 673; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1171



December 13, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 23, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 880145-8, David A. Dolgin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant criminal defendant sought to vacate the enhanced sentence imposed by the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), on equal protection grounds.


OVERVIEW: Appellant criminal defendant challenged the enhanced sentence imposed upon him following his guilty plea to multiple counts of robbery and attempted robbery. Appellant contended his equal protection rights were violated since his enhanced sentence was based on an old conviction, whereas other recidivist sentencing statutes had a "washout" clause excluding from consideration old convictions. The court rejected his argument, and upheld his sentence. It held that appellant had failed to show that similarly situated persons were treated unequally, inasmuch as the legislative scheme subjects all defendants convicted of a serious felony who had a prior serious felony conviction to enhancement under the section appellant was sentenced under, regardless of the washout provision of other sentencing statutes.


OUTCOME: Appellant's enhanced sentence was affirmed. Although it was true that some recidivist offenders were allowed to have stale convictions discounted, all who recidivist offenders with a serious prior conviction committing a new serious offense, such as appellant, were subjected to the enhancement to which appellant was subjected.

 

562 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Melton


No. F009378


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


206 Cal. App. 3d 580; 253 Cal. Rptr. 661; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1153



December 12, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 29, 1989, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 23, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Riverbank Judicial District of Stanislaus County, No. 972890, Terry K. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Municipal Court for the Riverbank Judicial District of Stanislaus County, California, convicting him of the offense of carrying a concealed weapon. Defendant challenged on the ground that his retroactive restoration to duty, pursuant to Gov't. Code § 19584, entitled him to carry a concealed weapon at the material time.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a police officer who, subsequent to his appeal of termination of his appointment, was retroactively reinstated to all his benefits as a police officer. During the termination, appellant was assaulted by a customer. Defendant pulled a holster and gun from his waistband. The jury found defendant guilty of carrying a concealed weapon. On appeal, the court determined that defendant did not have permit to carry the gun at the material time. It noted that although defendant was retroactively reinstated to his benefits pursuant to Cal. Gov't. Code § 19584, nothing suggested that the statute retroactively entitled defendant to non-benefits emoluments, such as carrying a concealed weapon on his person without a permit. Furthermore, the court held that the statute did not excuse defendant from compliance with the law during the period of suspension or termination. It affirmed the conviction.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction of carrying a concealed weapon was affirmed because the retroactive restoration of defendant to duty did not cover his carrying a concealed weapon without a permit. The court held that the thrust of the statute was the remuneration of affected employees whenever there was an adverse employment action.

Ins. Co. of N. Am. v. Dep't of Indus. Relations


No. B034767


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


209 Cal. App. 3d 760; 259 Cal. Rptr. 277; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1269



December 2, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 662373, Edward A. Hinz, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurer sought review from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), of an order dismissing its complaint for declaratory relief, temporary restraining order, preliminary and permanent injunction as to respondents director, fund, and industrial relations department after their demurrers to the complaint were sustained without leave to amend for lack of jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Appellant insurer issued a surety bond securing the workers' compensation obligations of employer on October 28, 1975. The bond was cancelled October 25, 1983, and another surety issued a new bond. Employer filed a petition in bankruptcy and the self insurers' security fund (fund) was ordered to assume its workers' compensation obligations. Fund sought reimbursement from insurer for the employer's obligations based on injuries occurring prior to issuance of the insurer's bond. The trial court sustained demurrers of respondent department of industrial relations, without leave to amend, based on a lack of jurisdiction. The court held that Cal. Lab. Code § 3701.5 applied and the director was to settle disputes between a surety and the fund with respect to workers' compensation claims against a private self-insured employer who failed to pay such claims and an appeal of the director's decision is to the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board. Where an administrative remedy was provided by statute, relief must have first been sought from the administrative body and that remedy exhausted before the courts would act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint because appellant insurer did not exhaust all administrative remedies as required by statute. The court did not have jurisdiction of the dispute between insurer and the fund and no exception applied.

 

564 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Thompson


No. H003949.


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


206 Cal. App. 3d 275; 253 Cal. Rptr. 513; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1116



November 29, 1988 


NOTICE:  [**1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied February 16, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged the denial of his application for a writ of habeas corpus, and the California Supreme Court issued an order to show cause returnable to the appellate court why the petition for a writ of habeas corpus should not be granted.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a prisoner serving an indeterminate term, petitioned for a writ of habeas corpus, arguing that he was unconstitutionally deprived of one-for-one worktime credits under Cal. Penal Code § 2933, which were granted to prisoners serving determinate terms, in violation of equal protection guarantees. Petitioner challenged the order of denial, and the state supreme court issued an order to show cause why the petition should not be granted. In denying the petition, the appellate court concluded that good cause precluded the issuance of a habeas corpus writ. Petitioner's equal protection claim failed, as persons must be similarly situated to be entitled to identical treatment, and persons serving determinate and indeterminate sentences were not similarly situated under conduct credit law. Further, petitioner was sentenced under Cal. Penal Code § 190, which incorporated by specific reference art. 2.4 of the California Penal Code, and those provisions governed how prisoners serving indeterminate sentences received worktime credits. As such, petitioner was entitled to one-third credits rather than the one-to-one credits available to prisoners serving determinate sentences.


OUTCOME: The petition for a writ of habeas corpus was denied for good cause shown. Petitioner failed to establish an equal protection claim where prisoners serving determinate and indeterminate sentences were not similarly situated. Further, where the statute under which petitioner was sentenced incorporate by reference a statute governing worktime credits, that incorporated statute governed the manner in which petitioner could receive credits.

 

565 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Huntington Park Club Corp. v. County of L.A.


Nos. B033071, B032775


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


206 Cal. App. 3d 241; 253 Cal. Rptr. 408; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1111



November 29, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 23, 1988, and respondents' petitions for review by the Supreme Court were denied February 23, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C527544, C532479 and C545204, Arthur Baldonado, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to enter a new and different judgment consistent with the views expressed in this opinion. Appellant to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted declaratory relief and permanent injunction in favor of respondent club and a permanent injunction restraining and enjoining appellant from preventing or otherwise interfering with the playing of the game known as pai gow in respondent's gaming establishments.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county sought to prevent respondent club from playing the game pai gow in respondent club's gaming establishments based on Cal. Penal Code § 330, which made percentage games illegal. The trial court granted a permanent injunction restraining and enjoining appellant from preventing the playing of pai gow in respondent's gaming establishments. The court reversed the judgment. The court found that although the term percentage game was not statutorily defined, that term had been the subject of judicial interpretation. Percentage game was defined as encompassing any game of chance from which the house collected money calculated as a portion of wagers made or sums won in play, exclusive of charges or fees for use of space and facilities. The court found under the injunction, respondent was allowed to charge a rental fee from the participants in the game based on the amount each participant wagered. The court concluded that it was a percentage game and violated Cal. Penal Code § 330.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, which granted declaratory relief and permanent injunction in favor of respondent club. The court found under the injunction, respondent was allowed to charge a rental fee from the participants in the game based on the amount each participant wagered. The court concluded that it was a percentage game and was illegal, and reversed in favor of appellant county.

 

566 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cal. Bldg. Indus. Ass'n v. Governing Bd.


No. B030733


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


206 Cal. App. 3d 212; 253 Cal. Rptr. 497; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1118



November 29, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
The petitions for a rehearing were denied December 29, 1988, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondents' petitions for review by the Supreme Court were denied February 16, 1989. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 651859, Jerry K. Fields, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court denying Building Industry's petition for a writ of mandate is reversed. Upon remand, the trial court is directed to grant such writ in accordance with the views expressed herein. Building Industry shall recover its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant building industry associations challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellants' petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent school districts to authorize the issuance of building permits without first requiring payment of certain taxes. Appellants contended that the tax levied by respondent was an invalid development fee that exceeded statutory limits.


OVERVIEW: Respondent school districts adopted a resolution, subsequently approved by the voters, to levy a special tax on new residential construction. Appellant building industry associations challenged the validity of the tax. The trial court denied appellants' petition to compel respondents to authorize issuance of building permits without first requiring payment of the challenged tax. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that if the tax was a "special tax" within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, § 4, then the tax was unconstitutional because it was imposed without enabling legislation. The court held that the tax was instead a development fee and was invalid because it exceeded the limits imposed by the state legislature. The court granted appellants' petition for writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's denial of appellant building industry associations' writ of mandate, and held that the tax levied by respondent school districts was a development fee assessed on new residential construction. The court held that as such, the tax was invalid because the amount exceeded statutory limits, and granted the writ, holding that the tax was unconstitutional.

 

567 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Lopez v. McMahon


No. A040750


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


205 Cal. App. 3d 1510; 253 Cal. Rptr. 321; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1100



November 22, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 16, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 133990, Richard H. Breiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County (California) that denied her petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent Director of the Department of Social Services to issue a license pursuant to California Child Day Care Facilities Act, Cal. Health and Safety Code § 1596.870 et seq., on the basis that this statute was unconstitutional insofar as it automatically denied a license to operate a facility.


OVERVIEW: Appellant applicant petitioned the superior court for a writ of mandate to compel respondent Director of the Department of Social Services to issue a day care operator's license pursuant to the California Child Day Care Facilities Act, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1596.870 et seq. The court affirmed the judgment denying appellant's petition, which consequently upheld respondent's decision to deny appellant's application. The court held that the challenged provision of § 1596.871(e) was constitutional as applied. The court ruled that this statutory classification was rationally related to the legitimate government purpose of protecting day care children from any potential risk of harm by denying a license to applicants who were either themselves convicted of certain felonies or who had such felons working or residing on the proposed day care premises. The court determined that this provision was narrowly drawn to limit the scope of the disqualification to only those convicted of specified felonies and not to all crimes. No exemption under § 1596.871(e) applied to appellant's husband because he was convicted of armed robbery, a specified disqualifying violent felony.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that denied appellant applicant's petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent Director of the Department of Social Services to issue a license to operate a facility pursuant to the California Child Day Care Facilities Act because the challenged provision of this statute was constitutional when rationally related to the legitimate purpose of protecting day care children from any potential risk of harm.

 

568 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ornelas


No. F009257


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


205 Cal. App. 3d 1406; 253 Cal. Rptr. 165; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1077



November 18, 1988 


NOTICE:   [**1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied March 2, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, Nos. 33770 and 33810, Lewis E. King, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which convicted him of petty theft under Cal. Penal Code § 666 with a prior burglary and robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and denied his motion to suppress evidence of his prior conviction of first degree burglary.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction of petty theft under Cal. Penal Code § 666, with a prior burglary and robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and the denial of his motion to suppress evidence of his prior conviction of first degree burglary. Two store employees accused defendant of leaving the store without paying for cigarettes. One of the employees reached into defendant's pocket and found a pack of cigarettes. Defendant was detained in the back room of the store until police arrived. Defendant argued that the search was illegal because the employees were performing a public function. The court held that the search was illegal under state law because detention of defendant until police arrived constituted state action, but that Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d) required that federal law be applied to exclusion of evidence question. The court affirmed the judgment and the suppression of evidence and held that federal law did not require exclusion of the evidence because no official of the government, federal or state, had any connection with the wrongful seizure or any knowledge of it until after the fact.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because federal law applied to the exclusion of evidence obtained while defendant was held until the police arrived for stealing cigarettes, and did not require exclusion because no official of the government, federal or state, had any connection with the wrongful seizure or any knowledge of it until after the fact.

 

569 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Gastile


No. C001271


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


205 Cal. App. 3d 1376; 253 Cal. Rptr. 283; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1075



November 18, 1988 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - The Reporter of Decisions is directed to publish all of the opinion except part IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied March 2, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yolo County, No. 8214, James F. Roach, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Yolo County (California), that convicted him of two counts of first degree murder and one count of attempted murder, and sustained a special circumstance allegation for multiple murder and other allegations.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that the trial court erroneously failed to (1) obtain a waiver of his right to a jury trial on the special circumstance allegation; (2) suppress his confession; and (3) recite the proper standard of proof for the determination of the voluntariness of his confession. The court found that the trial court did err in failing to obtain a separate jury waiver from defendant on the special circumstance allegation. However, employing the harmless error analysis, the court found that the error was harmless as defendant had counsel and was sentenced by an impartial adjudicator. Moreover, the record established the correct sentence was imposed not only beyond a reasonable doubt, but beyond a shadow of a doubt. With respect to his confession, the court found no evidence that defendant's Miranda waiver and confession were anything but knowingly and voluntary. The court followed the United States Supreme Court holding that a noncoercive confession obtained following the provision of a Miranda warning would not be excluded even though it followed a noncoercive admission obtained without the benefit of a Miranda warning. The judgment below was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed because the error by the trial court was harmless, and because the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to quash his confession.

 

570 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Graf v. San Diego Unified Port Dist.


No. D006230


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


205 Cal. App. 3d 1189; 252 Cal. Rptr. 889; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1060



November 14, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 582786, Richard D. Huffman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff boat owners sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied plaintiffs' motion for a temporary injunction against defendant San Diego Unified Port District in plaintiffs' action for declaratory and injunctive relief concerning the removal of boats from a harbor. Plaintiffs claimed that San Diego, Cal., Unified Port District Ordinance 1200 was unconstitutional and void.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff boat owners filed an action against defendant San Diego Unified Port District seeking declaratory and injunctive relief pertaining to a notice to remove boats from a harbor. The trial court dissolved a temporary restraining order and denied plaintiffs' motion for a temporary injunction. On appeal, plaintiff contended that San Diego, Cal., Unified Port District Ordinance 1200 was unconstitutional and void. The court rejected these contentions and found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying the motion for a temporary injunction. The court held that defendant was authorized to adopt all ordinances and resolutions to carry out its duties delegated to it as trustee over the tidelands and submerged lands of the San Diego Bay. Defendant's regulation of anchorage was a proper exercise of the powers under its enabling act. Inconvenience to plaintiffs and a diminution of value of plaintiffs' vessels did not constitute a taking or damaging of the property or an improper exercise of defendant's powers. The court summarily rejected plaintiffs' theory that the ordinance was vague. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the temporary injunction in plaintiff boat owners' action for declaratory and injunctive relief against defendant San Diego Unified Port District for removal of boats from a harbor. The court found that regulation of anchorage was part of defendant's authority as trustee over tidelands and that, therefore, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying plaintiffs relief.

Autoland, Inc. v. Superior Court


No. B036897


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


205 Cal. App. 3d 857; 252 Cal. Rptr. 662; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1024



November 1, 1988 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The alternative writ is discharged. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing respondent to vacate its order of July 27, 1988, striking petitioner's peremptory challenge against the discovery referee, and to conduct further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation sought a writ of mandate to respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which struck petitioner's peremptory challenge against a referee ruling that the peremptory challenge afforded by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6 did not pertain to discovery referees.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner corporation challenged respondent trial court's judgment. Respondent struck petitioner's peremptory challenge against a referee ruling that the peremptory challenge afforded by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6 did not pertain to discovery referees. Petitioner then sought a writ of mandate to respondent. The appellate court granted petitioner's writ of mandate and directed respondent to vacate its order of July 27, 1988, striking petitioner's peremptory challenge against the discovery referee because the peremptory challenge afforded by § 170.6 did pertain to discovery referees. The appellate court stated that if petitioner could have exercised his one peremptory challenge to disqualify a particular judge or court commissioner from hearing a discovery motion, there was no compelling reason why he should have been unable to disqualify a particular referee from hearing the motion.


OUTCOME: The appellate court granted petitioner corporation's writ of mandate and directed respondent trial court to vacate its order of July 27, 1988, striking petitioner's peremptory challenge against the discovery referee because the peremptory challenge afforded by the statutory provision did pertain to discovery referees. The appellate court remanded the cause for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in its opinion.

People v. Thompson


No. B027809


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


205 Cal. App. 3d 871; 252 Cal. Rptr. 698; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 1019



November 1, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 22, 1988, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 16, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR 21508, Allan L. Steele, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which convicted defendant of two counts of lewd and lascivious acts on a child under the age of 14 years.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction of two counts of lewd and lascivious acts on a child under the age of 14 years. Defendant contended that Cal. Penal Code § 1203.066 (a)(3), was unconstitutionally void for vagueness because it failed to specify what acts of defendant would have constituted "making friends" under the statute. The court held that the statute was not so unconstitutionally vague as to be misunderstood by the jury. Moreover, defendant was not denied equal protection because the legislature could rationally decide that someone who makes friends with a child in order to have sexual relations with that child was more culpable than the individual who was already acquainted with the child. The court found no error in the trial court's instructions to the jury where the court did not define words of common usage and with no special meaning. The court further determined that denial of probation did not amount to cruel and unusual punishment. Accordingly, the court affirmed defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for two counts of lewd and lascivious acts on a child under the age of 14 years because the penal statute that defendant was convicted under was not unconstitutionally vague, defendant was not denied equal protection of the laws, the trial court did not err in instructing the jury, and defendant's punishment was not cruel and unusual.

Bryce v. Superior Court


No. G006767


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


205 Cal. App. 3d 671; 252 Cal. Rptr. 443; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 997



October 27, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 15, 1988, and the Petition of Real Party in Interest for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied January 5, 1989.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged. Let a peremptory writ issue directing the trial court to adopt and enforce its own procedures for readiness or settlement conferences within the limits of the rules of court noted above, Code of Civil Procedure section 187, and the Penal Code (see, e.g., Pen. Code, §§ 1191.1- 1191.3, 1192.1- 1192.7 [restricting negotiated pleas in certain instances]) and to exercise the judicial power at its disposal to obtain obedience to its orders.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The public defender brought a petition for extraordinary relief on behalf of petitioner clients "and future clients" accused of felonies in the Superior Court of Orange County (California). Petitioners through the public defender objected to the trial court's acquiescence in the district attorney's policy of refusing to participate in felony case settlement conferences unless they were conducted in public and on the record.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest county district attorney issued a memorandum stating that all discussions of felony case disposition were to be held in open court on the record. Respondent trial court acquiesced in the policy because it felt that it could not force prosecutors to appear in chambers to discuss case settlement. The county public defender on behalf of petitioner clients "and future clients" accused of felonies in the trial court petitioned the court for extraordinary relief stating that the creation of a record of settlement negotiations could prejudice a defendant. The court held that because the district attorney's policy and the trial court's acquiescence in that policy clearly offended the separation of powers doctrine, a peremptory writ was required. The peremptory writ directed the trial court to adopt and enforce its own procedures for readiness or settlement conferences within the limits of court rules. The court held that neither the district attorney nor the public defender was free to ignore court orders and any litigant who willfully disobeyed an order to attend a settlement conference would be subject to sanctions.


OUTCOME: In response to a petition for extraordinary relief brought by the public defender on behalf of petitioner clients and future clients, the court issued a peremptory writ directing the trial court to adopt and enforce its own procedures for readiness or settlement conferences within limits of court rules. The court held that the district attorney's refusal to participate in closed felony case settlement conferences usurped judicial powers.

 

575 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Elias


No. B035233


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


205 Cal. App. 3d 166; 252 Cal. Rptr. 348; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 968



October 18, 1988 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review denied January 5, 1989 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought to challenge, through petition for writ of habeas corpus, her conviction by the trial court (California) on two counts of making telephone calls with the intent to annoy without disclosing her true identity in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 653m(b).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Penal Code § 653m(b), which prohibited anonymous phone calls made with intent to annoy. The court granted petitioner her habeas corpus relief and found that the statute was overbroad and violated the free speech protections of U.S. Const. amend. I and Cal. Const. art. I § 2, because the statute applied to phone calls made from or to any location, not just residential phone calls where the recipient has an enhanced privacy interest, the statute was not restricted to certain time periods, and the state did not have a right to regulate speech merely because it is annoying. Thus the court found the statute objectionable because it treated a telephone call made midday to the complaint department of a large retail store identically with a telephone call made to a private residence at 3 a.m. The court also found that just because petitioner's actions did not constitute protected speech did not mean that she did not have the right to bring a constitutional challenge to the statute.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner her writ for habeas corpus relief because the statute used to convict her was overbroad and violated the free speech protections in the United States and California constitutions.

 

576 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



City of Oakland v. Digre


No. A042876


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


205 Cal. App. 3d 99; 252 Cal. Rptr. 99; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 969



October 14, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 10, 1988.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged. The petition for writ of mandate is denied. The parties shall bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city filed a petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent, the city's director of finance, to enforce and collect a parcel tax, consisting of an annual flat fee assessed against city property holdings, which was voted on and approved by the city's voters as Measure M.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city adopted a parcel tax consisting of an annual flat fee assessed against city property holdings. Its stated purpose was to raise revenue that would be deposited in the city's general fund to provide revenue to maintain and enhance municipal services. After the tax was approved by the city's voters as Measure M, respondent, the city's finance director, declined to enforce it on the ground that it violated the constitutional requirement that all property be taxed according to value. The city maintained the tax was a valid excise tax and sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to enforce and collect the tax. The court denied the petition. Based on its incidents, and from the natural and legal effect of its language, Measure M was a tax on property. The tax was imposed on all property regardless of the use to which the property was put and it was due annually at fixed times. Measure M failed to apportion the tax by the type and extent of municipal services used. As a property tax, Measure M violated Cal. Const. art. XIII, §1, which provided that property was required to be assessed at the same percentage of fair market value. The parcel tax was a flat fee tax.


OUTCOME: Court denied the petition. Based on its incidents, including fact that it was imposed on all property regardless of use and was due annually at fixed times, parcel tax was a property tax and, because it was not based on property's fair market value, it was prohibited by California Constitution.

 

577 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (Holvey)


No. F010337


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


205 Cal. App. 3d 51; 252 Cal. Rptr. 335; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 949



October 12, 1988 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part I.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 7, 1988, and the Petition of Real Parties in Interest for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 8, 1988. Mosk, J., Panelli, J., and Kaufman, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that the second category of criminal behavior under section 368, subdivision (a), is not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad as applied to any individuals, including medical practitioners. The petition for a peremptory writ is granted. The stay issued by this court on May 16, 1988, shall remain in effect only until this opinion is final in all courts of this state, the Supreme Court grants a hearing herein, or the trial court complies with the above-stated directions. Respondent court is directed to vacate its order of May 11, 1988, granting the real parties' petition for writ of prohibition/mandate and enter a new order denying the writ.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California) that writs of prohibition and mandamus issue to protect the real parties in interest from prosecution under Cal. Penal Code § 368 (a), for endangering the health of a dependent adult that had been in their care.


OVERVIEW: The people asked the court review an order issued by respondent that writs of prohibition and mandamus issue to protect the real parties in interest from prosecution under Cal. Penal Code § 368 (a), in the form it was effective during 1985, for endangering the person or health of a dependent adult that had been in their care and custody. The court was required to decide an issue of first impression, whether § 368 (a) was unconstitutionally vague in its application to the charges against the real parties. The court held that the second category of criminal behavior under § 368 (a) was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad as applied to any individuals, including medical practitioners. In light of the strong public policy to protect vulnerable adults who are subject to unjustified abuses, the restrictions were minimal in contrast to a vindication of important societal interests.


OUTCOME: The people's petition for a peremptory writ was granted because respondent improperly issued writs of prohibition and mandamus to protect the real parties in interest from prosecution for endangering the health of a dependent adult that had been in their care and custody.

People v. Vargas


No. B028096


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


204 Cal. App. 3d 1455; 251 Cal. Rptr. 904; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 944



October 7, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 19, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 034788, Alexander R. Early III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, and the matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to compute appellant's presentence custody credits pursuant to section 2900.5 and to forward a corrected abstract to the Department of Corrections reflecting those credits. 9



9 Appellant correctly asserts that the trial court erred in failing to compute those credits and in ordering the Department of Corrections to do so. (§ 2900.5, subd. (d); People v. Sage (1980) 26 Cal.3d 498, 508-509 [165 Cal.Rptr. 280, 611 P.2d 874].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that after a jury found her guilty of committing felony child abuse and endangerment between September 8-17, 1986, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 273a(1).


OVERVIEW: A police officer went to appellant's home to investigate allegations of abuse to her son. The police found burns and bruises on the child and appellant was charged with committing felony child abuse and endangerment. A jury returned a verdict of guilty and punishment was set at the mid-term of four years. Appellant challenged the conviction arguing that the trial court failed to give a sua sponte instruction to the jury that they must unanimously make findings to arrive at a guilty verdict and that the trial court failed to instruct jury of knowledge requirement. The court found that under Cal. Penal Code § 273a, the exception applicable to offenses involving a continuous course of conduct the trial court had no duty to give such an instruction where appellant was charged with both willful and negligent conduct constituting child abuse and endangerment. It further concluded that appellant's sentence should have been calculated to give presentence custody credit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court finding that no sua sponte instruction to the jury was required where appellant was charged with negligent and willful acts that resulted in a continuous sequence of acts. The matter was remanded to the trial court with directions to compute appellant's presentence custody credit pursuant to the statute at issue.

 

579 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Gibson


No. B025616


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


204 Cal. App. 3d 1425; 252 Cal. Rptr. 56; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 941



October 6, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 2, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. PC4, Harry E. Woolpert, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Appellant is entitled to parole on terms without reference to the requirements of section 2962 et seq.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant rapist challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California) requiring appellant to be indefinitely confined in a mental hospital as a condition of parole, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 2962.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who was due to be released after serving a term of years for a forcible rape conviction, was required to accept inpatient treatment under a new statutory scheme, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 2962, because the trial court found him to be a severely mentally disordered offender. The statute was expressly retroactive and mandated a potentially onerous change in his terms of parole, without requiring a determination of whether he constituted a danger to the public. Appellant argued that the statute was unconstitutional. The court determined that the statute had overwhelmingly more penal attributes than therapeutic. As such, it violated constitutional ex post facto protections. Furthermore, the court held that the statute violated the equal protection clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because it did not require proof of any present dangerousness to the public by mentally disordered offenders, as required for other adult persons involuntarily committed for mental health treatment. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and held that appellant was entitled to parole without reference to the statute.


OUTCOME: The order of indefinite confinement was reversed because it was overwhelmingly more penal than therapeutic and was thus violative of ex post facto protections. Moreover, the applicable statute also violated equal protection because it mandated involuntary confinement and treatment of former prisoners who were mentally ill without proof of dangerousness.

In re Marriage of Cairo


No. A039295


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


204 Cal. App. 3d 1255; 251 Cal. Rptr. 731; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 914



September 30, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 853514, Claude D. Perasso, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband challenged a judgment of the Superior Court, San Francisco County (California) entered in a dissolution proceeding that, inter alia, characterized a parcel of real property as community and assigned to appellant certain credit card debts.


OVERVIEW: Appellant husband and appellee wife separated after more than 31 years of marriage. In their subsequent dissolution of marriage proceeding, the trial court characterized a certain parcel of real property as community and awarded half the proceeds of its sale to each party. The trial court also assigned certain credit card debts to appellant. On appeal, the court affirmed. With respect to the parcel of real property, the evidence supported the trial court's implied findings that appellant induced appellee to sign a quitclaim deed on the property by intentionally misrepresenting the nature of the transaction and that appellee relied on those misrepresentations to her detriment. Moreover, application of Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2 to the case would have impaired appellee's vested property interest without due process. Finally, the evidence supported the trial court's determination that certain credit card obligations were appellant's separate debts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment characterizing the parcel of real property as community and assigning to appellant certain credit card debts, holding that the evidence supported the trial court's rulings.
