North Bay Regional Center v. Sherry S.


No. A039381


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


204 Cal. App. 3d 1133; 251 Cal. Rptr. 795; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 904



September 29, 1988 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing granted October 28, 1988 (See 207 Cal. App. 3d 449)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Napa County, No. M87182, Herbert W. Walker, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant severely mentally disabled adult sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Napa County (California), which granted the petition of respondent regional medical center to admit appellant to the state hospital.


OVERVIEW: Appellant severely mentally disabled adult was disabled to the extent that she could not walk or use language, was incontinent, and could not feed herself. Appellant's difficult feeding needs exceeded the scope of services available in the nursing home in which she was placed. After several failed attempts, respondent regional medical center filed a petition seeking appellant's placement in the state hospital on the ground that she was "gravely disabled." The trial court granted the petition and ordered appellant placed in the state hospital. The court held that for a nonconsenting developmentally disabled adult, who was not a danger to herself, but no parent was available to make an application for admittance to the state hospital, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 4825 provided that the director of developmental service was to be appointed as conservator. The court held that the statutory remedy was adequate and should have been applied. Therefore, the court vacated the trial court's order and remanded with directions to follow the statutory procedure. The court directed that it was not necessary for appellant to be released from the state hospital prior to disposition on remand.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's grant of respondent regional medical center's petition to admit appellant severely mentally disabled adult to the state hospital because the statutory procedure for such admittance was not followed.

 

582 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cmty. Dev. Com v. City of Fort Bragg


No. A037754


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


204 Cal. App. 3d 1124; 251 Cal. Rptr. 709; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 900



September 29, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 49935, Arthur B. Broaddus, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded. The trial court is directed to order reinstatement of use permit USP 9-83 and issue a writ of mandate commanding respondent Fort Bragg to issue a building permit. Appellants are awarded costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developer sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), which denied issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate directing defendant city to reinstate a conditional use permit which defendant had found expired pursuant to Fort Bragg, Cal., Municipal Code § 18.76.100, or to set aside its finding that the permit had expired.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff developer sought review of a judgment that denied issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate directing defendant city to reinstate a conditional use permit. Pursuant to Fort Bragg, Cal., Municipal Code § 18.76.100, use permits automatically expired if work had not commenced or a use established within one year of the issuance of the permit. The court found that the development project involved complex governmental and financial commitments and plaintiff was proceeding with a good faith intent to commence upon the proposed use, even though there had not been any actual construction at the site. The court held that it was unreasonable for the trial court to apply a standard concerned with the extent of construction alone to determine if the use permit automatically expired. Regarding the permits validity, the court held that defendant could not arbitrarily revoke the permit without cause, notice, and a hearing. Because plaintiff was not afforded notice of defendant's intention to consider revocation, any attempted revocation was a nullity. Accordingly, the judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that which denied issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate directing defendant city to reinstate a conditional use permit. The trial court erred in applying a standard concerned only with the extent of construction on the site when determining whether a conditional use permit expired for failing to commence work or establish a use.

 

583 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Lockman


No. F009271


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


204 Cal. App. 3d 782; 251 Cal. Rptr. 434; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 880



September 16, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Tuolumne County, No. 25277, James R. Hardin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings according to law. Appellant to have her costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-wife challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Tuolumne County (California), which found that respondent ex-husband was entitled to reimbursement for his separate contribution to the acquisition of their residence because it was unconstitutional to apply Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2 to property acquired before January 1, 1984, where the judgment was rendered after January 1, 1987.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ex-wife and respondent ex-husband were married in 1980, and their divorce was final on July 24, 1987. The trial court divided the parties' property pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2. The court found that § 4800.2 abolished the rule that separate property contributions to community assets were presumed gifts to the community in the absence of a contrary agreement or understanding. The statute applied to proceedings commenced on or after January 1, 1984, and proceedings commenced before January 1, 1984, to the extent proceedings as to the division of the property were not final on January 1, 1984. The court found that there was not a sufficient basis for impairing vested property rights because the application of § 4800.2 depended upon the date of the acquisition of the property in dispute. Because the trial court applied § 4800.2 to property acquired before January 1, 1984, the court reversed and remanded the award to respondent of his separate property contribution to the residence. The court held that without a finding that the parties agreed to preserve a separate property interest, appellant's vested property interests were impaired without due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's decision that granted respondent ex-husband reimbursement for his separate contribution to the acquisition of his residence with appellant ex-wife. The court held that appellant's due process interests were violated because there was no finding that the parties had agreed to preserve a separate property interest.

 

584 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Beck v. County of Santa Clara


No. H003922


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


204 Cal. App. 3d 789; 251 Cal. Rptr. 444; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 873



September 16, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petitions for a rehearing were dened September 28, 1988, and appellants' petitions for review by the Supreme Court were denied November 23, 1988. Panelli, J., was of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 631732, Henry Ramsey, Jr., Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: By placing the measure for transfer of the jail administration on the ballot, county satisfied the legal requirement that electoral power not be frustrated by a midstream transfer of power from an elected to an appointed official. Under these circumstances, we affirm the judgment of the trial court insofar as it denied injunctive or other relief preventing the transfer of administration of the Santa Clara County jail to the county-created department of detention. We express no opinion on other findings of the trial court which are now moot touching on whether the resolutions could be validly enacted as urgency measures and whether a referendum could be denied on the measures. The judgment appealed from is in relevant part affirmed, and the question of attorney fees is remanded to the trial court for further appropriate proceedings.

 [***2] The petition for writ of mandate is denied as moot. Our temporary stay of implementation of the transfer of power is vacated, effective immediately.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sheriff and sheriff's association sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which found in favor of respondent County of Santa Clara in appellants' action for a declaration that resolutions transferring the management of county jail facilities were invalid. Appellants claimed that the statute allowing transfer of authority, Cal. Gov't Code § 23013, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sheriff and sheriff's association brought an action against respondent County of Santa Clara seeking a declaration that respondent's resolutions transferring the management of county jail facilities from appellant sheriff were invalid. The trial court granted judgment to respondent. On appeal, appellants contended that Cal. Gov't Code § 23013 was unconstitutional as a transfer of elected official's duties to an appointed official. The court rejected this contention, finding that Cal. Gov't Code § 23013 did not infringe upon electoral power because the voters ratified the transfer of the county jail management away from appellant sheriff. Furthermore, appellant sheriff's duties were modifiable by the legislature because the legislature possessed plenary power to define appellant sheriff's duties. Therefore, Cal. Gov't Code § 23013 was not in conflict with the state constitution. The elimination of a portion of appellant sheriff's responsibilities did not infringe upon separation of powers because Cal. Gov't Code § 23013 authorized the transfer of function. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment and remanded on the issue of attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent County of Santa Clara in appellant sheriff and sheriff's association's action for declaratory judgment and remanded the issue of attorney fees. The court found that the statutory authority under which respondent transferred the management of county jail facilities from appellant sheriff was constitutional.

City of Westminster v. County of Orange


No. G006360


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


204 Cal. App. 3d 623; 251 Cal. Rptr. 511; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 855



September 14, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of intervener and respondent for review by the Supreme Court was denied December 15, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 528505, William F. McDonald, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to proceed in accordance with the views expressed above. All parties shall bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied appellant declaratory relief and found that Proposition 62, a statewide statutory initiative passed in November 1986 which added Cal. Gov't Code §§ 53720-53730, was constitutional. The initiative nullified appellant's utility users' tax unless the municipal electorate approved the levy. Respondents were county and political movement.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city enacted a utility users' tax effective January 1, 1987. California voters approved Proposition 62, a statutory initiative, in 1986. Sponsored by respondent political movement, the initiative added Cal. Gov't Code §§ 53720-53730. As of November 15, 1988, Cal. Gov't Code § 53727(b) prohibited collection of a general tax enacted after July 31, 1985, unless a majority of appellant's voters approved it within two years of the date of the initiative. If voter approval was not forthcoming, any utility users' tax revenues realized after November 15, 1988, were offset by a reduction in appellant's property tax allocation. Appellant sought a declaration that Proposition 62 was unconstitutional and an injunction to restrain respondent county from withholding property tax monies. The trial court found in favor of respondents. The court reversed and held that Cal. Gov't Code § 53727(b) was unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. II, § 9(a). The court further found that the severability clause contained in Cal. Gov't Code § 53730 provided that the remainder of the proposition did not survive after finding partial unconstitutionality.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that denied appellant city declaratory relief and found that a statewide statutory initiative that nullified a utility users' tax unless the electorate approved the levy was constitutional. The court found against respondents, county and a political movement, because the requirement of local voter ratification violated the California Constitution.

People v. Hernandez


No. F008912


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


204 Cal. App. 3d 639; 251 Cal. Rptr. 393; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 857



September 14, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied December 1, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 361162-1, Robert Z. Mardikian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of convictions is affirmed. The sentence is vacated and the matter is remanded for resentencing after preparation and consideration of a new probation officer's report.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his convictions of residential burglaries and his prison sentence of 18 years and 4 months entered by the Superior Court of Fresno County (California). Among other things, defendant argued that the trial court erroneously denied his motions to suppress his confession as involuntary.


OVERVIEW: Two days after his arrest during an interview, defendant confessed to the charged burglaries and admitted involvement in other burglaries. At the time of the interview, defendant was suffering from heroin and cocaine withdrawal and had not slept since his arrest. Before he confessed, the officers told him if he worked with them they would give him some medication, take him outside, and get him some food other than jail food. At the interview, defendant did not have difficulty speaking, and the officers did not have to repeat any questions. Defendant was convicted of burglary, theft, and unlawful driving with a vehicle. He was sentenced to 18 years and 4 months in prison. The court affirmed and held that under the totality of the circumstances, the record supported the trial court's finding of voluntariness insofar as the issue of drug withdrawal was concerned. Further, the court held that the inducements that were purportedly offered were not the type that would have motivated defendant sufficiently to render his statement involuntary. However, the court remanded for resentencing and held that the trial court should have stated its reasons for imposing consecutive prison terms.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions of residential burglaries because defendant's confession was voluntary. However, the court vacated defendant's sentence and remanded for resentencing after preparation and consideration of a new probation officer's report because the trial court failed to state reasons for imposing consecutive terms.

 

587 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Duncan


No. F005097


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


204 Cal. App. 3d 613; 251 Cal. Rptr. 355; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 865



September 13, 1988 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II through IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied December 8, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 27849, Marvin E. Ferguson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In view of our conclusions in part I of this opinion, the judgment is reversed on all counts and remanded for new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which convicted him of sodomy in concert by means of force and violence, Cal. Penal Code § 286(d), oral copulation in concert by means of force and violence, Cal. Penal Code § 288a(d), sodomy with a person under the age of fourteen by force and violence, Cal. Penal Code § 286(c), and oral copulation by means of force and violence, Cal. Penal Code § 288a(c).


OVERVIEW: After a jury trial, appellant was convicted of four counts of sodomy in concert by means of force and violence, Cal. Penal Code § 286(d), eight counts of oral copulation in concert by means of force and violence, Cal. Penal Code § 288a(d), one count of sodomy with a person under the age of fourteen by force and violence, Cal. Penal Code § 286(c), and two counts of oral copulation by means of force and violence, Cal. Penal Code § 288a(c). During trial, statements obtained in violation of U.S. Const. amend. V and VI were used to impeach appellant. The trial court did not instruct the jury that the statements could only be used could be used to impeach appellant's credibility. Rather, the trial court instructed the jury under Cal. Jury Inst. No. 2.13 and Cal. Jury Inst. No. 2.71, which, in effect, told the jury that the statements could be weighed and considered in resolving the issue of guilt. The court reversed the convictions. In so ruling, the court held that the failure to give a limiting instruction was reversible error because, in light of the other instructions, there was a reasonable possibility that the error had materially affected the verdict.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the convictions of sodomy in concert by means of force and violence, oral copulation in concert by means of force and violence, sodomy with a person under the age of fourteen by force and violence, and oral copulation by means of force and violence, because the trial court failed to give a limiting instruction after appellant's statements, obtained in violation of his constitutional rights, were used to impeach him.

 

588 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



County of Fresno v. Clovis Unified School Dist.


No. F009253


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


204 Cal. App. 3d 417; 251 Cal. Rptr. 170; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 839



September 6, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 351952-7, Nickolas J. Dibiaso, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondent is awarded its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county sought review of an order the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which granted summary judgment to defendant school district in case involving payment of the cost of an unsuccessful recall effort, pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 5420 et seq., and Cal. Elec. Code § 10000.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff county filed suit against defendant school district for payment of the cost of an unsuccessful recall effort of certain members of the governing board of trustees. The trial court granted summary judgment for defendant, and plaintiff appealed. The court, in affirming the trial court's decision, interpreted the election and education statutes involved. The court held that Cal. Educ. Code § 5424 referred only to the costs of an election that was held. Section 5424 said nothing about costs expended in preparation for an election, whether held or not. The court found that the legislature was aware of this distinction because other related statutes differentiated between the "preparation for" and the "holding of" elections and allocation of costs of these services between the county, local districts and municipalities. Cal. Educ. Code § 5303, Cal. Elec. Code §§ 10000, 23524. The court found that the language of § 5424 was clear and unambiguous and was susceptible of only one interpretation. The language of the section did not require districts within a county to pay costs expended by the county to determine that a recall election would not be held.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of defendant school district in a suit brought by plaintiff county. The court held that defendant was not required to pay the pre-election expenses of a recall election of school board trustees that was not held because the plain language of the statute did not provide for defendant to pay those expenses.

Jimmy Swaggart Ministries v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. D005707


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


204 Cal. App. 3d 1269; 250 Cal. Rptr. 891; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 807



August 29, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 28, 1988, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 17, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 517830, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nonprofit religious corporation challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) refusing a refund of sales and use taxes paid under protest to the State of California after an audit was conducted by respondent Board of Equalization of the State of California.


OVERVIEW: Appellant nonprofit religious corporation sought review of a judgment refusing a refund of sales and use taxes paid under protest to the State of California. The court of appeal affirmed. The Sales and Use Tax Law, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6001 et seq., as applied to appellant was constitutional. Appellant pursued evangelistic goals by means including the sale of bibles, religious books, religious tapes, and other religious and nonreligious materials in the state of California. The determination whether such activities were religious or purely commercial was difficult to make, but the distinction was vital. The trial court properly found that appellant was a "retailer" within the meaning of the tax laws and that it had a sufficient nexus to California to allow imposition of the sales and use taxes. Neither the California Constitution nor the California Sales and Use Tax Law exempted religious organizations generally from sales and use taxes, other than for the serving of meals. California could justify limitations on religious liberty in the form of taxes as essential to accomplish an overriding governmental interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed. The sales and use taxes as applied to appellant were constitutional and did not prohibit the free exercise of religion. The trial court properly found that appellant nonprofit religious corporation was a "retailer" within the meaning of the tax laws and that it had a sufficient nexus to California to allow imposition of the sales and use taxes.

People v. Warner


No. B014840


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


203 Cal. App. 3d 1122; 250 Cal. Rptr. 462; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 749



August 17, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied December 8, 1988. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A565759, Coleman A. Swart, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that convicted him of unlawful sexual intercourse in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 261.5 and two counts of lewd conduct in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288(a).


OVERVIEW: Following his conviction of unlawful sexual intercourse in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 261.5 and two counts of lewd conduct in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288(a), appellant sought review and asserted that the trial court erred in admitting some of his post-arrest statements made after he had invoked his Miranda right to remain silent. Respondent people argued that the statements were properly admitted under the newly enacted Proposition 8. The court noted that Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d), known as Proposition 8, prohibited the exclusion of relevant evidence in any criminal proceeding with the exception of the application of any existing statutory rule of evidence relating to privilege or hearsay. The court agreed with respondent that Proposition 8 made the federal constitutional standard the sole determinant of admissibility. Under that standard, the court held that the trial court properly admitted the statements because there was no evidence of police misconduct when appellant invoked his right to remain silent and the second interview was conducted after an overnight interval and after appellant indicated his willingness to talk. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction because it held that the trial court did not err in admitting appellant's post-arrest statements made in a second interview after appellant had previously invoked his Miranda rights but then indicated on the following day at the second interview that he was willing to talk.

People v. Brych


No. B017274


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


203 Cal. App. 3d 1068; 250 Cal. Rptr. 402; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 746



August 16, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 27, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 364118, David J. Aisenson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The appeal is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state filed a motion to dismiss the appeal of appellant's convictions of grand theft, practicing medicine without a license, and fraudulently providing cancer treatment from the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), based on the fact that appellant severed all contact with his attorneys and voluntarily placed himself beyond the jurisdiction of the courts.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of numerous offenses involving grand theft, practicing medicine without a license, and fraudulently providing cancer treatment. He was sentenced to six years' imprisonment and successfully completed the sentence. Respondent state filed a motion to dismiss based on the fact that appellant resided out of the country and had severed ties with his attorneys. The court granted the motion. Appellant served his sentence and lawfully emigrated from the United States. He deliberately severed all involvement on his part in the appeal. An appellate court only had jurisdiction to entertain a case and decide it if there was a litigant before it against whom the court could have enforced its decision.


OUTCOME: Respondent state's motion to dismiss was granted because the appellate court did not have jurisdiction over an appellant who placed himself out of the country and who severed contacts with his attorneys.

 

592 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Lucas v. Superior Court


No. D007744


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


203 Cal. App. 3d 733; 250 Cal. Rptr. 76; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 731



August 9, 1988 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] We find no deprivation of due process nor denial of equal protection in Penal Code section 1330's requirement that counsel file an affidavit stating their belief the nonresident witnesses to be subpoenaed have material evidence and their appearance is necessary. Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandamus to compel respondent Superior Court of the County of San Diego (California), to subpoena trial witnesses who resided more than 150 miles from the trial site without the necessity of petitioner submitting an affidavit of materiality under Cal. Penal Code § 1330. Petitioner contended the statute violated his equal protection rights pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner requested that respondent trial court issue subpoenas for his criminal trial to witnesses who resided more that 150 miles from the trial site. Respondent refused to issue the subpoenas until petitioner submitted the affidavit of materiality required by Cal. Penal Code § 1330, which sought to assure the testimony sought would be material and not cumulative. Petitioner refused to submit the affidavit and sought a writ of mandate to compel issuance of the subpoenas, contending his equal protection rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a) were violated because the same requirement did not apply to witnesses in civil actions. The court denied the writ, finding that the distinction between criminal witnesses in Cal. Penal Code § 1330 and civil witnesses under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1992 did not constitute an equal protection violation because the legislative intent in the distinction pertained to the compensation of the witnesses and not the rights of the litigants. The court determined that the legislative distinction was rationally based and did not constitute a significant interference with the exercise of a fundamental right.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ sought by petitioner to compel respondent trial court to issue subpoenas for witnesses to attend his trial that lived more than 150 miles from the trial site, finding the statutory requirement that petitioner submit an affidavit of materiality to support such subpoenas did not violate his equal protection rights because it had no real and appreciable impact on his right to compel attendance of witnesses.

 

593 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gilmore


No. C002608.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


203 Cal. App. 3d 612; 249 Cal. Rptr. 914; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 718



August 4, 1988 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified August 9, 1988; Review denied October 27, 1988 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 77376, John J. Boskovich, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), for involuntary manslaughter, Cal. Penal Code § 192(b). Defendant claimed that the trial court misdirected the jury in a matter of law and that the verdict was contrary to the evidence and law under Cal. Penal Code § 197(4), for justifiable homicide.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of his conviction for involuntary manslaughter, Cal. Penal Code § 192(b), in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California). Defendant argued that the jury was incorrectly instructed in a matter of law and that the verdict was contrary to the evidence and the law regarding justifiable homicide, Cal. Penal Code § 197(4). Defendant encountered a burglar and chased him, firing shots at the ground in front of the burglar. One of the shots ricocheted, striking and killing the burglar. The court looked to the use of deadly force to defend against the commission or attempt to commit a "forcible and atrocious" crime, which at common law included nighttime burglary. The court found defendant's conviction for involuntary manslaughter was not supported by the evidence and held that crimes which were felonies at common law were forcible and atrocious as a matter of law. Thus, the use of deadly force by private citizens to apprehend felons fleeing from the commission of common law felonies was justified under § 197(4). The conviction was reversed, and the lower court was directed to enter a judgment of acquittal.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of involuntary manslaughter was reversed because the evidence did not support the conviction. The court held that crimes which were felonies at common law were forcible and atrocious as a matter of law and, therefore, justified deadly force by private citizens. The lower court was directed to enter a judgment of acquittal.

Pasadena Star-News v. Superior Court


No. B035286


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


203 Cal. App. 3d 131; 249 Cal. Rptr. 729; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 676; 15 Media L. Rep. 1867



July 27, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 24, 1988, and the petition of real party in interest D.M. for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 13, 1988.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing respondent to vacate its order of June 10, 1988, denying petitioner's motion for summary judgment, and to enter a new order granting the motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner newspaper filed an application for a writ of mandamus seeking to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to grant its motion for summary judgment in an invasion-of-privacy case filed against petitioner by plaintiff, the mother of an abandoned child, who had been mentioned by name in a series of articles published by petitioner about the abandonment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner newspaper published two articles regarding the abandonment of a baby by plaintiff, the baby's mother, who was mentioned by name in the articles. Plaintiff filed an invasion of privacy suit against petitioner, contending that petitioner's disclosure of her name in the articles invaded her privacy. Petitioner filed a motion for summary judgment, contending that all of the information published in the two articles was newsworthy as a matter of law and hence not actionable as an invasion of privacy. Respondent, the lower court, denied petitioner's motion, and petitioner filed an application for a writ of mandamus compelling the lower court to grant its motion for summary judgment. The court granted petitioner's application for a writ of mandamus, holding that even though plaintiff was not a public figure, she became so by virtue of the newsworthy nature of the matter, and thus petitioner was entitled, as a matter of law, to disclose plaintiff's identity in the articles. Accordingly, the court directed the lower court to vacate its order denying petitioner's motion for summary judgment and to enter an order granting the motion.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner newspaper's application for a writ of mandamus and ordered respondent, the lower court, to grant petitioner's motion for summary judgment, holding that plaintiff mother's abandonment of her child was a newsworthy event and thus petitioner was entitled to disclose plaintiff's identity.

City of Santa Monica v. Yarmark


Nos. B024751, B024754


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


203 Cal. App. 3d 153; 249 Cal. Rptr. 732; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 671



July 27, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On August 17, 1988, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 12, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the County of Los Angeles, No. WEC108182, David M. Rothman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed. Respondents shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, City of Santa Monica and the Santa Monica Rent Control Board, sought review of the order of the Superior Court of the County of Los Angeles (California), which denied appellants' application for a preliminary injunction against respondent landlords attempting to evict their tenants.


OVERVIEW: Respondent landlords sought to evict their tenants and remove controlled units from rental residential housing use. Appellants, City of Santa Monica and the Santa Monica Rent Control Board, filed suit for injunctive and declaratory relief, contending the evictions violated Santa Monica, Ca., City Charter art. XVIII, §§ 1806 and 1803, were not authorized by the Ellis Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 7060 et seq., and the Ellis Act violated Cal. Const. art. XI, §§ 5 and 7. The trial court denied appellants' application for preliminary injunction against two respondents, holding that the evictions were authorized by the Ellis Act and appellants failed to show the likelihood of prevailing on the merits. The reviewing court affirmed, holding that the Ellis Act preempted portions of appellant city's removal and eviction controls. The court found that §§ 1806 and 1803 were invalid to the extent they conflicted with the Ellis Act by prohibiting residential landlords from evicting tenants in order to go out of the rental residential housing business. The court found that the Ellis Act was a general law with a comprehensive scheme addressing aspects of landlord-tenant affairs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order denying a preliminary injunction. The court held that the Ellis Act gave respondent landlords the authority to evict their tenants and portions of the city charter were invalid to the extent they conflicted with the Ellis Act.

 

596 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Bankovich


No. D005940


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


203 Cal. App. 3d 49; 249 Cal. Rptr. 713; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 670



July 26, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. DN36904, James A. Henenhoefer, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: That portion of the judgment pertaining to the division of the sale proceeds of the former residence is reversed and the matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-wife challenged the decision from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which found that, in dissolution of marriage proceeding, respondent ex-husband was entitled to the proceeds of the sale of the marital home because respondent paid the down payment with his inheritance.


OVERVIEW: During their marriage, appellant ex-wife and respondent ex-husband purchased a home. Respondent used his inheritance as a down payment. After filing for dissolution, the parties sold the house, and the trial court determined that the proceeds were respondent's separate property pursuant to Cal. Civil Code § 4800.2. On review, the court found that prior to the enactment of § 4800.2, the law was that separate property contributions to community assets were deemed gifts to the community absent an agreement to the contrary. However, § 4800.2 provided that a party was entitled to reimbursement for separate property contributions toward the acquisition of community assets unless the contributing party executed a written waiver. While the court found that uniform treatment of property was a worthy goal, its advancement alone did not justify disrupting property rights that were acquired under the previous settled law. Therefore, the court found that the house should have been treated as a community asset, subject to equal division between the parties. The matter was reversed and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: Trial court's decision which found that respondent ex-husband was entitled to the proceeds of the sale of the marital home was reversed. Even though respondent paid the down payment with his inheritance, appellant ex-wife was entitled to half because appellant's rights in the property vested prior to the amendment of the relevant statute.

 

597 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Takahashi v. Board of Education of Livingston Union School Dist.


No. F008684


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


202 Cal. App. 3d 1464; 249 Cal. Rptr. 578; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 675



July 25, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 18, 1988, and Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied October 12, 1988.
Rehearing denied by, 08/18/1988
Review denied by, 10/12/1988
Writ of certiorari denied Takahashi v. Livingston Union School Dist., 490 U.S. 1011, 109 S. Ct. 1654, 104 L. Ed. 2d 168, 1989 U.S. LEXIS 1729 (1989)
Related proceeding at Takahashi v. Merced County Dep't of Educ., 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 16 (E.D. Cal., Jan. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Merced County, Nos. 70836 and 71869, George C. Barrett, Judge.
Takahashi v. Board of Trustees, 476 U.S. 1182, 106 S. Ct. 2916, 91 L. Ed. 2d 545, 1986 U.S. LEXIS 1317 (1986)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Defendants are awarded their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenured teacher challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Merced County (California), which held that her causes of action for wrongful termination and discrimination against defendant public school, board of education, and related individuals were barred by res judicata.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff tenured teacher worked for defendant public school board for 20 years. After several evaluations, plaintiff was dismissed for cause on the basis of incompetency under Cal. Educ. Code § 44932(d). The Commission on Professional Competence (commission) held a hearing as to her dismissal pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 44944 and upheld her dismissal. At the administrative hearing, plaintiff argued only that the commission lacked jurisdiction. Thereafter, the state court denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate. She then filed a federal action based on 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 1981, 1983, which was precluded by the prior state action. Plaintiff then filed two complaints in the trial court alleging breach of employment contract, conspiracy to defraud, wrongful termination, and discrimination. The complaints were consolidated and dismissed. The appellate court affirmed and held that the previous state and federal court litigation barred these actions on the basis of res judicata. The appellate court further found that plaintiff should have brought all of her claims against defendants in the administrative hearing in front of the commission.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing plaintiff tenured teacher's causes of action for wrongful termination and discrimination against defendant public school, school board, and related individuals because they were barred by res judicata.

 

598 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Martinet v. Dep't of Fish & Game


No. D006073


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


203 Cal. App. 3d 791; 250 Cal. Rptr. 7; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 729



July 25, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 572007, Patricia D. Benke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff commercial fisherman challenged the denial of his petition for writ of mandate by the Superior Court for San Diego County (California), to compel California State Department of Fish and Game to issue him a shark and swordfish drift gill net permit.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff commercial fisherman was denied a shark and swordfish drift gill net permit. The California Department of Fish and Game denied his permit because he did not qualify as a prior permitee under Cal. Fish & Game Code § 8568(d) as he did not previously have a shark drift gill net permit license. The application was forwarded to an office which was to hold a lottery if the number of prior permitees were fewer than 150 under Cal. Fish & Game Code §§ 8568(b), 8570. Since more that 230 permits were issued in the previous season it was unlikely a lottery would have occurred. Plaintiff appealed to the Fish and Game Commission under Cal. Fish & Game Code § 8569, contending that circumstances beyond his control prevented him from qualifying as a prior permitee. Plaintiff then filed a petition for wit of mandate, which the trial court denied and the court affirmed. The court found the statute limiting the number of permits did not deny plaintiff equal protection as it was reasonably drawn to protect against over fishing, while protecting the fishing industry, and those persons who had invested in and practiced drift gillnet fishing of shark and swordfish.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of plaintiff commercial fisherman's petition for writ of mandate to compel California Department of Fish and Game to issue him a shark and swordfish drift gill net permit. The statute limiting permits was reasonably drawn to protect against over fishing, while protecting the fishing industry and those persons who had invested in and had practiced drift gill net fishing of shark and swordfish.

 

599 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Wilson's Heating & Air Conditioning v. Wells Fargo Bank


No. C000558


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


202 Cal. App. 3d 1326; 249 Cal. Rptr. 553; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 664



July 21, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Shasta County, No. 69586, Joseph H. Redmon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff subcontractors challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Shasta County (California), which refused to award attorney's fees to plaintiffs and against defendant bank, in plaintiffs' suit against the property owner to foreclose on mechanics' liens and for breach of contract.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff subcontractors brought an action against the property owner to foreclose on mechanics' liens and for breach of contract. While the complaint was pending, defendant bank foreclosed on the property and became the owner subject to the mechanics' liens. The trial court refused to award plaintiffs attorney's fees against defendant. The appellate court affirmed the judgment. In so ruling, the appellate court stated that no statute provided attorney's fees in an action on a mechanic's lien. Further, the appellate court held that the contract between plaintiffs and the property owner, which provided for an award of attorney's fees, could not be exercised against defendant. In so ruling, the appellate court noted that defendant was not a signatory to the contract. Further, defendant's foreclosure did not cause it to step into the shoes of the property owner. Also, the mere fact that defendant's consent was a condition precedent to the existence of the contract did not operate to make defendant a party to the contract or in privity with a party.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which refused to award attorney's fees to plaintiff subcontractors against defendant bank because no statute provided for attorney's fees in an action on a mechanic's lien and because the contract between plaintiffs and the property owner, which provided for attorney's fees, could not be enforced against defendant even though defendant subsequently became the property owner.

 

600 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Garcia v. Superior Court


No. H004117.


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


215 Cal. App. 3d 695; 249 Cal. Rptr. 449; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 653



July 20, 1988 


NOTICE:  [**1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted October 27, 1988 (S004813). (See 50 Cal. 3d 728)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, decedent's kids and heirs, sought a writ of mandate seeking reversal of order of respondent Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) which sustained the demurrers filed by defendants, parole agent and state, to claims based on defendant parole agent's conduct, leaving intact only claim against defendant state based on state psychologist's failure to warn decedent, murder victim, of danger of parolee in wrongful death action.


OVERVIEW: Parolee was released from prison, and he killed decedent. Plaintiffs, decedent's kids and heirs, brought a wrongful death and civil rights suit against defendants, parole agent and state. The trial court sustained demurrers as to all claims based on conduct misleading decedent into believing parolee was not dangerous and left intact the claim based on state psychologist's failure to warn decedent of danger. Plaintiffs sought a writ of mandate seeking to compel reversal of the trial court's rulings. The wrongful death claim involved issues of breach of duty, proximate cause, and immunity. The civil rights claims involved issues of whether defendant state could be sued and whether defendants pursued an official policy under color of state law that caused decedent's death. The appellate court denied the petition for writ of mandate because there was no recognized cause of action for misleading advice as no duty was breached, defendant state could not be sued under Civil Rights Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 because it was not a person for purposes of § 1983, and, since defendant parole agent's actions were not part of an official state policy, no federal cause of action existed.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for writ of mandate filed by plaintiffs, minor kids and heirs, in plaintiffs' wrongful death and civil rights suit. The petition was denied because the provision of misleading advice was not a breach of duty, defendant state could not be sued because it was not a person under statute, and no federal cause existed where defendant parole agent's acts were not the result of an official state policy.
